THE RED PAGE
THE WHITE POLYNESIAN
mHE theory, to which special promin-
|_ ence. was recently given in this page,
that the Maori was of a white Cau-
casian stock receives scientific verification
and a remarkable amplification at the hands
of Professor J. Macmillan Brown, of Christ-
church, M.L., in his book Maori a/nd Poly-
nesian (Hutchison, per Dymock’s). The
author is now expounding his interesting
theories in a series of University extension
lectures in Sydney. His book is indeed a
remarkable and convincing contribution
to the study of the ancient races of the
Pacific. He proves an immense anti-
quity in the story of man in Polynesia.
The first observers regarded the Maori
as a primitive race, but subsequently facts
came to light that led to the announce-
ment of an earlier race, the Moriori,
and finally evidence accumulated that
proved the presence in the Pacific of a
still earlier white Caucasian race. Now
Brown sweeps us back to megalithic times,
and depipts a great migration of white Cau-
casians east from the Mediterranean to
Maoriland, British Columbia, and South and
Central America. These megalithic wander-
ers have left their footprints plain ; their
track is marked, as with a series of unmis-
takable mile-stones, by the gigantic un-
mortared stone monuments they left by their
roadside. And the trail of this people that
displayed such remarkable engineering skill
without the assistance of metal weapons,
wheeled machines, or draught animals, leads
from Mauritania, or the north of Africa,
across Northern Asia, and down into the
Pacific. They wandered west as far as
Ireland, north as far as Norway, and
then, baulked by the Atlantic, they moved
east. Their route, as marked by their
dolmens, was across the Russian and Asiatic
steppes, past Lake Baikal, through Mon-
golia and Manchuria, thence into Japan, and
over the long-since-vanished stepping-stones
of innumerable islands into the Pacific. In
Samoa, Tonga, Tahiti, and Hawaii they set
up their colossal monuments ; in Maoriland
they left a miniature Stonehenge ; they
reached far east to Easter Island, and finally-
halted in Central and South America.
Another branch of this migration skirted the
northern coasts of Asia, and, by way of
Kamschatka, reached British Columbia.
Evidences are also found of another, and
probably earlier, eastern route of the same
megalithic people from the northern Medi-
terranean shores through Syria to the Per-
sian Gulf and Northern India, down the
Malay Peninsula, and into Java. But here this
migration ended, its path invaded and its
passage closed by the overflowing of the
Mongol peoples southwards. Thus it
was by the northern Asiatic route—the
path now marked by the steel rails of the
Siberian railway—that the Pacific was in-
vaded in early neolithic times. And it was
through the funnel of Japan that some ten or
fifteen thousand years ago the white race from
the Mediterranean reached Maoriland and
America. And this white race possessed,
even in that stage of its evolution, the
peculiar characteristic of the Caucasian : it
was a maritime people. And its power’ to
raise enormous unmortared stone monuments
was not a stage in the evolution of any other
race ; it was the special possession of one
superior race. None of these remarkable
structures are to be found in the lands of the
negroids—Central and South Africa, Aus-
tralia and Papua, nor in the regions of the
pure mongoloids—China and Central Asia,
Central and Western America. This lordly
track, the special highway of the Caucasian,
is marked, too, by the prevalence to this
day of blue-eyed peoples all along the
north of Central Asia. Brown proceeds to
show that Maori legends point to the far
north—Japan—as the route by which the
successive migrations reached so far south,
and he cites striking ethnological resem-
blances between the Maori and the primitive
race of Japan, the Aino. In the arts of
sculpture and design he finds a resemblance
between that of the Maori and that of the
Japanese, pointing to a common racial source.
Brown notes also that :
Many of the Maori children’s plays and games
are almost the same as those of Japanese and
those of European children : flying kites, skipping
with a rope, the top, the hoop, the bull-roarer,
the giant’s-stride swing, walking on stilts, throw-
ing somersaults, hide-and-seek, ducks and drakes,
counting out, hunt the slipper, knuckle-bone, and
cat’s cradle.
His conclusion is that:—
The Japanese must have absorbed much of the
race that they drove off southwards, and absorbed
with them not only their maritime habit, but
their artistic habit.
There are still closer resemblances, especi-
ally in the art of carving, between the
Maori and the northern olf-shoot of the
megalithic migration, the Indian of Brutish
Columbia.
Maoriland was the ultima thule of the suc-
cessive migrations, the later of which, a
brown race, came via the Malay Peninsula.
Thus into this cut de sac poured wave after
wave of humanity, and, piled stratum upon
stratum, we- still find in Maoriland the
remnants of these civilisations.
Maoriland 11 ‘is the palimpsest, or many-
times-re written record of the prehistoric
history of the Pacific.” As each new-comer
conquered and absorbed the prior occupants,
he absorbed some of their social customs.
Maori and Polynesian is mainly an elaborate
investigation into these customs, and a care-
ful tabulation of their origins.
J*
Of the Maori language, Brown makes the
astonishing statement that its true classifica-
tion “is with the modern European lan-
guages, and especially with the most modern
of all languages, English.”
It has reduced inflection to a minimum, and
expresses the grammatical relationship of words
chiefly by their order in the sentence, and the use
of auxiliaries and particles. ... It is as uninflee-
tional or isolating a language as English, and it is
agreed by most philologists that thi§ type is by no
means primitive : it is, in fact, the end of a long
process instead of the beginning. .. . But the pro-
cess of rubbing out the inflections has gone much
further in Maori than in English. It has retained
no external method of indicating the number or
gender, except by the addition of another word.
... In the Maori pronoun, the disintegration of
grammatical form is perhaps not quite so ad-
vanced, for we have a dual in the personal pro-
nouns. ... In the Maori verb, the old inflections
have left fewer traces than in the English verb;
in fact, there is only one, that for voice.
... • Now, the only explanation for this striking
linguistio development is that which we know fits
the modern evolution of English. Two languages
far rtlore highly inflected than modern Maori have
come together in the same region and mutually
destroyed the formal grammar, leaving only a few
traces of the inflections.
It was in Indonesia that this change took
place. Two Caucasian, and most probably
two Aryan, tongues came into collision in
Indonesia through the amalgamation of two
Caucasian races; and this grammar was
brought by the conquerors and acquired by
the conquered throughout the Pacific some
centuries before our era. The Maori
language is the descendant of several
languages, and the same evidences of strati-
fication are seen in it as in the manner and
customs of the Polynesians.
It has thrown off almost all the formal elements
that speech could dispense with, and it has
attained an absence of inflection and a simplicity
of system that few tongues in the world but
English have reached.
Jt
Among other interesting deductions Brown
shows that the Malay was not a migrant into
the Pacific q for the iron age began in the
Malay regions about the beginning of our
era, and, till the European ships arrived,
iron was unknown in Polynesia. \ He traces
the last migration into the Pacific by Way of
Java, as was indicated in our recent article ;
buthemakesthe important point that this was
almost wholly a migration of males. These
adventurers were a nation of wife-deserters.
They left their wives at home; the danger
and length of their explorations into the
unknown deterred any but males from the
venture ; and these, finding a plentiful sup-
ply of wives—or more probably widows—in
the islands they had conquered, did not
trouble to return. This deduction is con-
vincingly worked out from Brown’s analysis
of the arts and customs of the Maori. In
some of his arts he displayed an extraordi-
nary development, in others he was in an
equally extraordinary primitive stage. And
the reason 1 .Seek the woman !
It was the men who brought with them
the more developed arts, and taking the
aboriginal women into their households
allowed them to stagnate among their primi-
tive customs. It was mankind’s universal
mistake of leaving such an important art as
cooking in the hands of that universal in-
competent, Woman. When Man had to go
forth, to hunt, or kill his enemies, he left
Woman to nurse his child and light the fire
in readiness for his return. He left her in
the kitchen—and since the beginning of
time no woman has ever done any good In a
kitchen. No woman has ever invented
a labor-saving device for use in a kit-
chen, and, if left to herself, no woman
would ever use one. The only improve-
ments made in cookery were due to
the incursion of the orderly male into this
region of female j muddle. All the great
cooks are men ; and modern labor-saving
devices in the kitchen were only made possi-
ble by the fact that steamers and hotels
brought into that desolate arena—the
kitchen—the triumphant male. Woman to
this day is content tp get her results by the
most primitive means : in making puddings
she still measures by heaped teaspoonfuls
and half teaspoonfuls, and her cookery-books
are as vague and elusive as the novels of
Henry James. She is a hopelessly paleo-
lithic cook in what should be the most exact
of all sciences.
J*
So it was in Polynesia. The men invented
and raised the masculine arts of canoe-
building, carving, tatooing, and military
engineering to an extraordinarily high state of
development, while their women, taken
from the aborigines, left muddling along in
the kitchen, never thought of inventing pot-
tery, never improved upon the method of
rubbing two sticks together to make fire,
and, though they wove textiles, never had
the sense to use a distalf or a spindle. Had
it not been that modern civilisation allowed
men the leisure to interfere in the kitchen,
household arts would still be with us as they
were with the Maori—in the paleolithic stage.
Thus Brown—who, it is fair to state, is
only by implication responsible for the above
criticism of the modern kitchen—shows us a
Polynesia peopled ten or fifteen thousand
years ago by a white race that took their
women with them across the innumerable
stepping-stones of the vanished archipelagoes
of the Pacific—though it is probable that
even these migrants found the islands already
in the possession of one of those inferior
races that seem to have been indiscriminately
spawned in the beginnings of history. But
when the stepping-stones, one by one, sank,
the women of the new migrations were left
behind. 4ud all the evidences in Maori-
iand point back thousands of years, and
prove the immense antiquity of man in the
Pacific. The white Australian may note
that historically he has pre-empted the
Pacific, but, also historically, he was sub-
merged by a colored race. However, he
can try again.
The proprietor of The Commonwealth Annual
has been justified by the reception of his earlier
issues to proclaim this —the third—number jßow-
landson's Success (N.B.W. Bookstall Co.). With
an attractive cover design and contributions by
many eminent Australian authors and illustra-
tors, the magazine is a readable shilling’s-worth/
It is excellently produced, and illustrates once
more the large amount of “ copy ” available with-
in the Commonwealth.
Arthur Streeton, fresh from his record sales in
Melbourne, has placed before the art patrons of
Sydney a delightful exhibition of his art in rooms
in Victoria Market Chambers. The critic dis-
covers that there are five Streetons —the earlier
brilliant colorist of “Fires On,” the delicate
dreamer in pearly greys, as illustrated by his
poetic studies of London mists, the solid and satis-
fying painter of English sunlight and cloud effects
as in the rich and sombre “ Chepstow Castle,” the
equally painstaking student of the nude, and the
brilliant impressionist, returned to Australia with
a more assured craft, the painter of heat and the
blaze of the southern sun. And in all his pictures
Streeton paints himself, his unique poetic vision,
subjectively. And-upon all his work lies a delicate'
and almost unanalysable charm, the charm'of a
Conder or a Whistler. Streeton is a poet who has
chosen the brush, and chosen rightly.
Is it worth while? John Davidson, author of
Fleet Street Eclogues, Ballads and Songs, and
twelve other poetical or dramatic works —on an
average, since 1893, he has published one book
every year—is recognised as one of the foremost
living English poets—and now at fifty he is glad
to take a pension of £lOO a year ! And Ouida,
with probably thirty successful novels to her
name, thankfully receives from a generous monarch
£3 a week. Expressed 1 in terms of cash, literary
fame seems mud.
If there are any theoretical or practical Free-
traders left in Australia, The Fundamental Fal-
lacies of Freetrade, by L. S. Amery (“National
Review,” London,* per G. Robertson), may be
recommended to their perusal. The case for Pro-
tection is concisely and convincingly put.
“Women begin to be socially tolerable at
thirty, and improve until the deepening of their
consciousness is checked by the decay of their
faculties. But they begin to be pretty much
earlier than thirty, and are, indeed, sometimes at
their best in that respect long before their, chat-
tering is, apart from the illusions of sex, to be
preferred in serious moments to the silent sym-
pathy of an intelligent pet animal.” — G. Bernard
Pshaw.
UNGUARDED GATES.
A certain similarity of conditions gives an Aus-
tralian interest to the appended verse by the
American man of letters, Thomas Bailey Aldrich,
whose death in his 71st year was recently recorded.
“ Unguarded Gates ” first appeared in the Atla/ntic
Monthly for J uly, 1892.
Wide open and unguarded stand our gates. ...
A later Eden planted in the wilds,
With not an inch of earth within its hoimd
But if a slave's foot press it sets him free !
Here, it is written, Toil shall have its wage ,
And Honor honor, and the humblest man
Stand level with the highest in the law.
Of such a land have men in dungeons dreamed ,
And with the vision brightening in their eyes
Gone smiling to the fagot and the sword.
Wide open and unguarded stand our gates,
And through them presses a ivild motley throng —
Men from the Volga and the Tartar steppes,
Featureless figures of the Hoang-Ho,
Malayan, Scythian, Teuton, Kelt, and Slav,
Flying the Old World’s poverty and scorn ;
These bringing with them unknown ggds and rites,
Those, tiger passions, here to stretch their claws.
In street and alley what strange tongues are these,
Accents of menace alien to our air,
Voices that once the Tower of Babel knew !
O Liberty, white Goddess! is it well
To leave the gates unguarded 1 On thy breast
Fold Sorrow's children, soothe the hurts of fate,
Lift the down-trodden, but with hand of steel
Stay those who to thy sacred portals po-me
To ivaste the gifts of freedom. Ha/ye a care
Lest from thy brow the clustered stars be torn
And trampled in the dust. For so of old
The thronging Goth and Vandal trampled Rome,
And where the temples of the Caesars stoojf
The lean wolf, unmolested, made her lair.
OUR SAUSAGE SERIAL
Following the example of the great morning
dailies, The Bulletin has made arrangements to
run a serial story in this page. In order to save
ourselves trouble, and in order to get a local serial
almost as cheap as the imported article, we mean
to ask our contributors to write our serial them-
selves. Our—or their—serial will have every
week as many sensations as it is possible to crowd
into that instalment—say, as many thrills as occur
in a fortnight’s batch of the imported serial in the
great morning dailies. Each chapter will be built
upon the sausage principle—everything will be
chopped fine, and nothing will be left out. The
first chapter (compiled by the pink page who
figures in the heading of this page) is given below;
and, after a silence of a fortnight, due to the
fact that this page goes to press something like
seven months or less before it is published, each
week we will print the chapter sent in that most
convincingly develops the plot and enchains the
interest of the reader. Each weekly chapter must
not exceed 200 words; and ten shillings will be
paid for the instalment used. Contributions
should be marked “Serial” on the envelope, and
addressed to this page, and must reach this office
by the following Tuesday. The serial starts
thus /
“ To render permanently navigable three great
rivers for a distance of three thousand miles
inland ; to provide a plentiful and regular supply
of water to vast territories sometimes smitten by
drought] to throw open fresh fields for home-
seekers is a -policy which should appeal to the
imagination as Well as to the patriotism of Austra-
lians.” In pursuance of this policy The River
Murray League of S. Australia publishes The Nile
of Australia: Nature's Gateway to the Interior
(Thomas and. Co., Adelaide), a series.of informa-
tive articles by David J. Gordon.
An English review mentions that; Madame de
Stael was the original of Charley’s Aunt. Invited
by the undergraduates to visit Oxford, Madame
de Stael at the last moment excused herself. In
order not to disappoint the large gathering of
undergraduates, a student who knew French un-
dertook to impersonate the celebrated woman. It
was comparatively easy for the actor in a gorge-
ous robe and the celebrated turban to be Madame
de Stael, She was so much a man that the manly
voice and the masculine manner were helps rather
than hindrances. So Madame de Stael has some
claims to fame, after all.
G. K. Chesterton is of the opinion that a
grocer’s is the most romantic profession in the
world, and though we have never yet met a
romantic grocer, his shop is a storehouse of pro-
ducts brought from all the corners of the earth, and
collected and transported and adulterated under
conditions full of danger and heroism arid ad-
venture. This romance of coirimon things is fully
brought out in the superbly illustrated The
World’s .Commercial Products, by W. G. Freeman,
Superintendent of the Colonial Economic Collec-
tions of the Imperial Institute (Pitman’s, per
Dyniock’s), with contributions from various other
scientific writers. Excellent maps showing the
world-distribution of all the chief commercial pro-
ducts, useful and popularly written accounts of
the culture and manufacture of the fruits and
roots of the earth, arid beautifully reproduced
photographs of every step in the process of their
preparation for the use of mankind, Wmbine to
make up a very useful volume.
THE TREND THAT DION'T.
Chapter I. The Knocking at the Door.
As Alice entered her boudoir after a busy day,
she stumbled over five dead bodies.
“I wondered what it was, that stuff that
Oarruthers was licking up on the stairs,” she
exclaimed.
Heroines never say anything: they always
exclaim.
Oarruthers was her pet poodle.
“And now Oarruthers will have indigestion.
That blood must be quite stale.”
There came a knocking at the door.
Alice stood aghast, frenziedly raking up her
back hair,
Alice was a woman, and spent most of her life
trying to make hair that nature meant to grow
down grow up.
Which is opposed to the law of gravity.
But what has gravity to do' with a superb crea-
ture like Alice ?
She strode hastily to the wardrobe, and started
back with a shriek.
Four policemen (three sergeants and a red-
whiskered constable) were hanging by their necks
among Alice’s nasturtium silk underskirts.
“The telephone-wire ! ” she exclaimed. “I
can escape by it! ”
A face appeared at her window, the face of a
telegraph-line mender. She listened aghast as
he snipped the sole connecting line that lay
between her and safety—and Charles.
Again came that importunate knocking at the
door !

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Metamorphosis.
Surely you remember Nancy, surely it is more
than Fancy
That comes floating like a bubble over my be-
wildered brain,
When I seem to dream in Cloud-land, in the
Shadows of the Shroud-land,
That I've known you through the aeons, and we’ve
only changed again.
Were you once a lovely Lily, grown in Me—an
old Tin Billy—
I, so bent and badly battered, red with rust from
years of rain ;
You, like opalescent morning, all the garden
sweet adorning,
Showing ’midst the common blossoms like a poppy
. in the grain ?
Were you Fiddle, was I Nero ; I Leander, were
you Hero,
Watching for my nightly swimming of theHelles-
pontine stream,
Till at length the stormy water, jealous of earth’s
fairest daughter,
Reft you of your hapless lover, robbed you of
your first young dream ?
In the ages past, near Dutton, was I Wolf when
you were Mutton
(Lamb before I met you, skipping in the wood-
land fair and free) ?
Did I eat you like a glutton, leave your bones
without a cut on,
Dine upon your bleating mother, have your
brother for my tea ?
In the meads of old Meander, were you Goose
when I was Gander,
Lurking by the Trojan city when ’twas battered
by the Greeks ?
Did we just escape the billies of the cooks of
brave Achilles,
Hide from Hector and from Ajax in the marshes
and the creeks ?
But at present, darling Nancy, you’re a most sub-
stantial Fancy—
Twelve stone ten, and growing stouter, as the
seasons swiftly flee ;
Nought about you, light and airy, indicates a
latent fairy
Longing for Titania’s kingdom, underneath the
greenwood tree.
TAS.
ALBERT J. WOOD.
The Eiders.
A man rode down our track one day ;
I gave of my best, and he went away.
I loved him well, so I called him back ;
He whistled as he passed up the track.
Another man came, but I said him nay
(I thought of the first who rode away).
This man loved me, so he wandered back.
’Twas me or his future. He took the track.
And whether we give them nay or yea.
Sooner or later they ride away.
QUEENSLAND.
S.S. SEVERN.
The Leaves.
Leaves that circle and soar together,
Withered and shrunken, sere and pale,
As you whirl and fly in the wild June weather
Like wrinkled hags on a broomstick’s tail,
To me a sorry sight seems your revel,
As if the spectres of cold, dead sins,
Their sweetness soured i’ the mouth and evil,
Were dancing in darkness with mirthless grins.
Alas, how soon doth the summer fade !
You danced your fill ere your race was run,
When the warm west wind in your branches
played,
And twittered the sparrows and shone the sun.
But the sweetest fruit hath the soonest riping ;
Stern Winter came with his icy breath,
And lo ! to the sound of his joyless piping
You dance on dead in the dust of Death.
In the long, cold nights, when the moon gleams
sadly
Through clouds that darken with drifts of rain,
The wet, waste fields where the wind flaps madly,
Of the days that are dead you shall dream again ;
Of the scent of roses, the hum of bees,
The song of the stream as it ceaseless floweth,
Till the drip and sob of the sodden trees
Lull to the sleep whose end none knoweth.
For all that is on the earth and o’er it,
And all in the waters under the earth,
Must yield again to the earth that bore it,
Itself the seed of a newer birth.
And ever with sudden and stealthy pace
Death smiteth strongly nor pitieth ;
And lo ! where they flourished, full little trace
Bemaineth —dead in the dust of Death.
The lips we press and the cheek that glows,
The eyes that we drink to, laughing lightly,
Must fade as surely as last year’s rose
Has fled from the spot where it bloomed so
brightly.
Nothing remaineth save God or Death ;
And there comes the thought, like an old refrain,
Shall He abide when all vanisheth ?
When all else passeth, shall He remain ?
Howe’er it be, let the question pass;
We have learnt one lesson, poor leaves, from you.
Though we see but darkly as in a glass,
Could we bear, if we knew of, a clearer view ?
While the limbs are free and the warm blood
glows,
Let us make the most of each fleeting breath ;
Till we tread a measure at last —who knows ?
With them that dance in the dust of Death.
N.S.W.
F.S. BURNELL.

By sending direct to The Bulletin Office, Twenty-Six
Shillings sterling you can have The Bulletin posted
regularly to you every iveek for a year.
Cheques ; Drafts, etc., and all Business Communications
to be addressed to William Macleod, Managing Director.
11 mv to Address Betters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the pager
(and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some care as to
methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, advertise-
ments, etc.) should in no case be addressed to the Editor,
but to the Manager. To send them to the Editor is to
invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters to
anyone by name. “ The Editor, Bulletin,” is the proper
superscription for “ copy." Delay, and even loss, is
sometimes caused by addressing to persons instead of
officials. The person may not be there; the official
always is.
Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be refused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the pub-
lishers. The right to reproduce or translate any part of
the contents of this number is hereby expressly reserved by
The Editor will carefully read and, where practicable,
acknowledge in the “ Correspondence” column all contri-
butions submitted, whether in the form of Political,
Social, or other Articles, Verse, Short 'Tales or Sketches
(those dealing with Australian subjects, and not exceeding
two columns in length, or, say, 5000 words, are specially
acceptable), Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper Clippings.
All communications will be regarded as strictly confi-
dential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. xvhen the
address is specified and stamps are enclosed But no
MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these conditions
are observed. When MS. is forwarded without stamps
for return it ivill be understood that the copy is to be
destroyed if not acceptable. Personal applications for
return of MS. cannot receive attention. _ The Bulletin
desires, further, those sending MS. to distinctly under-
stand that it is not responsible for the preservation or
transmission of MS.

or other topical drawings in line or ivash, of sufficient
merit and interest and adapted for direct reproduction, or
any interesting photograph illustrative of a current event,
will be paid for same on acceptance. _ Any picture con-
taining a good idea, but unfitted for direct reproduction
— i.e., any picture which has to be re-drawn —will be paid
for as a suggestion only. The conditions under which
such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

THURSDAY, AUGUST 1, 1907.
WANTED A N.S. WALES CHAMPION.
The Bulletin, though an Australian newspaper,
and as such knowing no State, but only the
Commonwealth, cannot quite forget the fact
that it is published in Sydney (N.S.W.);
and that it would prefer to be published
in Sydney (N.S.W.) than in any other city of the
Commonwealth for reasons which will be obvious
to the intelligent Australian who has ever lived in
Sydnev (N.S.W.) for any length of time. With
the charm of winter in Sydney (N.S.W.) warm
upon it, The Bulletin prints, without charge, this
advertisement: —
WANTED, a champion for N. S. Wales. Should be a
man of Brains and Energy. Previous experience not
absolutely necessary. The Pay is Poor, but the Work is
Hard. Apply at once, in view of the imminence of the
State elections.
* * * *
It is another of the consequences of Federation.
If it were not for Federation the champion of the
State of New South Wales would not be needed.
As things are, he is a sternly necessary personage.
Consider the facts. N.S. Wales is the “Mother
State” of the Australias. It was the first
founded, and, excepting for an interval of about 36
years, it has always been the greatest in popula-
tion and production. At present it is ahead of any
rival in all that makes for material greatness.
Some few years ago, after standing for a long
time in the w&y of the n&tioiuil cispniition for
union, N. S. Wales invited the sister States to
join in a Commonwealth. The invitation was
accepted, and the Commonwealth came into
being, not without some shameful incidents,
arising from the fact that at that early stage
N. S. Wales had not a champion, and was
abominably misrepresented and traduced as a
faith-breaking community by its State politicians.
But that is of the past. The present position
is that N. S. Wales—the greatest and most popu-
lous State of the Commonwealth, the State which
has gained most from the Commonwealth in
manufactures, trade and revenue, the State which
has almost had a monopoly of the best positions
created by the Commonwealth—is represented by
its political leaders as a mean-minded, huckstering,
lying and generally disreputable community,
full of nasty little tricks and devices, with no
manners to speak of, no dignity, no sense 9 f
iustice, and but little honesty. The champion is
wanted to give the lie to all that, and to speak up
for New South Welshmen, and to declare that, in
spite of their horrid name, they’re as good Aus-
tralians as anybody else.
* * * *
A N.S.W. champion of the right sort would
devote his first speech, and his second speech
and his every speech, until this stigma had been
cleared off the N. S. Wales name—to declaring
that the people of N. S. Wales, however much
they have been misrepresented by their politicians
in the past, do not want any special concessions
in the Federation ; that, being the big brother in
the family, so to speak, they don’t want Pa to be
always interfering to give them all the jam and
the cake ; that they are quite as well prepared to
be neighborly and decent as the Tasmanians and
the Queenslanders. He would state, which is the
truth, that even the people of Sydney are not
filled with a ravening greed for special favors and
concessions, but are prepared to stand on their
own chances, like the people of any other city in
the Commonwealth. He would deny with indig-
nation the allegation that for some miserable
extra twopence of trade, to which it has no moral
or legal right, Sydney is prepared to throw bombs
at the temple of the nation’s unity. He would
gibbet as slanderers of N. S. Wales those un-
worthy citizens who, for the sake of some paltry
personal interest, or from sheer stupidity and
rheumy-eyed incapacity for seeing clearly, voice
discontent with Federation, and suggest a mad
attempt at secession.
* * * *
For such a champion there is a fine large honor-
able job. He could hope to get into Parliament
for almost any constituency— The Bulletin re-
fuses to believe that there are more than five
N.S.W. constituencies so disloyal and stupid as
to vote deliberately Anti-Australian when a clear
challenge is uttered. In Parliament he would be
very soon able to get to power, for nothing is
more certain than that the State Frights crowd
—even with all its miserable insectarians and
other noxious allies thrown in—does not represent
N.S.W. opinion, and that “ the other side’ only
wants to be advocated to be successful. Then for
the N.S.W. champion there would be open a life
of glorious usefulness, and when he died in
poverty the people would take up a collection and
bury him decently. But that is a side issue. All
politicians die poor because their pay is so miser-
able, and it is better to die poor and gloriously
than to die poor and a petty whiner and a miser-
able Mrs. Gummidge.
THE OLD, OLD STORY.
Melbourne, Thursday.—The restriction of Chinese
workers, particularly Chinese cabinet-makers, by special
clauses of industrial legislation, is deplored by the
Council of Churches. At its monthly meeting to-day,
the Council passed the following motion
“ iiince God made of one blood all nations, and in
Christ Jesus there is neither Jew nor Gentile, but all are
equal, this Council views with dissatisfaction the proposal
to differentiate between persons on the grounds of race
differences, and interference with the natural human
rights of families, whose heads are domiciled within the
Commonwealth.”
The Council also resolved to obtain an expert to inquire
into the charges levelled against Chinese workers, par-
ticularly those in the cabinet-making trade, of working
for under the legal wage, and more than the statutory
hours.— Daily paper.
It is all so like the Council of Churches—the
gathering of the Scribes and Pharisees who, as
their Lord said, lav heavy burdens upon others
and never dream of giving a personal lift with
these same burdens. No doubt the Council of
Churches is a wholly unconscious hypocrite ; it
has been the rich man’s satellite and parasite for
so very long that the part has become second
nature to it, and it hasn t the least idea that there
is any degradation attached thereto. The bulk
of the churches, sad to say, have always been
pro-nigger. They are not pro-mgger in
the sense of denouncing the system whereby
more than three-fourths of the people of the
British Empire are deprived of the political rights
of free British citizens, and are so deprived simply
because they are black or brown or yellow. That
would constitute an attack on the rich governing
classes of England, pn old British tradition, on
the doings and policy of the Throne and the
aristocracy j and the Council of Churches would
die first. But, so far as being pro-nigger means
the upholding of the right of the employer to
bring cheap colored labor into Australia, so as to
either deprive the white worker of his job, or
compel the white worker to get down to the
nigger’s level in wages and in his method of living
in order to keep his job, the churches are
mostly anti-Australian to the core. It is in
the working man’s districts that the discolored
races take' up their quarters, not in the
localities where the employer and the parson
reside, and, so long as that is the case, the
Churches don’t see why anybody should object.
Also, it is mostly the laborer’s daughter—not the
daughter of the parson or the capitalist—at whom
the Kanaka and the Hindu leer with the leer of
lascivious desire, and the Churches can’t see why
the laborer should object to have his daughter
leered at by Asia or Polynesia. And it isn t
worth the laborer’s while to explain. He would
have to leave off coal-lumping for much longer
than he can afford to get even the first beginnings
of his case into the understanding of the Council
of Churches, which is as utterly out of sympathy
with the masses as if they were beings from a
different planet, so that there is no common
ground from which the explanation can start.
* * * *
“God made of one blood all nations, says the
resolution of the Council of Churches. The state-
ment may be true, but, if the practice of the
Churches is anything to go by it is a lie. -there
are in Australia something like 2400 clerics, not
including the celibate priesthood of the Roman
Catholic Church. There are in the world perhaps
700 000,000 of the better variety of colored races—
the people of China, Japan, India, I ersia, liigypt,
Syria, Burmah, Siam, Arabia, and such like
folk with a capacity for education which makes
them quite distinct from the semi-animal
negro Many of the 2400 clerics are travelled
men who have seen at least something of these
700 000,000 better-class colored people; yet if
there is one cleric in Australia who has taken unto
himself a Chinese, Burmese, Indian, Cingalese,
or even a Persian wife, his address is quite un-
known at this office. And the address of the
parson who has married a Kanaka woman or a ne-
exessis buried in still deeper oblivion. The parsons
who make up the Council of Churches say that
God made all the races of mankind equal and of
one blood, but not one of them marries a colored
lady, even by accident. This enormous and con-
tinuous unanimity can’t be a mere casual hap-
pening. It can only arise from the fact that the
parson doesn’t, when it comes down to absolute
practice, believe a single word of his own theory.
He draws the color line as absolutely as any-
body else when the color line affects himself. He
doesn’t suppose that God made all races of one
blood—not when it becomes a matter of busi-
ness. And even if one crazy parson should here-
upon go off in a fit of despair, and marry a Chinese
or Cingalese woman in order to prove by a dead
lift the sincerity of his class, the proof will be no
further advanced. There should be no dead lift
in the matter. If the preacher person believes m
his own doctrine, it should be as casual and ordi-
nary and unremarkable for the young cleric who
goes on a holiday trip to Asia to come back
with a Jap or Cingalese wife, as for the one who
goes on a European trip to bring back an English or
Scotch or Irish or German bride. And it certainly
isn’t. The reverend brother who came back from
his holiday with a Cingalese wife would be as
great a cause of alarm and amazement to his kind
as the one who came back with an elephant.
There is only one thing which prevents the
Tory and nigger-loving Churches being regarded
with absolute hatred by the mass of the Aus-
tralian people, and that is the fact that they are
regarded with absolute contempt. The thing
that is despised can hardly be hated. The masses
are more intelligent -be Council of Churches
is aware of —far mon .celligent than the Council
itself. They observe that the clerics raise no
protest because the colored brother, who makes
up three-fourths of the population of the iLnipire,
is deprived of his political rights; if he wasnt
so deprived this alleged brother and equal would
seriously inconvenience the governing classes of
England. They know that the white missionary
carefully lives out of smell of his native flock,
though he professes total inability to understand
why the white laborer should object to the Syrian
and the Kanaka and the Chow intruding into the
localities in which poverty compels him to dwell.
They note that the white preacher never, by any
chance or accident, marries the colored sistei
whom he declares to be of the same blood as
himself, though the same preacher can’t see why
the white laborer should object to the Chinese
vegetable person making ad vances to his daughter
at the back door or over the rear fence. They are
fully aware that the Council of Churches is
chiefly concerned about its colored kin when it is a
question of getting the nigger into Australia as a
low-wage laborer, to deprive the white worker
of employment and to swell the profits of the
employer. They have realised long since that in
this matter the Churches are a public enemy—the
enemy of the white race and of the rising Aus-
tralian nation. They are not blind to the fact that
a whole horde of clerics have spent so much time
on the theory that God made the nigger of the
same blood as themselves, that they have had no
leisure to realise the much more obvious fact that
He made the wharf-laborer of the same blood as
the Archbishop of Sydney.
* * * *
In the special instance quoted at the head of
this article the Council of Churches shows its
usual complete lack of logic, its usual utter
lack of sympathy with the people, its usual
hopeless incapacity to understand that there
can be any side to any case except
the side of the Tory and the capitalist;
and this also the masses, who have a reasoning
power which the Council of Churches seems to
have lost, will not fail to note. For the Council
first denounces the proposal to differentiate
between the Chow and the Australian cabinet-
maker, and then decides to appoint an expert to
find out if there is any good reason for the pro-
posed differentiation. It denounces first, and
proposes to inquire afterwards. And it TPP a ’
rently assumes off-hand that the Victorian Chief
Inspector of Factories, who is an expert, and who
has reported over and over again that special
legislation is urgently required to make the China-
man obey the Factory Act, is a wholesale and
persistent liar. It would probably hesitate to
throw such an insult-by-implication at a nigger,
but the Victorian Chief Inspector of Factories
is a mere white man, so he doesn’t matter.
Furthermore, the mass of the community
is well aware that, though the Scripture says
that all races are created of one blood, it does
not say that it is well for alien races to live
together and to intermarry ; in fact, the vast pre-
cautions taken to ensure the racial purity of the
Hebrew people suggest the exact contrary. On
all these matters the Churches, in their haste to
assume that the nigger is always right, and that
the white man—unless he is an affluent lory white
man—i B always wrong, has got out of step with
the nation. Also it keeps out of step with
amazing persistence. If Australia becomes a god-
less community, it will fie because the Churches
have taken its 'God away from it. They have laid
it down —or many of them have —that religion
involves cheap alien labor, which must either
drive the white man out of the industrial field or
compel him to go down to the Asiatic or African
or Polynesian social level in order to stay in it;
and they seem to hold that piety is the natural
concomitant of Chinatown and mixed marriages
and strange smells and racial degradation. In
fact, the Council of Churches makes it a case of
religion v. civilisation. And the mass of the
community chooses civilisation.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
James McGowen, ’Bus-Loser.
The saddest figure in N.S.W. politics to-day is
that of Labor-leader McGowen. The opportunity
has come, but the man has not. As regards all
the big questions on which the coming State elec-
tion ought to turn, McGowen is either a person
who feebly says ditto to Carruthers, or a person
who contradicts him so faintly that the voice of
his dissent is hardly heard. There are two im-
portant matters on which a great leader would
fight the political battle, and on both of them
McGowen missed the ’bus so long ago that the
hardest running that a stout, middle-aged boiler-
maker is capable of isn’t likely to bring him again
within gunshot of the vehicle during the rest of
his life. In the first place, the Australian party
wants a leader. It wants someone who will take
the field against the parochialists, and who
will point out straightly and definitely tne mon-
strous lies with which the Sydney Anti-I ederal
party and its press organs are attempting to
create a secession movement. To a patriot and a
man with the fighting instinct this would be con-
genial work. It would also be congenial work to
a man who was no patriot, but a mere astute poli-
tician, with sufficient intelligence to see his adver-
sary’s weak point. Carruthers goes to the
country, in a measure, on a ridiculous platform of
alleged State grievances, and the alleged griev-
ances are bolstered up with fables that are so crude
and gross, and inventions that are so glaring, that
their exposure would be easy. In the Sydney
papers the supporters of Carruthsrs daily assail
the Government and Parliament, and people of
Australia, with vast slanders, and shameless
charges of ill-faith, and wild accusations re
alleged breaches of non-existent agreements and
alleged breakages of imaginary clauses of the Con-
stitution. A leader is to be judged by the land
of people he leads, an.d these individuals
are Carruthers’ followers to a man, and they
expound the same policy that he does. But
James McGowen, middle-aged, stout and ordi-
nary, says practically nothing. Apparently he is
just as parochial as the other person. Apparently
he cares just as little for the honor of his
country as the other fellow. Apparently he
has no more heard that there is such a place
as Australia than the other individual. He
has no enthusiasms and no large outlook. The
results of the last Australian elections showed
that there is in N.S.W. a strong Federal senti-
ment and a strong Australian party. The
yappers about bogus State grievances went
down in an amazing fashion when the
people could find candidates with a strong,
healthy Australian sentiment to vote for.
They would almost certainly go down again
if McGowen and his followers had sense enough
to possess a strong, healthy Australian sentiment,
but the part of the good Australian is left to the
lonely Owe’Sullivan ; and his past financial
riotings and his lamentable Sporting League are
against him. And as regards his other great
opportunity, McGowen misses the ’bus just as
badly. Carruthers goes to the country as the
man who is going to reduce the income-tax, and
to keep on borrowing. The McGowen party
objects to the reduction of the income-tax, and
also proposes to keep on borrowing. If it asked
the people to continue paying income-tax, so that
the loan disease might be abolished, and so that
N.S.W. might pay for its works out of revenue,
and make a begim mg at the reduction of its debts,
there is sufficient healthy anti-borrowing senti-
ment in the State to give the party a
great following. To urge the people to
go on paying income-tax out of mere cussed-
ness only raises the metallic laugh. No doubt
there are plenty of ways in which the
money might be honestly enough spent, but no
country wants a Government that proposes to
absolutely hunt for ways in which money may be
spent. N.S.W. is run by two party machines.
One machine has fallen into the hands of men
who are coarsely, viciously and mendaciously
anti-Australian. The other is run by men who
are dull and small and indifferent. As there
isn’t time to construct a new machine and
obliterate both Carruthers and McGowen, and
as the people with a strong Australian sentiment
are hardly likely to see any value in upsetting
Joseph, who is bad, to make room for James,
who is much the same, the probabilities are that
nothing worth mentioning will happen. That
will be McGowen’s epitaph ; it will be written on
his political tomb that he was the man that
nothing worth mentioning happened to. Also,
the inscription will say: “ Here lies the Loser of
the ’Bus.”
The Cry of a Crow-Eater Teacher.
“ Koala ’’—her wail: —
About a year ago I applied for a position as a pro-
visional teacher in S. Australia. Shortly afterwards I
received an offer of a special school, and as I was anxious
to be at work, I took it. For teaching this school I was to
receive £5 per child per annum, up to 12 children in
average attendance. Out of this I had to pay my board,
which is generally given to such teachers at 10s. pvr
week. I have been able to keep myself, but no more.
First of all I had to get three blank maps at a cost of
about 4s. each. Every school in the State must have
these, and instead of the Department getting them for
the schools and leaving them at the building, each
teacher must get her own and take them with her from
school to school as she is transferred. Then it is neces-
sary to teach geometry. The Department does not
supply the material. I had to buy a blackboard,
compass, and a book on geometry. Nature Study must
be taught, so books of some sort are needed. I got two
of Gillies (3s. 9d.) and a small magnifying glass (Is.).
Geography for the younger children is partly taught
from a new “ Collins Header” (10d.). There are three
manuals of arithmetic which every teacher must have
to teach from (ss. 6d. for the three). I got all these.
Dyes for brush work must be bought. I got
five colors at 6d. a tin. Had to get a Gillies’ history, at
Is. 3d., to teach from. Now it is considered too hard for
the children, so for next exam, we are to have a new
history. Tuning-fork for singing cost Is. Now we have
to make relief maps of the district, etc., on a foundation
of canvas, strongly framed in wood. So far, I’ve only a
makeshift one on calico, as the canvas is expensive. The
material for the relief cost me about Is. 6d. Now I’m
told I absolutely must have a book on teaching, so have
to get “ Colonel Parker’s Talks on Teaching.” I have
not procured this yet, so do not know the price. I ha\e
to pay carriage on all parcels of books, except those
from the Department, on which I only have to pay coach
freight. Other parcels have both rail and coach freight to
be paid. Of course, I get a discount on these books, but
it does not make up for the freight; so I lose on them.
Then I have to keep the school in towels, soap, broom,
dusters, pannikin, and wash bowl, also stamps, papei,
etc., for writing to the Department. In provisional
schools teachers are g'iven £2 a year for these last, but in
a special school the teacher has to find them, although
she has a lower salary.
Until this last month I did not have an average of more
than eleven for the year, so lost at least Bs. 4d. a month.
The boarding accommodation is awful. The first place 1
was at, they objected because I left the window open at
night, although there was no one in the room but me.
Where I am now they also object, but I’ve grown callous,
so take no notice. I have two miles to walk to school,
and while I’m there the family spends its time prying
over my things, trying on my dresses, and wearing my
boots, etc. Verily, a teacher’s life is not a bed of roses,
although I’m not the worst off. Some teachers do not
have more than nine or ten scholars.

The Woman Who Doesnt Know.
Some of the tales of life in Freetrade England, as
related by our present visitor, Miss Jessie
Ackkrmann :
Many of the mothers, who are forced through stress of
circumstances to seek employment during the daytime,
hand their infants over to a woman to look after tor a
certain consideration. This woman makes quite a pro-
fession of the care of children, but mars the nobleness o
her occupation by renting the babies out to or„an-
grinders and other similar classes of people. Ihe rate
charged for the use of an infant is usually 3d. per head
day. . . Someof the houses inhabited by the class of poor
alluded to were in a disgraceful condition, not " lt ‘
standing the fact that the tenants to pay rent
averaging between 6s. and 7s. a week. Oases we - a
where a person in each room had to sit up all n, = "t
keep the rats away from the children. The surroundings
were so repulsive that they beggared description.
Imagine four hundred thousand children in the Umtea
Kingdom annually born into unspeakable poverty, ine
33 per cent, who died within 12months were; ra her tc.be
env led than pitied. . . Girls received 6d. for making a
blouse that sold at 40s. A Royal Commission on sweat
ing, which cost £lOO,OOO, sat for two years and filed a
report, but nothing had been done. . . , v e
Miss Ackermann had often been asked-What can be
be done ? What is the solution of the problem ? But she
could not say. ,
That last item is the incredible part of the story,
Miss Ackermann “ could not say. bhe g
up. She is the Woman Who Doesn t Know.
Yet if she left London in a fast steamer l
land her, in about two days, in the capital
country that to a great extent does kn ,
country that has practically abolishe P
It had to break up the old land monopol
it, and Freetrade went under in the s * ru % b
ail severe poverty was abolished. _ 1 £
the bulk of the reformers are trying to abolish
poverty and yet retain land monopo y , . j
man still have 25,000 men s share of the nat
wealth and yet prevent the.other 24999 being
poor; to retain the system which has
landowner to drive the agricultural P P
of about 16 counties off theMand to
for game, and yet to prevent the citie , for(
the places these outcasts naturally , g th(J
being congested. Such mock i Britain
boss hypocrisy of the age. wealthy the
is sufficient to make its people the r ibuted,
world, if it were only more evenly
and other countries have tackled th


problem, and found it not wholly insurmountable.
The land difficulty comes first. Something might
be done there with a big graduated land-tax
which would compel the great British land-
owners to sell their estates in small ]o£s,
or by a wholesale system of compulsory
purchase ; these devices being accompanied by
laws which would rigidly limit the area of country
land which one man could hold, and which, when
the small holder preferred to let his land instead
of personally cultivating it, would give his tenant
the right of compulsory purchase at a moderate
price. That kind of reform would turn
Britain into a country of small farmers, each
owning his own bit of soil, and start the popula-
tion, which is now pouring into the cities, on the
back track to the land. The next essential is a
new tariff and a change in the incidence of taxa-
tion. The Briton, in his anxiety to promote
the foreign commerce which makes great,
bloated seaports like London, took almost
all the duties off imports, and as revenue
was necessary he heaped on himself a load of
direct taxation such as no other country on
earth endures. The landlord and the tax-gatherer
have almost killed agriculture and driven the old
agricultural population into the cities, and the
systematic petting and encouragement of foreign
commerce have bloated one or two seaports out
of all reason. By abolishing the landlord and
making the tax-gatherer deal more with the im-
porter and less with the agriculturist, Britain
could start some of these bloated populations on
the return journey. The High Protection which
this change would involve would create an in-
crease of manufacturing employment, alongside
the increase of agricultural employment. Then
to make sure that the worker benefited by that
increase of employment, Wages Boards could
enact that the girl who now makes the forty-shilling
blouse for 6d. should in future receive at least
3s. And as for the slums in which the sixpenny
girl now lives, such places can be rebuilt, provided
the peer who owns the slums is expropriated, to
begin with. Old-age pensions can provide for the
venerable poor. Alien restriction laws can pre-
vent the influx of a new mass of almost incur-
able poverty. Technical education can prevent
the growth of a new generation of people without
visible lawful means of support. The land-reform
idea Miss AcKERMANNcan find in pretty good work-
ing order in Denmark ; the High Protection and its
results, also the technical education system, can
be studied in Germany ; and, ii the lady wishes to
consider the possibilities of a rebuilt city, Paris is to
a great extent just such a city. Miss Ackermann
can ha,ve a look at the Wages Board system in
Victoria; at the old age pension method in
N.S.W.; at the undesirable immigration restric-
tion idea in Australia generally. Of course, it
may be complained that this policy is gigantic
and incredibly difficult. That may be so, but
that is not the point at issue. Miss Ackermann
doesn t say that the remedy is difficult, but that
she can’t suggest any remedy. She is the Woman
Who Doesn’t Know, and she ought to know. It
wasn’t worth while coming all this Way to tell us
that she Doesn’t Know. We have six State
Upper Houses filled with People Who Don’t
Know.

Land Laws and the Fruits Thereof.
Maoriland isn t satisfied with reckoning itself
up once every 10 years; it has a midway tally
on its own account, and some of the things it
found out 12 months ago have just been printed
inside the usual blue covers. And a few of them
are very fine things. The prevalence of the little
immigrant who didn t have to pass the Customs
is about the best of these fine things. Consider
the story of the little Maorilander of under five
years:—
•• •• Decrease 3,624
i»yi-ib9b .. .. Increase 455
1896-1901 .. .. Increase 3,147
1901-1906 .. .. Increase 15,939
When Ballance, and McKenzie, and Seddon
arrived with their policy of financial prudence
(sadly diluted since), and anti-big-landlordisin
and the cutting up of big estates, and a land-tax,
the little Maorilander was actually following the
moa and the other birds that have gone. In the first
five years of the new order of things the worst
possible movement in the way of depopulation
was checked ; and since then the gates have been
gradually swung right round. The young Maori-
lander stays nowadays and marries, and Maori-
land gets the best of all immigrants with-
out advertising for them or paying anything
for their passage money. Considering the
growth of the baby population, the propor-
tion of married Maorilander might have been
expected to fall; yet it hasn’t—the same influ-
ences have been at work to stiffen up those
I n . 1874, before the cream of the
gold had been skimmed off in the rickety islands
and just before Vogel had introduced a financial
policy more rickety still, and before the big
landlord was squeezing at his best (or worst), 35
out of every 100 Maoriland females were married.
Gradually the proportion dropped as the landlord
squeezed, until in 1891 it was under 31. In that
year Ballance arrived with his policy of land
settlement and arbitration, and, that policv
having continued without a break, the proportion
has risen steadily, until now it is back very nearly
to the old 35 per 100. The figures prove quite
conclusively that the Maoriland land policy of
compulsory purchase and progressive land-tax,
with something extra for the absentee, not only
attracts population from outside, but has brought
a prosperity which means also a larger increase in
the native-grown population.

No License.
“Num” writes:—
In the interests of temperance, let me raise a word of
warning about the no-license craze. At the outset, I
may say that lam a Prohibitionist. If I could wipe out
the sale, manufacture, and importation of all alcoholic
liquor, except for medicinal purposes, I would do so
to-morrow. But, at the forthcoming N. S. W. referendum
I intend to vote for a continuance of the existing number
of licenses, or, at most, for a reduction, simply because I
ion t want to see a large proportion of the residents of
this State converted into habitual secret drunkards.
I admit frankly that, under Prohibition, there would be
an enormous amount of sly-grog selling. Shanties would
spring up in all directions to cater for the demands of a
community, sprung from five hundred generations of
U ar u ke^ s ’ bufc tlle s, y-grog shanty in an absolutely
1 rohibition land would supply only the demands of those
who craved for drink. On the other hand, the mere
existence of no-license districts in a country where the im-
portation and manufacture of liquor were allowed, would
encourage the moderate drinker within the no-license
district taking the term “moderate drinker” to em-
brace the man who takes one glass a day and the man
who takes 10—to become a rum-soaked ruin. The
no-license advocates are for ever trotting out the
example of Balclutha (M.L.) as an evidence of the
excellent results of no-license. They allege that since
the system has been operative, the convictions for
drunkenness have numbered only one or two a year.
In precisely the same way, Sydney might have plumed
itself that there was no gambling in its boundaries prior
to the passing of the recent Acts, because street betting
was practically non-existent. The gambling went on
inside the tote shoes and two-up schools, just as to-day
the drinking goes on inside the houses of Balclutha.
This is absolutely proved by the statistics of the district,
which show that, since no-license was carried, more
alcohol is brought in than in the days when hotels
flourished. The only difference is that, while the old-time
Balelutha citizen had three glasses at a bar and was
locked up when he reached the street, he now
has ten glasses in his home and sleeps on the
stairs. With no license in Sydney, the same thing
would happen. The man who takes his one glass daily
would buy a keg to get that one glass, and, having it on
the premises, would proceed to empty it at a
rate that would never have been dreamt of
if it had remained under the publican’s counter.
Women, who would not otherwise acquire the taste
for drink, would learn the habit by having it
always handy ; and little Johnny, who would be bounced
out of a pub if he dared to think of a whisky, would go to
the cupboard when nobody was looking, and feel that he
was a real man as he surrounde l his nobbier. As for the
man who likes his five glasses a day, yet contrives to be a
tolerably decent member of society all the same, he
would lay in a barrel as big as a piano, and solemnly
sit throughout the days and nights in joyous sodden-
ness till the undertaker came. No-license—the spotting
of the country with casual Prohibition districts —simply
means wiping out drinking under restrictions in these
districts, and substituting drinking without restrictions.
While drink is made and imported there will be drunken-
ness, but to try and develop temperance by aggravating
that same drunkenness is the most remarkable curative
theory advanced since the days when they used to cure
burns with red-hot irons, and flog lunatics to make them
sane.
The Transcontinental Railway.
The Bulletin receives a letter from an old
and supposedly intelligent pressman well-known
throughout Australia, which letter fills it with
wonderment. The author writes in serious argu-
ment against the transcontinental railway on the
ground that it is impossible to fetch the trade of
Bort Darwin and thereabouts to any South Aus-
tralian or eastern Queensland port, and among
other things he puts forth this :
Three-fourths of the possibility [of settling- the Northern
lermory] depends on means of transit to the natural
ports. And where are they? Down at Port Pirie or
spencer s Gulf or on the coast of Queensland? I say no.
Port Darwin must be the main port of the new Common-
wealth State, with another at a site yet to be fixed on the
Itoper river. Dragging trade south or south-east is just
about as feasible as was the old notion of dragging* the
Barrier ore half-way across Australia to Newcastle.
It seems fair and reasonable to ask this person,
and other persons who write in the same
strain, who ever thought of carrying the trade of
the lerritory— most of the Territory, at all
events—anywhere else than to Darwin, or some
other Northern port? No sensible individual
that this paper ever heard of imagined that the
object of a transcontinental railway was to send
Port Darwin produce for shipment anywhere near
Adelaide, nor was it to send Adelaide produce for
shipment at Port Darwin. And in the same way
the object of the railway from Falmouth, in the
south of England, to Wick, in the north of Scot-
land, is not so that Wick can send all its goods to
be shipped at Falmouth, nor that Falmouth can
send all its goods to be shipped at Wick. Even
the great U.S. lines were not constructed so that
New York, New Orleans, San Francisco,
and Seattle might uselessly swap goods and
put them on board' ship at each other’s
wharves instead of at their own wharves. It
might even be added that the railwav from
London to Liverpool was not built so that each
city might uselessly send its heavy property for
shipment ac the other city. The idea of the Aus-
tralian transcontinental line is the same as the
idea of every other transcontinental line. It will
carry people to the Territory, it having been
proved that the prospects of settling the North
with a dense population, when that population
has to be carried all round Australia by sea and
depends on a long sea voyage for its communica-
tion with the rest of the country, are just about
as good as was the chance of settling British
Columbia when people had to reach it from
Eastern Canada by walking across the Continent,
or had to go by sea round Cape Horn, or across
Panama. Prospective settlers who can afford a
week to visit the North and look at a possible
property in an accessible place, but who can’t
afford two months or so to look at a property in
an inaccessible place, will begin to drift across
when the railway is constructed. Mails, dogs,
bagmen, insurance agents, land-jobbers, globe-
trotters and other hurried produce will also go
that way, and with the habit of going the Territory
will become known and familiar. It is only by it
becoming known and familiar that settlement can
be guided thither. Some perishable produce will
go right across the continent to save time, but
not much. Produce grown along the line will go
north or south, as it pleases. People who can’t
afford to ride by train will find the line handy to
walk on, and it will be used by bullocks to tres-
pass on. There will be stations at long intervals,
and water will have to be found there for the use
of the engines and passengers. Here and there
more water will be found than was anticipated,
and towns will spring up. Prospectors will go
forth and locate new mineral fields. Even a
desperately hard-up prospector will take chances
when there is a railway to fall back upon ; if the
worst kind of luck happens to him, he will get
back illegally concealed in a truck. Murderers
from the interior will be taken by train north or
south to be conveniently and comfortably
hanged, and inland lunatics will be conveyed
thereby to asylums. All this movement will
make people aware of the Territory, and
keep settlement moving that way. It will
change the North from a Robinson Crusoe
island to a part of the mainland. The arrival and
departure of a mail three times or six times a
week ; the sight of the train coming in from what
used to be the foreign land of Australia; the mere
existence of the two visible threads of rail, and
the knowledge that all along them to the big
cities lies a smattering of population, wayside
stations, tanks, goats, policemen, and railway
officials; the further knowledge that for defence
purposes the Territory has, by reason of that rail-
way, become part of Australia, instead of being a
place that is as completely outside any scheme of
Australian military defence as Timbuetoo; the
new temptation that will come upon old Terri-
torians to go forth once in a way and get drunk at
Adelaide or Melbourne, and see the great world
outside—all these things will start the ancient
property along. They will set it going, even as
the railway woke up British Columbia, and the
Washington Territory, and Arizona and Utah.
But as for the idea that the purpose of the line is
Port Darwin’s heavy goods a needless
2400 miles for shipment at Port !Pirie, or vice versa
—well, the man who dreamed of that is a person
of infinite jest, and should get a job at the Tivoli.
Even the railway from Sydney to Melbourne
wasn’t constructed so that New England wool
could be sent to Melbourne for shipment, and
Mallee wheat to Sydney.

The Cheap Clerk.
Regard this advertisement from the Melbourne
Erald of Saturday, July 13 :
QLERK thoroughly competent and trustworthy,
highly recommended, will take small wanes.
Apply Rev. S. J. Hoban, Central Mission, City.
It is a text which calls for the preaching of a
sermon to Methodist S. J. Hoban, parson of a
Church which at one time did a good deal to gain
the thanks of sweated Victorian workmen, but
which has now lost all its dignity and most of
its socal value by embarking on a wild spree of
J uddy intoxication. In this shameful advertise-
ment Hoban identifies himself and his pre-
cious “ Mission ” with the mercenary and greedy
class of employers, the “ State-salary-required ”
breed of vultures, the “ single-men-preferred ”
society, the “ bag-barons” of Ruskin.
Why does the miserable clerk of the Rev.
"'„~? 0 2 AN say that he “will take small
wages . It can’t be because he likes a small
salary better than a large one ; even clerks, who
mostly sue for the favor of the sweater all their
lives, are not built like that. This parson-boomed
clerk 7 is thoroughly competent and highly recom-
mended. He offers himself cheap, disloyally to
his fellows, because in cheapness he sees his best
chance of a job. His disloyalty to the army of
clerks, however, is nothing to the disloyalty of
Ti ®r- AN - to the Founder of his creed. His so-called
Mission ’ has become a touting-agent for the
modern sweater. It is an unutterably mournful
thing when some weak, poverty-distressed man
writes such an advertisement of his own volition ;
when a parson writes it for him, it stamps that
parson s church as the depraved servant of com-
mereial gretid. Parson Edgar once brought honor
to the Methodists by allying them to the anti-s weat-
uig movement. Parson Hoban proclaims him-
self the immediate agent of the sweater. Horrible
is the church variety of charity and of religion
when it begins to breed in a democratic denomiria-
tion, m a democratic community, the clerk “ who
will take small wages.”

And 10 per cent, for Modesty.
Carruthers having dropped his proposal to build
something on three legs between a remote suburb
and some scrub, and call it the Sydney-North
Shore bridge, another streak of humor has been
introduced to the matter of the bridge by an
engineer who proposes to do the work by Private
Enterprise. Private Enterprise, represented in
this case by a civil engineer with a solicitor to put
his humor into letter form, proposes, in short, that
it shall take the profits while the State takes the
risks. All it wants is that “your Government
will guarantee 3i per cent, on the cost”; in
other words, the Government is to pay a shade
more than it could itself borrow the money for
if it borrowed the money itsblf it would only
have to pay 3J per cent, at most, and anything it
got over- 3§ per cent, would be its own profit: if
it lets Private Enterprise, C.E., do the work, it
will still have to make itself responsible for 31
per cent., and anything the bridge brings in over
A - per cent, will be somebody else’s profit.
Private Enterprise remarks, upon the subject of
the guarantee, that it expects to make so much
profit that the guarantee will be really quite a
formal matter; but it proposes to take the
guarantee nevertheless. It doesn’t occur to it
that if there is going to be a profit in the business,
the people who guarantee the interest, and there-
fore practically provide the money, might as
well have it. In a burst of generosity,
however, Private Enterprise says that in
oO years the State may take over the bridge
at a price to be ascertained by arbitration plus
the cost of maintenance, if not charged against
the revenue from time to time, and plus the usual
10 per cent. The Bulletin doesn’t believe
Private Enterprise can afford to do it. After the
btate has raised the money to build the bridge
(whioh is wliat the3J per cent, guarantee means),
it hands the concern over to Private Enterprise
to make what it can out of the public which uses
the bridge. _ Private Enterprise takes no risk and
pays no price for that privilege. Then in 30
years Private Enterprise finds out what the bridge
is worth (not to the State which provided the
money to build it, but to the Private Enterprise
which sat on it and took toll for its use); and the
State which provided the money to build it will
be allowed to take possession of it by paying that
price, plus the cost of the paint used upon it, plus
the usual 10 per cent. Presumably the usual 10 per
cent, is a reward for Private Enterprise’s usual
modesty. But really the State can’t take so much
modesty for 10 per cent. Can’t Private Enter-
prise, C.E., suppress its natural feelings and make
it 25 per cent. ?

The Melbourne Building Trade.
Ihe disputants in the Melbourne building trade,
who came to a deadlock some seven months ago,
are likely to strike further trouble presently,
owing to the absence of any authority to settle
the terms of employment and enforce its decisions
on employee and employer. Towards the end of
last year the employees and employers, after some
weeks of idleness through a strike or lockout,
agreed to accept the arbitration of Justice Cussen
as to whether the men’s request for a 44-hours
week, or the employers’ offer of slightly increased
wages for 48 hours, or neither, or both, should be
accepted. The arbitrator’s award—leaving the
hoursat4Sand raising wages allround by about l|d.
per hour—was accepted on January 11, and ex-
pired on July 11. While it lasted it was loyally
by both parties, the “union” employees
and the ‘ union ” employers— i.e. , those belonging
to the Buildersand Contractors’Association. But
there were certain non-unionists—both employers
and employees—who did not regard themselves as
bound by the award. Consequently, though the
union builder, paying the stipulated wage, got
the best workmen and the other got the worst,
the latter was, and is, able to tender at a lower
price; and as the lower offer mostly gains the
contract, it follows that the non-union contractor
is getting more than his share of work, to the
detriment of the loyal unionist contractor and his
unionist employees. Since the award was made,
in January, both parties have been endeavoring
to persuade the non-unionists to come into the
respective folds, but without much success ; con-
sequently the unionist employers, seeing work slip-
ping away from them, and being no longer bound
by any agreement, may at any time decide to revert
to the former conditions, which will mean trouble.
Before the award ran out both the union em-
ployees and the union employers made advances
towards a conference ; but it was discovered that
the employers declined to discuss the question of
reducing the hours from 48 to 44, while the em-
ployees saw no use in a conference from which
this question was eliminated. Thereupon all
correspondence ceased, and there is the possi-
bmty of trouble arising any day. Justice Cussen
saw ahead as far as this at the time he inquired
into the matter, wherefore he made a suggestion
which has been studiously ignored—viz., that
some authoritative body should be appointed to
fix the legal hours, wages, terms of employment of
apprentices, and so forth. That body should be a
VVages Board under the Vic. Factories Act, for that
alone would abolish the non-unionist or blackleg
employees and employers, and would force them
to pay and to accept the same wages and hours as
the unionists, thereby putting all on an equal
footing, and giving both union employers and
workmen the fair deal that they do not get at
present. The Bulletin presents this suggestion
principally to the union employees, for, if a strike
or a lockout comes, it is these non-union men of
both sides who will be the cause of their defeat
if they lose, and the cause of much suffering,
bitterness, and pecuniary loss if they win. There
is but one way. to block these non-unionists—by
compulsory arbitration or a Wages Board. And
a Wages Board can be had for the asking.
