A Prayer.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
If a man dare send a Prayer
Forth to Thee, Almighty Goo-
Up the night’s translucent stair,
Where the planets, silver-shod,
Pace in shining robes of glory,
And the stars
Lift their heads ; where all the hoary
Constellations speed their cars—
Lo ! in humbleness of spirit,
A petition and a vow
Do I send—O Godhead, hear it—
Hear it now !
Not for splendor not for riches
Do I pray, 0 God, to Thee ;
From my soul’s new-hallowed niches
Have I cast idolatry !
I have broken all the idols—*
Images
That I bowed to ere the tidals,
Ere the mighty, swinging seas
Of Thy Purpose and Thy Power
Swept my life of all its fears !
Loud, I ask this simple dower—
Length of years !
Let me live that I may labor—
That mine hands may toil for Thee.
I would wield, 0 God, the sabre
Of the New Democracy !
Ere of death I feel the presage—
Ere my lips
Speak their latest earthly message,
And my soul sinks in eclipse !
I would lift a standard starry
For this people here below ;
Give me strength that flag to carry
Ere I go !
Lord, Thy priests they cringe and palter—
They have made a god of Gain,
And have #e't it on the altar
In Thy consecrated fane !
Yea, Thy laws they have forgotten—
In their eyes
Shines the lust of riches rotten,
And their mouths are filled with lies !
Sleek apostates—l would blast them—
I would smite them hip and thigh ;
From Thy Church, Lord, help me cast them
’Ere I die!
Lo ! they draw a tainted plunder
From the city’s reeking slums ;
I would wake them with the thunder
Of a new Crusader’s drums—
Not with brazen-clanging steeple,
Not with bells,
Would I lead, 0 Lord, Thy people—
I would sweep away the hells
Where Thy children drudge and perish,
Where the ghosts and paupers are ;
Lord, the wealth Thy churches cherish
Stinks afar !
Though the earnings of the harlot
Are the rack-rent of a soul,
From the woman clothed in scarlet
They have drawn a heavy dole !
Yea, Thy priests, O Lord, have battened
On the fee
Of the Magdalene, and fattened
On her shame and agony !
Beetle-like, in ebon raiment,
Thro’ the streets Thy “ servants ” creep;
Selling Heaven, on time-payment,
Very cheap.
Thou for man hast reared a palace
Roofed with clouds and sapphire skies;
Lo, where streams wind thro’ green valleys,
Nature’s shrines all leafy rise !
Gentle winds and treetops bending
Overhead.
Lift a requiem song transcending
For our dear ones lying dead.
Thy command was “Toil in gladness—
Life for ye shall be a feast! ”
But alas ! there entered sadness
With the Priest!
If a man dare send a Prayer
Forth to Thee, Almighty God —
Up the Night’s translucent stair
Where the planets , silver-shod,
Pace in shining robes of glory ;
And the stars
Lift their heads, where all the hoary
Constellations speed their cars —
Lo! in humbleness of spirit,
A petition and a vow
Do I send—O Godhead, hear it—
Hear it now !
VICTORIA.
GRANT HERVEY.


The Showman’s Moneymaker.—The Edi-
son Cinematograph—write for particulars. Films
in great variety in stock. We stock everything
for showmen. Osborn and Jerdan, Ltd., 393
George-street, wholesalers for Edison Phono-
graphs and products.*
and indoor physicians and surgeons—by the whole body
of governors. A governor is any gentleman; per-
son, bloke, cove or inhabitant who has, up to the day of
election, contributed the sum of one jim, quid, or
sovereign to the coffers of the institution. A legal
decision hath it that anyone who walks into the polling
booth, hands over £l, and gets a receipt, is entitled to
immediately vote. It is plain that any doctor or the
friends of any doctor can, on the afternoon of election
day, by a simple process of distributing subscriptions
amongst dependable folk, obtain a majority limited only
by the length of his purse. The most incompetent quack
in the world, for £3OOO or £4OOO or £5OOO, can make it a
certainty that he will be elected senior surgeon or senior
physician, whichever he chooses, of the Melbourne Hos-
pital. There are no Marquis of Queensberry or any other
sort of rules to govern the election. Nobody can be dis-
qualified for anything short of garrotting the returning
officer. Plumping is allowed, and the buying of votes is
part of the system, the act of incorporation actually
fixing the legal price of a vote at £l.
Of course, the system of election was adopted in order
that the very best medical men might be chosen. To be
on the honorary staff is worth a great deal of income to a
doctor. It gives him exceptional opportunities for
practice. He has t o lecture to embryo medieoes, who
thereby imbibe a profound respect for the members of
the honorary staff, and, when practising themselves, call
them in to consult. mean,big fees. Then
there is the prestige of the job—the prestige of having
been chosen out of all doctordom as the glassiest agate of
the lot. The public does not understand how the agate
is selected, and believes in its apparent glassiness. But
it is plain that the man with the most money, or even the
man with the biggest practice, is not the best man either
as a practitioner or a lecturer, and it is in these two
capacities that an honorary staff candidate has to be
regarded.
What is wanted at the Melbourne Hospital is a modern
method cl selecting honoraries. An electoral college
should be established—not an unwieldy body like the
S resent committee, which consists of nearly 30 members,
ut a handy college of about seven members. These
could be delegates chosen by the University, the hospital
governors or committee, and the Government. This
would provide a board of experts who could select the
honorary medical men with regard to ability only. All
the scandals and iniquities incidental to the present
system of election by bribery and canvass would then
disappear.
But the Melbourne Hospital is the most conservative
institution in Australia. It looks on the elections
as a means of getting extra subscriptions, and is loth to
make any change. Still the community is becoming not
a little scandalised, while the doctors—those of them who
still retain shreds of self-respect—are disgusted, and the
time is ripe for a new system. Thomas Bunt is known to
take a burly sort of interest in hospitals, and it is hoped
that on his return, if he is not at once thrown into ob-
sourity, he will forcibly set things on a fair, straight basis.
“Mexican Silver.”
A DEPUTATION from the Jewellers’ Trade Union,
supported by the Manufacturing Jewellers’ Asso-
ciation, interviewed Victoria’s Acting-Premier
lately, and gave him evidence of the existence of
the same sort of swindle that the Commerce Act
is trying to squelch when practised by the im-
porter. Instances were given of “customs of the
trade ” that really amount to plain fraud, swindle
and steal; of 10-carat gold being branded 15-carat;
of white metal being sold as “ Mexican silver ”
(it might just as honestly be branded Mexican
silk or oysters, for there is no atom of silver about
it); of brass rings being stamped 9-carat; and
of gold sleeve-link shells being stamped 15-
carat and filled with silver solder bought forls. lOd.
per oz. The deputation asked that the whole
matter should be handed over to the Australian
Parliament; but though Davies himself was
agreeable, he recollected that such action must
be agreed to by all the States, and that one State
is bossed by a cantankerous Premier who would
almost rather lose his billet than give the Com-
monwealth a chance to do some good. However,
he opined that the compulsory adoption and
registration of trade marks on the part of all
jewellers, together with the licensing and in-
spection of those individuals, would be of great
service. Doubtless it would; but there is no
necessity for more laws and regulations if
the existing law would only get over its
unhealthy reverence for the dishonest trader.
Take the case of the “Mexican silver”
man. Mexico is a great silver-producing
country, and its silver is the genuine
article of which the “ bob ” is manufactured—
is as genuine in its line as “ Mount Morgan
gold.” So the man who sells something worth
about Id. an ounce as “ Mexican silver ” could be
gaoled for six months on a charge of false pre-
tences, just like any other swindler, if the statutes
would cease to tremble in their boots at the idea
of active interference with a scoundrel because he
keeps a shop. And the man who sells brass stamped
as 9-carat gold, ought to be good for three years
if the law would only move—same as the indivi-
dual who sells a gold brick or a heap of shares in
an imaginary mine. There is no need for more
regulations; what is needed is a plain warrant
and a large, plain bobby, trap, cop, John, or
policeman.

Auditing the Ledger.
St. Ledger, the Queensland Senator, ought to
get himself audited and found incorrect. He has
been talking about the tremendous size of the
States’ surpluses, the result largely of the Com-
monwealth refunds; and the people and papers
that are always ready to grab at an excuse for
reducing taxes have jumped at his text that
£3,000,000 a year is being “ wrung out ” of about
4,000,000 people, and that “if Great Britain
exacted a proportionate surplus the Treasurer
would be £30,000,000 to the good.” Senator
Sledger and the whole brood carefully don’t
mention the other fact that if Great
Britain had a proportionate debt it would owe
£2,395,000,000 instead of the £788,990,000 which it
does owe ; and if Britain owed this extra
£1,606,010,000, it mightn’t reckon even £30,000,000
too much to “ wring out ” of its people to help
wipe the burden off. As a matter of fact, though
the British debt is less by £1,606,010,000 than it
would be if it were as big in proportion to popu-
lation as the Australian debt, Britain did “ wring
out” enough in 1906 to reduce it by £7,746,304.
When owing to heavy loan expenditure, the
British debt got up to figures more like our own,
though still a long way from them, the struggle
made by Britain to reduce the burden by
“wringing out” taxes would have horrified
Senator St. Cheque Book. In 1816, the
year after Waterloo, the funded debt was
equal to £900,436,000; twenty years later, when
Victoria arrived, it was down to £788,000,000,
which means that, in those few years, £112,436,000
was “ wrung out ” of the British people, in addi-
tion to the cost of current expenditure at home
and on wars, etc. St. Bill Book had, therefore,
probably best leave Britain out of the case
when he starts to talk about the tax-wringing
capacity of the Commonwealth. A nation that
thinks a debt so good a thing to get rid of that it
h as collected an income-tax of as much as 2s. in
the £ isn’t a good example for a man who wants
to argue as Sledger and his friends evidently
want to.

Listen to the voice of Joey Carruthers, as
reported in Sydney Telegraph :
Mr. Carruthers stated that Ministers had no prefer-
ence. “ I do not think,” he said, “ that any of us have
seen Canberra. Mr. Waddell certainly is in favor of
Lyndhurst. But our object is to place before the Federal
Government and Parliament the. whole of the sites which
New South Wales has offered, and at the same time to do
this in an impartial manner.
Once more it should be pointed out to the Aus-
tralian Parliament that Joe isn’t on in this act;
and that it is the business of the Australian
Parliament to select the best site—as it has appa-
rently done—whether Joe offers that site or not.
Above all, it is the duty of the Australian Parlia-
ment to remember that it is the guardian of the
rights and the dignity of the people of
Australia. One of these rights is that of choosing
their own capital—within the limits fixed by the
Constitution —through their elected Federal repre-
sentatives, and Joe isn’t a Federal representative.
Then, supposing the said representatives make a
selection which is in any way not according to the
Constitution, every citizen has the right to apply to
the High Court, and ask it to declare the act
null and void, but Joe isn’t the High Court. In
the whole business Joe has no status save what
he acquires through his own boundless imperti-
nence. And if the Australian Parliament gives
way to the brassy insolence of the JoE-creature,
and lets the people’s rights slip out of its hands
through mere lack of the courage to face Joe’s
clamor, it will be unworthy of its high position.
Australia lost a rare and valuable sort of bene-
factor in David Scott Mitchell, book-lover, who
died last week. Partly by inheritance, partly as
a result of his own efforts, Mitchell had a neat
fortune, as fortunes go in Australia. Also he had,
which was of greater importance, a love of his
native country, a taste in books, and a very
notable sturdiness of purpose. These three
together fitted him for the carrying through of a
unique task. He hoarded up, with the greed of
a miser and the devotion of a saint, all
the books, letters and documents appertaining to
the beginnings of this Commonwealth on which
he could lay his hands. It was work fitter for the
State to undertake, because of the vast expendi-
ture of money and industry which it involved.
But the State neglected the work, and Mitchell
stepped into the breach. His full purse, his
active brain, his stubborn purpose were devoted
with absolute singleness of aim to the one object—
the Mitchell Library. All Australian history to
the present day ishiddenin that library, in its books,
pamphlets, newspapers, playbills, racecards, pic-
tures —its complete record of all the activities of a
young nation in the making. His work finished,
Mitchell gave the collection to the State and
gave with it the bulk of his fortune, in order that
there might be funds always available for further
purchases, should there be anything left to pur-
chase. Then he died, refusing up to the last with
quiet persistence all thanks, all eulogy, all adver-
tisement. He even refused to be interviewed or
to supply his portrait to the papers. He wanted
nothing for his gift except an assurance that it
would be carefully housed. A truly curious
patriot who would take nothing for his great
work, not even huzzahs !

Religion keeps strange company at times. Miss
Jessie Ackermann, now representing the West-
minster Gazette hereabouts, tells of a woman
who worked for 62 years constructing confirma-
tion wreaths for the nice girls who put on white
robes, and make a solemn profession of faith in
the religion which teaches justice and charity,
and are blessed by the nice old bishop-character,
and sent away feeling thatthey have been very near
to the gates of Heaven. The woman received Is. 9d.
a dozen for making these wreaths, and into each
wreath 140 flowers had to be worked. She must
have made about 84 wreaths a week in order to
live on the most meagre principles—436B wreaths
a year—27o,Bl6 wreaths in 62 years of servitude.
And presumably these wreaths were worn by
270,816 Christian girls, who mostly passed on to
be Christian women, and married Christian hus-
bands, and reared Christian families. They wore
them in Heaven only knows how many Christian
churches, in the presence of Heaven only knows
how many Christian congregations, and they were
blessed by Heaven only knows how many nice, old
Christian bishops. But as not one among all that
vast army of Christians cared a darn for the
woman who made the wreaths, she finished up
quite naturally in the workhouse, after 62 years of
semi-starvation.

Slatyer, the Sydney architect who ran against
Spruce Myth last Federal election, is liable to
have some disrespect for the Law, The Sydney
Daily Telegraph, which just at that time had
been preaching that Socialism meant confiscation
and all sorts of horrors, placarded Slatyer
as a Socialist; and Judge Backhouse awarded
him some damages. But the Telegraph appealed
to the Full Court—the same Full Court that
couldn’t trust itself to decide whether Crick is a
gentleman or not—and the Fidl Court set aside
the verdict and ordered Slatyer to pay the
heavy costs of the double proceedings. In
other words, Slatyer is ordered to pay two
lots of costs instead of one because, according
to the Judges of the Full Court, a Judge of
another Court didn’t know his business. Or, in
the alternative, he has to pay two lots of costs
instead of none at all because the Full Court
doesn’t know its business. Either way, Slatyer
is entitled to be an annoyed architect, especially
as he didn’t appoint or choose either lot of judges.
If he had appointed Judge Backhouse, or even
if he had picked Judge Backhouse out of a list
of a dozen Judges, as a visiting team of footballers
picks out an umpire, there might be some justice
in asking him to put up with the consequences.
But when the public appoints the Judge, it would
look a lot more like a fair deal if the public had to
stand the racket.
The A.M.P. Society has just paid £lO2 10s. per
cent, for the privilege of investing £300,000 with
the Melbourne Metropolitan Board of Works, so
the society is really lending at about £3 18s. per
cent. That means that the tendency for bonuses
must be downwards, because until now the average
interest-earnings of the society were £4 9s. 7d.,
and they used to be much more. The fact is, the
society is evidently getting afraid of the land
mortgages that it used to put the bulk of its
funds into at sor 5J per cent. Land values have
been so forced up, because holders of good
lands monopolise such large areas, that the
A.M.P., with a vast amount of data in hand,
. knows that men can’t rely on making a
living at the prices. Some years ago the men
who control the huge business came to the con-
clusion that in the average well-favored district
£5 per acre was the maximum price that wheat
growing could afford to pay ; yet in many districts
£7 is demanded nowadays. So the A.M.P. has
to shrug its shoulders, pass that sort of
risk, and take one that returns £3 18s. per
cent.; and bonuses suffer. Meantime the men
who suffer most from the reduced bonuses
are, in very many cases, the very ones that kick
hardest against that progressive land-tax which
would bring land to something like its honest
value —the value at which the A.M.P. could again
afford to consider country mortgages in a large
way as gilt-edged security.

The Ward-Willoughby co. reopens at
Sydney Criterion on Saturday, in “ Mr. Hopkin-
son,” which is a comedy rather than a farce, and
abounds in dukes and other personages of great
social burden. Miss Grace Palotta is to be a
Duchess in “Mr. Hopkinson,” and The Bul-
letin would remark that she surely will
look like a Duchess, but it has a sad
memory of the only Duchess it ever saw
at close quarters. She was 65, and took
snuff visibly ; so it refrains. “Mr. Hopkinson,”
according to the judgment of some parts of Aus-
tralia, is the best of the pieces in this co.’s collec-
tion. It will probably be the farewell production
of the Ward-Willoughby combination. Mean-
while “The Squatter’s Daughter” pursues the
hurried and improbable tenor of its way until
Friday night. It isn’t leaving because its possi-
bilities are exhausted even now, but the lease is
up, and the new lessee wants possession, and has
told Mr. Anderson to take his flocks and herds
off the premises so that “Mr. Hopkinson” can
graze in the paddock.
** * *
“Dorothy,” with Lauri to introduce
modern variations, makes room at Her Majesty’s
(Sydney) at the end of this week for a revival of
“ La Mascotte.” Apropos those variations, which
the critic always grieves over while the audience
applauds, it is worth recalling that this isn’t the
first time the principal comedian did something to
turn “Dorothy” into “Laurithy.” When the
lady on the stairs says she’s embarrassed, the
hard-of-hearing sheriff’s officer used to ask : “ Oh,
you’ve been backing Paris ! ” To-day there is a
generation which only faintly remembers Paris as
a horse that left a hole in many pockets, so Lauri
in these times asks the lady if she has her leg in
plaster-of-Paris. It isn’t much of a joke ; but
then an established principal comedian’s jokes
needn’t be much better than a Judge’s to rouse
the vacant laugh. “La Mascotte” will come to
many as an even dimmer memory than Paris ; for
the fanciful little opera hasn’t been staged here
by J.O.W. for something like 12 years.
THE ONLY MEANS OF IDENTIFICATION,
OR THE AWFUL EFFECTS OF COAL-
DUST AT ADMIRALTY HOUSE.
The complaint "’as against the Pastoral Finance Asso-
ciation, Ltd., that, from its warehouse next door to
Admiralty House, there issued noise and smoke to such
an extent as to cause a nuisance. When the wind is
from the west, coal-dust permeates the whole of Ad-

carriages, seats, lawn, shrubs, trees, and flowers, to their
serious injury. —Daily Paper.

The Irish comedian has to be pretty bad
in order to make a failure of it, and, as Australia
found out some years ago, Michael Nolan isn’t a
bad comedian. He comes back with the old
mixed humor and pathos done up in new dis-
guises ; but the Irish comedian knows better than
to hang too heavy a disguise over the old stage-
Irish humor and pathos. After singing three
songs on Saturday at the Tivoli to an enthusiastic
house, he had to draw on his reserves and circulate
a new edition of “ Conn the Shaughraun.” Alto-
gether, Nolan is going to be a success. Bentley,
the other sort of comedian, made a reappearance
amidst a lot of joyful noise; and the Svengalis
continue their well-mounted piece of elaborate
trickery.
New English Farce and Song Co., led by
Miss Florence Baines, opens at Sydney Palace
next Saturday night in “Miss Lancashire
Limited.” The piece was written for Miss
Baines, who is said to have remarkable
sleight ot tongue with the dialect of the
clog-wearing country where the men used to
have kicking matches; also she is said
to be very handy with her feet in the matter of
clog-dancing. The fun of “Miss Lancashire
Limited ” rests mainly on the situations that
arise from the change of places of an heiress and a
servant girl. The latter floats herself into a com-
pany, and gives away debentures right and left to
tradesfolk and visitors, and the housedog, and
anything alive and friendly that comes along.
* * *- *
After a brief spell in which to give its
ear-drum and its artistic soul a rest from high-
class music, Sydney is bidden again to assemble
at the Town Hall, next Saturday, to welcome
Mrs. Blanche Arral. The charming little French
woman, with her sweet, amiable voice, is already
well known to Sydney; and she should be
especially welcome after a course of German
music that was mostly wrangling and confused
explanation.
To-morrow night will see the last of * ‘ The
Beautiful Fiend,” at Sydney Royal. On Saturday
Miss Hildyard will be seen in a play written by
herself and Russell Vaun, entitled “The Great
Awakening.” This should give the much-boomed
lady a fair chance of showing what she is really
made of. “The Beautiful Fiend ’ didn’t. It
only showed a good-looking lady in very fine
clothes acting an absurdity. The present dramatic
season at the Royal will last but six weeks in all.
Then a new co., with the well-known Harcourt
Beatty and Miss Madge Macintosh at its head,
will take the boards with “ Her Love Against the
World,” which title certainly suggests that she is
“ up against it.”
** * *
Roberto Hazon, Sydney’s greatest con-
ductor, who leaves for Europe shortly, will
be keenly missed. Full of talent and ambi-
tion, he dropped in here about 1887 as the
conductor of Simonsen’s Italian opera co.
Kowalski having resigned from the Philharmonic
in 1889, the committee reached out a large hand
and captured Hazon, and the society has never
looked back since the memorable concerts with
Santley, when Hazon wielded the oratorio baton
in three famous performances of “Elijah.”
** # *
The Besses o’ th’ Barn gave their first
grand uproar (in Melbourne) at the Town Hall
last Saturday, the usual organ recital having
waived its business claim in favor of the brass
band. Critically considered, an evening with
the band is not so improving to the musical taste
of the masses as a couple of hours with the_ city
organist. Nor can one pay the Besses a higher
compliment than in saying their full volume of
brass tone is sometimes suggestive of the organ’s
mellowness. Most of the time the Town Hall
seems the wrong place for an entertainment which
is naturally adapted to the freer atmosphere of
the outside world. The Besses play very
admirably. Some of their items have the value
that is peculiar to brass music. They impress
you at first. To the thousands of ears that have
not previously heard a first-class band the effect
of, say, “The Lost Chord ” comes as a new sensa-
tion, when they get it blown at them. But a long
selection from “Tannhauser” grows monotonous,
no matter how artistic the brazen treatment of the
music which was written for strings. The band
will boom twice a day throughout this week, and
on Monday it moves in the direction of Ballarat
and Geelong.

In trying to think of a not unkind, remark
to make upon “The Ragged Earl,” at Her
Majesty’s (Melbourne), this scribe has suddenly
struck the idea that Andrew Mack’s ornate
absurdity in three acts is as distinctively new
to this country as “The Fatal Wedding” was
sixteen months ago. “ The Wedding ” differs from
reasonable domestic melodrama to the same
degree that “The Ragged Earl” differs from,
say, “Dorothy Vernon.” Mack’s production,
described as “ a romance of 18th century Ireland,”
is a travesty of the ordinary picturesque costume
drama, but the impudence of its absurdity may
carry it along. Australia is getting much food
for astonishment from America, and if the public
be not already “full up” of such tough
fare, it may swallow even “ The Ragged
Earl.” The 18th century titled person is heroically
Irish, and frankly anti-English. Owing to his
habit of not exacting rint from his tenants (a
magnanimity quite foreign to the rale Irish lords
of the period), he is very hard up and intensely
popular. Somewhere in the neighborhood lives a
swate colleen named Kathleen, whose perfidious
English stepbrother wishes her to marry a dodder-
ing old English lord with _ a pantomime nose.
Therefore the maiden, disguised as a boy, flees to
the Ragged one’s dilapidated castle for refuge,
and that invincible young man decides to marry
Kathleen himself. After she is restored to the
care of her evil relative and the persecutionsof her
amorous scarecrow, the Earl comes along and
performs prodigies of ingenuity and valor in up-
setting the bridal arrangements. He sings love
songs at inopportune moments, arms himself with
a six-foot sword, disguises himself as the ancient
dodderer (nose and all), and secures his bride in
defiance of probability. It is a laughable night-
mare of a play, performed mainly by Andrew
Mack in his best artificial manner. A tuneful
chorus of 30 voices, a number of horses, an old-
fashioned carriage, and some highly respectable
scenery are features of the wild proceedings.
When it is all over there is nothing left for a
thoughtful observer but to pinch himself to see
whether he’s awake. And to his great surprise
he is.

Science and Rheumatic Rings.
Recent scientific investigation of the curative properties
of “ anti-rheumatic ” rings has resulted in a severe shock
to sceptics. It has been found that rings of a special
steel can be made in the form of powerful magnets,
which possess, beyond a doubt, curative powers in a
remarkable degree. This is less surprising when it is
remembered that magnetism is an essential element of
electricity, and the opinions of such men as “ Sir Charles
Locock, M.D., M.R.C.P., Henry Holland, William Fergu-
son, physicians to her late Majesty Queen Victoria, i.
Oppolzer, Chief Physician Imperial Hospital, Vienna, and
of a host of other well-known medical men who adopt such
agencies, carry with them the Hall-mark of science. It
must, however, be conceded that of the thousand and
one so-called “galvanic,” “electrical” and “anti-rheu-
matic ” rings at present manufactured for a few pence,
and retailed chiefly by jewellers for a few shillings, it is
doubtful if any one of them really possess a trace of
“ galvanism,” “ electricity,” or any curative virtue what-
ever. The world is itself a magnet, with magnetic currents
flowing continuously from North to South, permeating
every living thing. Science has long since demonstrated
that'these currents exercise a constant influence upon all
terrestrial matter, and the cur.tive powers of “anti-
rheumatic ” rings apparently exist in proportion to their
capability of increasing the normal flow of these currents
through‘the body. Whilst any ring constructed of
magnetic metal, such as iron, has this power in a slight
degree, the extraordinary efficacy of a ring which w
itself a powerful magnet has only recently been
realised as a result of scientific experiment. Anyone
can at once tell a genuine from a worthless ring. If it us
actively magnetic it ivill pick up or attract, by its o\i n
inherent power, any iron or steel article, e.g., a key,
penknife, etc., while persons of highly nervous tempera-
ment, possessing what is known to science as the “ mag-
netic sense,” are, on putting on a ring, immediately con-
scious of a tingling sensation in the hand and arm, and,
in rare cases, through the whole side of the body.
The construction of this valuable remedy in various
forms and amongst others in the form of rings for finger
wear, has been made the subject of world patents, which
include all methods known to science of constructing
permanent mag'nets in annular shape ; and whilst
medical men were at first amongst the most sceptical ot
believers, a Limited Company has now, with their sup-
port, been formed for the'sole purpose of the manufac-
ture and supply of these rings. This Company, under
the name of “ The Rheumagnetic Ring Company
Limited” (the rings are called “ Rheumagnetic " Rings!
has established its head office in Australia at io-4i
Melville-street, Hobart, Tasmania, whence full particu-
lars of the treatment can be obtained.*

Australia, will come to pass on Saturday, and
rumor alleges that Hall Caine’s dramatisation of
his own novel is a powerful play. Seemingly
Bland Holt reckons on large results from “The
Bondman.” He has engaged George Rignold to
come along and assist in the production, and fill
the part of Jason. George Rignold ! The name
has been merely a tradition to a number of young
people who will see Henry V.’s impersonator
for the first time on Saturday night. He has not
acted in Melbourne for some years. He was
commonly supposed to be now retired to the Cold
Country, whence he came. There was even
a dim suspicion that he had gone to the Undis-
covered Country from whose bourne no traveller
returns. Now he emerges suddenly from his
seclusion at Middle Harbor, Sydney, where he
has been chasing the snails off his chrysanthemums,
or something. A revival of George Rignold at
Bland Holt’s theatre is one of those unexpected
events that happen sometimes, and are received
with gladness like a first prize in a raffle. And
the Bland Holt mounting of the Hall Caine
drama is to be stupendous.
* * *
At Melbourne Princess’s the new musical
comedy co. will shortly cease to skip by the light
of “ The Blue Moon.” On Saturday it is to intro-
duce “ Lady Madcap ” to this easy-going country,
and, seeing that the rest of the season is limited to
a fortnight, the Melbourne district must lose no
time in trying the change of bill. The box plan
is fully prepared to receive booking in advance.
Also, the public is exhorted to remember an
ancient maxim concerning the early bird and the
early door.
** * *
Matters at the Opera House are much the
same as they were last week, with two additions.
One of them is Hamilton Hill, an Australian
baritone, who had a fine voice and a painfully
wooden deportment eight years ago, when his
fresh young manly warble procured him an
American engagement. The pleasant voice has
lost some freshness, but in place of the wooden
young Hamilton of yore, who sang with his chin
pressed against his collar, there is a rather festive
Hill, who spreads himself considerably after he
has walked on in a hurry. Another recent arrival
is Carden Wilson, an Australian mimic, whose
“People I Have Met” lacks only one weighty
consideration. It isn’t an imported “turn.” By
the way, an advertisement in the Rickards pro-
gramme states that business manager Fred
Aydon has become the licensee of the Opera
House Bars, and all the bottles on the premises
will henceforth contain Nothing but the Best.

AT POVERTY POINT.
A genuine bush girl seems as hard to depict
on the stage as in a book, and Eugenie Duggan, in “The
Squa,tters Daughter,” looks her part as badly as she acts
it. Imagine a bush girl arriving home immaculate and
smooth-haired after a mad kangaroo gallop ! And she
doesn’t, as a rule, require the assistance of half a dozen
men to get off her horse ; neither does she strike an atti-
tude and dramatically tell the hands how the ’roo was
fought and defeated. Neither does she call a coward a
coe-ward,” or say “lahve” for love. The stage bush
daughter does everything that a bush girl does not do.
That sort never went mustering or kangarooing further
away than Redfern. The blacks with fat legs are also
very unlifelike, and finally the sight of a German sausage
on an out-station sent me home no longer wondering why
a cocky in the gallery had called it “ Blather”—the play,
I mean, not the sausage.

A NOVEL APPLICATION.
Mary Nugent Jones sought to recover 255 11s. 7d. from
Margaret Ellen Costello, the executrix of the estate of
the late Patrick Jones.
In June, 1893, the applicant obtained a divorce from
Jones, who was ordered to pay costs, but he had died
without paying them.
0 Death , where is thy sting? O grave, thy victory?
Silas Snell ”: The Irish population of the Common-
wealth seems to find itself very much under a compli-
ment to Andrew Mack for his gracious representations of
sons of Erin. Because his somewhat narrow dramatic
bent induces him to confine his attention to picturesque
Irish characters, he is plied with compliments, fed with
gratuitous dinners, and rewarded with illuminated
addresses, setting forth the quaint idea that Ireland and
the Irish are under a great burden of gratitude to him for
turning - down the conventional stage Hibernian, and
giving the true Irish Irishman. This is pure spoof.
Mack s Irishman is as much a stage convention as any
Boueicaultian creation. Mack’s Paddy, in “ The Way to
Kenniare,” immediately he returns to the “ Ould Sod,”
becomes a true son of Boucicault, and as utterly unlike
any sane Irishman I have ever met as is the broad
travesty of the music-halls. Then every Mack drama has
its Irish villain of the Feeney type—the peculiar, whining,
w’heedling, black-browed scoundrel whom Irishmen
loathe. What has Mack done for the Irishman that
Boucicault did not do?

write theatrical notes for your paper, I hustled round

Julius Knight. (2) That Miss Orton and J. Knight do
not speak to each other off the stage. (3) That Miss
Orton is engaged to some quite insignificant member of
the co., name unknown. (4) That Miss Orton is breaking
her heart because Julius Knight is so disagreeable at
rehearsals. (5) That J. Knight admires Miss Orton more

Elbert Orton gets £25 a week. (7) That she doesn’t get

(13) That she is English, and a widow. (14) That she is

facts.” (There was more, but memory gives out here.)
Melbourne thus far has two sorts of indoor rinks where
the conditions are antagonistic to the sentiment of
skating as an open-air exercise. The atmosphere is lack-
ing at the Glaciarium, as at the South Yarra roller rink.
But a new skating ground, on the lines of the popular
Bondi resort, is to be opened at St. Kilda in a few weeks
time, under the management of young Bernard Quine (an
usher at Her Majesty’s Theatre), who has fixed up a little
syndicate, with one of the Taits as the moving spirit.
The paled enclosure, situated in the “ Dreamland ”
vicinity, will have a granolithic floor and an assorted
stock of skates, including some of the newest roller type.
Given a balmy spring, the St. Kilda Sea-Air Rink should
get a boomsome beginning, anyhow.
In her marriage certificate Edna May is described as
aged 26, which indicates that she must have been an
infant prodigy when she first took on a theatrical career.
It is seven years since the much-boomed beauty jumped
into prominence in London with Musgrove’s “ Belle of
New York” co,, and for some years previously she was a
chorus girl in New York. She had a considerable experi-
ence of married life as the wife of Fred Titus, at one time
a champion racing cyclist. Edna’s real age is probably
nearer 36 than 26, but probably her 22-year-old husband
wouldn’t believe it. The writer saw Edna in London
on two occasions in 1903, and came to the
conclusion that, both as an actress and a
beauty, she is one of the most glaring false alarms
that ever fooled the British public. On the stage in
“ The School Girl,” her acting didn’t justify her rise to
anything beyond the dignity of a seat warmer. Still she
looked as nice as her photos—even nicer, seeing that her
face has in real life more animation. The second time
this scribe saw Edna they met in the crush room of the
Lyric Theatre. At close range, the fair one is a sad
disillusionment. With a good press agent and a good
photographer, it is dead easy to be a beauty actress in
London.
a : 0t another dose of Boucicault in Melbourne,
Andrew, Mack supplying- “ Arrah-na-Pogue” at Her
Majesty s, and I have formed myself into a society for
being dog-weary of this make of Irish play. Was a more
wearisome character than Shaun the Post ever put on the
boards bv a reputable play-maker ? The inhuman good-
ness of Boueicault’s hero as interpreted by Mack is only a
little less irritating than his unmitigated'blarney, broken
with spots of bathos, clotted and unwholesome, barren of
the smallest touch of passion. Never does Shaun become
an actual thing, a living, breathing sinner. He’s a sort
or Irish doll, stuffed with “ purty ” sentiments. His
love-making is the most artificial gammon a nice girl was
ever doomed to suffer under. He is an Irish arcadian
carman, as real as a pottery shepherd after Watteau, and
what anybody can want with him passes understanding.
I weary of Shaun, and Con, and the rest of the tribe.
“Hamer”: My heart goes out in sympathy to Mus-
grove over his troubles with his opera co. I have had
troubles myself ; in fact, 1 was such a small item occa-
?L° na L ly r com P arison with the troubles that I may say
they had me. One occasion was when my leading'lady
had received a notice in the Poverty Flat Gazette
that, she said, made her ill. She didn’t trouble to get a
medical certificate. She just came down to the theatre
and expressed her bitterness of spirit in a volume of scorn
—mostly scorn of me—and had hysterics in the centre of
the stage. As the hysterics seemed permanent, and
the house was filling (or, rather, getting less empty),
I decided that a change of play was necessary. The com-
pany, with the alternative of being owed one night’s
salary less, was mostly willing, but the “old man”
informed me that he was “ an actor, not an acrobat,” and
refused to change his costume. Also, he insisted on
bringing in his “ fattest ” lines from the other piece
in quite inappropriate places. The situation got
more mixed when the leading lady, finding her-
self ignored, rushed on to the stage and announced
with conviction that Heaven would guard her. She
was so annoyed at the idea that the show could go on
without her that members of the company, when they
were off the stage, had to be detailed to watch her
and prevent her running on to enact the heroine of
the postponed piece ; and this further confused matters.
Still as most of the company took advantage of the
general confusion to punch out all the good lines in all
the pieces they knew, the audience seemed pleased ; a
townsman, who professed to be travelled, said it was the
finest performance he had seen since he witnessed
Julius Irving in “ The Bells.”
King Ned is reported to have been much annoyed at
the Lord Chamberlain’s absurd vetoing of Gilbert and
Sullivan’s “Mikado,” what time the little Jap prince was
in London. Presumably it was a seared War Office, or a
syndicate of Anglicised German financiers, that induced
the Lord High Prohibitioner to truckle thusly to the
small, brown stranger.
For anyone who remembers the first Australian pro-
duction of “Dorothy,” at Melbourne Princess’s, with
Leonora Braham in the name part, there will be sadness
in this mention of the fact that Miss Braham has lately
been playing Mother Hunt—an old hag—in a London
production of “Olancarty.”

Still another fashion in stage names finds an exponent
in Fanny Dango, younger sister of Letty Lind. The fan-
dango-named lady is to make an Australian start in musi-
cal comedy with “ The Dairymaids.” Are there any more
such names aroundV “Poll Carr” would fit a light-
comedy lady nicely, and “ Tarrant Eile ” wouldn’t be bad
for the adventuress of B. Stormer’s Dramatic Company.
“ U.” : How girls rush anything with a kiss suggestion 1
The allegation that Arrah-na-Pogue’s name has a kiss
meaning always brings along a crop of damsels who were
too young to see the play last time it was revived, and
they arrive to find in it less hug than they expected.
When I start out as a dramatist I intend to make strong
appeal to this demand, especially in the titles. For
instance, “ Tracked by a Kiss ” (detective drama), “ Three
Kisses and a Jest” (farce-comedy), “Kissed in a
Culvert ” (curtain-raiser), “The Kiss Fiend ” (for B. Holt),
“ Kissed to Hades ” (Andersonian), and “ The Conquering
Kiss ” (for dear Julius).
According to telegrams from Maoriland, Musgrove is
working a troubled passage. He and his Germans began
to collide in Auckland. One programme was abandoned,
and the old uncertainty, whereby the announcement of
“ Faust ” made it almost certain that the result would he
“ The Flying Dutchman,” set in once more.

English reputation.” Nothing very definite is said about
her mumming capabilities.
“ Bill Outback ” : Have sampled every theatrical show
in Sydney during my month’s sojourn in the Big Smoke.
Can you tell me why the first member of the theatrical
orchestra to take his place is invariably the double-bass?
Once the cornet-player nearly broke this weird and won-
derful rule. He poked his head out from underneath the
stage for a moment—then he changed his mind and dis-
appeared. The procedure may be show etiquette. If
there happened to be no double-bass player, which
instrumentalist would be likely to appear first? Please,
reply. The information will cheer me during my wan-
derings West-o’-Darling.
A recent “draw” at London Hippodrome was “Fred
Lindsay, the Australian Stockwhip King.” With a thong
24ft. long, Lindsay cuts a wine bottle in halves; he
captures a fleeing man with it, using it as a lasso ;
and he gently ties the lash in a knot around a
girl’s bare arm (“ unskilful manipulation would re-
sult in the limb being cut clean off,” says the
reassuring advt.). He also skips within the “deadly
coils,” and generally seems to play with the fearsome
length of leather as if it were so much darning wool. At
the same show are “Jackson and MacLaren, Double-
handed Saw Champions of Australia,” who divorce logs
in record time, what time Jackson, who’s also “ Champion
Wood-chopper of the Commonwealth,” isn’t strenuously
obscuring the scenery with chips, amid great applause.
A cynical commentator on Edna May’s wedding and
the well-advertised secrecy of the performance, remarks
that the motor-car which bore the bride to the place of
marriage was “decked with white blossoms, in order to
escape observation.” Owing to Edna’s precautions, she
was chased to the ceremony by a numerous force of news-
paper reporters and camera fiends, and the latter con-
trived to “take” her whilst she was shrinking from
publicity.

The stepmother story was varied in the
case of the wife of Mackgar Lucas, a Collingwood
(Vic.) confectioner hailing from the JEgean Sea.
Mackgar, on the third night after his marriage,
remarked cheerfully to his bride, “Every time I
go near you I see the face of my dead wife,” and
the children of Mackgar would not say “Thank
you” when the supplanter brought in the break-
fast. After six days, the new-made missus went
out to buy some curry-powder, and did not return,
in addition to having no curry for dinner, Mackgar
claimed to have lost £3O, and his flying spouse
was arrested. She described the money as a
wedding present, and was discharged. Australian
maidens should henceforth be careful not to marry
widowed Greeks. Wedded life is made unpleasant
by the presence of a ghostly third party in the
bridal chamber.
‘ ‘ The Gunyah ” : If the law is an ass it is
at least a serious ass. Consider this instance :
The other day Justice Isaacs heard, in Chambers,
a case in which a man was applying for an ex-
tension of time within which his patent could be
sealed. After listening to the desiccated skite of
counsel for nearly a day, the Judge suddenly
became doubtful whether he could hear the case
in Chambers, as the matter was one in which
the public was interested. After lassoing and
perusing various Acts and legal authorities, he
decided to hear the case in open Court within
10 minutes. In the meantime the Court was dis-
robed of its holiday attire and dressed out in its
every-day working togs. The associate, crier, etc.,
were rounded up and yarded, while the Bar
decked itself out in full war paint. The crier
uttered his usual chant, the Court was declared
sitting, and thedoors wereopened to admit the rush
of the General Public. One dog looked in. After
it had gone, the Court started afresh, while
Counsel repeated the same old arguments. In
this way the Law was fully carried out, but how
much extra Costs arose through that false start
only the client knows. The starting gate is much
needed in Court.

“B.” writes :
Botanist Maiden’s “Life of Joseph Banks ”might in
any case lie heavily on the stomach of N.S.W., hut Joseph
Carruthers’promise to superintend the cooking- threatens
widespread indigestion. Banks has for some years past
been appropriated by a small semi-political party in
N.S.W., which has come to regard him as its own find,
and is determined to rake in the whole proceeds of its
luck. In the circumstances, it would be a fair thing for
the party to work the mine at its own expense, and then
nobody could complain, but it wants a State subsidy for
deep-sinking into Banks. So, having got the manuscript
of the “Life ” together, the party went to Joseph Carru-
thers to induce that financier to impose the cost of pub-
lication on the public funds. And Joseph agreed, pro-
vided he had a finger in the pie. That is no safe-
guard for the public. It is only adding to the number
of cooks, and it is reckoned by competent authorities
that no sausage which was fried by more than four
people ever amounted to much.
“ G. Elb.” complains :—•
The military department is a wonderful institution.
The warriors of Mount Gambier (S.A.) some months ago
forwarded a requisition for the regulation overcoats, but
when General Hoad arrived at the potato town to inspect
them he found that the men had been charging the
blank enemy through a wet season without proper pro-
tection from the rain. However, the central authorities
had made a desperate effort, and the coats travelled in
the same train as the General. But that keen soldier’s
eye detected a grave error—the buttons were of the
wrong pattern, and the slit at the back was an inch too
long, or short. The dry-goods were accordingly re-
turned, and, probably, when S.A.’s thermometer h s
climbed so high that you can only reach the temperature
with a ladder, the heavy garments will arrive at Mr.
Gambier with proper buttons, and torn in the rear to the
legal extent.

yarns, “ Hamer ” has had many royal times with

from which brand he draws his inspiration—
D.C.L. does the trick. Such a merry blend of
humor must be helped into being by The Whisky
of The Wise. It is branded D.C.L. on every
joke.*
About some banking people :
T. A. Dibbs the other day finished his 60th year with
the Commercial Bank of Sydney, 40 of the time as general
manager; and the shareholders are going to have his
picture done in oils and hung up in the banking chamber
at the head office. There are two portraits there already
—those of Richard Jones and Edward Knox. The latter,
who was managing director as long ago as 1847, was asso-
ciated with the bank until six years back, as chairman.
Richard Jones, who was chairman for many years, in
between, lived a strenuous life. He was a printer by-
trade, and, coming to Sydney- in 1838, worked for some
years on the Monitor, and then went up to Maitland and
established the Mercury. Afterwards he was Treasurer
in Oowper’s Ministry. As something of a coin-
cidence, the first time he ran for Parliament
he bumped against R. G. Massie, and Massie
defeated him. Later on he helped Massie’s son—H.H.,
the cricketer—into the bank, where in eight years he be-
came an inspector, and is likely to get still higher ; also,
as a side-issue, he married the general manager’s
daughter. It is nearly always a good thing to marry the
general manager’s daughter ; and it is even better when
you are nearly good enough to deserve lier.
Dust-laying Experiment.— A number of
representatives of the railways, tramways, Harbor
Trust, and other Government departments, and of various
municipal councils, were present at a dust-laying experi-
ment on Wednesday, conducted by Messrs. Tyree Ltd.,
with the Tyree Spray. The street selected is one of the
dustiest in Sydney—the thoroughfare running from
Circular Quay to Flood’s wharf. A 40-gallon sprayer was
used. The machine covered 600yds. of road with a fine
coat of tar in 15 minutes, and then covered the same area
with another coat in the next 15 minutes, making 1200yds.
in 30 minutes. The cost of the carbide used was Is. Mr.
Tyree, managing director, explained ifiat for big jobs
larger machines could be used, and still better results
obtained. With tar at 3d. per gallon, and carbide at 2d.
per lb., the cost of spraying is I-10th of a penny per yard
for a thin ccat, and l-sth of a penny for a thick coat,
plus cost of man, horse, and dray. These figures compare
very favorably with the cost of spreading tar in Sydney,
which varies for different work at from 4d. to Bd. per
yard.*
WITH THE GODS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I paid my modest shilling at the little window wicket,
And made my upward journey on a winding stair of
stone;
A sombre-faced collector took my somewhat greasy
ticket,
And I found a seat, and waited, with the High Gods, all
alone.
I conned the flimsy programme in a half-light, dim, un-
certain,
And peered through voids abysmal, looking stageward
from the height,
As I waited for the rising of the heavy velvet curtain
That shuts the promised myst’ries of the wedded arts
from sight.
In a sort of dim enchantment did I watch the motle3'
faces
Of the lovers of the Muses —in the gods the love is
strong ;
In the stalls and in the circle they were filling in the
spaces,
And around me ever thicker, ever denser, pressed the
throng.
The lights shot up, and louder rose the hum of conversa-
tion ;
The orchestra came trooping in from regions under-
ground ;
The amphitheatre simmered with a pleasant animation,
While tuning fiddles quavered out their wailing threads
of sound.
And then a hush! The baton for a moment pauses
lifted —
A faint and dying murmur, and the overture begins.
A sound of sea wind stirring ; a melody has drifted,
Like a misty breath of music, from the leading violins !
It was then the spirit took me right away to realms of
splendor;
Right away from sordid shadows that had dogged me
night and day,
Into regions of romances, grand and ancient, fresh and
tender;
Bore me out with wondrous music, took me upward and
away !
By the art of some great master, who had conjured with
a keyboard,
Did the music speak its wonders—dream, and doubt, and
quake with fears;
Wail like wind among the pine-trees, roar like breakers on
the seaboard,
Shake with passion, laugh with pleasure, tremble into
human tears.
As the curtain rose I heard it like a theme behind the
voices ;
I heard it drift to pathos, or to triumph rise and swell
In a storm of mighty melody that riots and rejoices,
Did it touch impassioned summits where apart the High
Gods dwell.
’Twas a tragedy to music, and it moved through all its
phases
With a soul-absorbing action that was ominous with fate.
’Twas a play of human passions, set in pitfalls and in
mazes,
Where love was like a dove held in the vulture claws of
hate!
And beyond it all, like spirits, I could hear the fiddles
moaning ;
I could hear the human sobbing of the cornets, flutes,
and strings,
For the wrongs that have no righting, the deeds past all
atoning,
The Dead Sea fruit of riches, and the hopes with broken
wings.
Then I felt myself borne downward from the heights of
splendid sorrow;
Down the stony stairs, and outward to the real and dingy
street—
Yet I knew T that I had lived, betwixt the sordid day and
morrow,
A whole sublime existence that w'as full of bitter sweet.
What of these, the dingy alleys, and the coarse and ribald
voices—
The details, all unlovely, of the life I knew too well?
I had soared on w’ings of music, as it sorrows or rejoices
To the passions, to Olympus, w’here apart the High Gods
dwell!

SOCIETY
PENFOLD'S
WINES
John Henry Whitton pleaded guilty
t’other day to a charge of theft from the ware-
house of his employers, E. W. Mills and Co., of
Wellington, Maoriland. The police report indi-
cated that J.H.W. (who possesses £5OOO apart
from his salary, by the way; also a motor car
and other things) had worked a systematic fraud
on his firm for at least two years, and the Judge
agreed that the evidence established the existence
of such a fraudulent system. Then the Mayor of
Wellington, and the Rev. Joshua Jones, in whose
church Whitton is a vestry man, and about 14
other prominent citizens stepped forward and
gave the prisoner a “character,” i.e., explained
how amazed they were to find he hadn’t got one.
And the Judge, after expressing “the regret and
sorrow he felt in seeing a gentleman of such
standing in such a position,” proceeded to admit
the persecuted one to probation. Now, was this
the precise sort of case, one wonders, that the
Maoriland Probation Act was designed to meet ?
THE ONLY WAY.
“ And do you never think seriously of getting married?"
“And It is the only way I do think of it, and it’s why I don’t,"
Maoriland Tourist Department received a
weird communication the other day. It came
from an individual on the other side of the planet,
He stated that he was a missionary, and he
wanted to know “ how it would do to try and get
a large lot of Bibles, New Testaments, religious
tracts, etc., carried through to your country.”
He evidently took the Maorilanders for heathens
of the worst type. If only he knew that every
second shop in M.L. is a book store, carrying a
large stock of Bibles, and that every second per-
son is either a prohibitionist or a fanatic yelling
for the immediate abolition of the tote !
If Mildura has not convinced all Aus-
tralia that there is a fortune in fruit-growing it
has at least solved one little problem of some im-
portance —and that is the best modus vivendi
’twixt the worker and his beer, until, anyhow, the
State or the municipality takes the liquor busi-
ness in hand. Mildura started on dead Prohibi-
tion lines, and consequently Mildura became
rotten with sly grog. Finally,
to stamp that out, it took the
business into its own hands
and started clubs. There
are three of them now—
the “ Goldtop,” the “Sil-
vertop, ” and the “ Toe-
riggers.” The “Toe-
r iggers’ ” club consists
of the bulk of the small
settlers and the work-
ers of the settlement.
It gets its beer from
Melbourne, keeps it in
a cool cellar, and re-
tails it at 3d. a pint.
It sells nothing that is
not true to brand ; it
pays its manager and
stewards liberal wages ;
it has billiard, card,
reading and writing rooms
for members. There are roaring fires
in the winter, and electric fans and
any quantity of ice in the summer.
The club enforces strict discipline.
If any member becomes offensively
drunk, or is shown to be drinking
to excess, it cuts his supply off for a
month. Its subscription is ss. per
year, and its last half-year’s profit
ran close up to £l7OO. It has a fair
building now, but purposes to go one
better next year. Much depends, no
doubt, upon how such a concern is run.
There have been similar clubs in
Western N.S.W. that have slowly
degenerated into drinking hells —the
decent members have “ pulled out,”
and those remained who simply
wanted their liquor cheap so that they
could get more of it for the money.
The best way to guard against that is
to make the municipality responsible.
Then the boozers couldn’t set them-
selves up in a club of their own. But,
when well-managed, the Mildura device
is a practical bit of co-operation, with some
advantages that even ordinary co-operation hasn’t
got.
* * * *
The pigtailed brother now smuggles opium
into the Common wealth by a very simple process.
Tins half-filled with the drug are dropped over-
board—if possible, on a rising tide—as an incoming
vessel enters the harbor, and the waiting con-
federates either pick them up as they wash ashore
or go out in a boat to meet them. Quite a little
fleet of opium was sighted recently drifting into
Watson’s Bay, and in the race between the
Customs people and a boatload of Chows the
white men got there first. Probably this is only
one of 3G ways in which opium comes in ; in fact,
the Customs have so many suspicions that they
think of having an officer on each Asiatic boat as
a permanent passenger.
A Melbourne coffee palace proprietor was
lately asked by P.M. Dwyer what excuse he had
for putting water in the milk he sold. He had no
excuse whatever, but he was a very indignant
milkseller nevertheless ; and he said he considered
that the inspectors had visited his establishment
under false pretences, because they didn’t tell
him who they were. Dwyer’s reply met the com-
plaint amply : “ There is such a thing as receiving
money under false pretences.” But the milk-and-
water vendor was unabashed : “ I hope your
Worship will deal with the case with the contempt
it deserves. It is a paltry matter, and I should
not be brought here like a criminal.” But this
“criminal” had met the wrong sort of beak:
“The matter is simple enough. Instead of
getting the natural product they asked for, you
give the inspectors an adulterated article. You
are fined £lO, with 265. costs.” The indignant
adulterator didn’t say whether Dwyer, P.M., was
contemptuous enough to suit him. Perhaps he
was. A man who sells one part of water to
every four parts of milk would probably not worry
much about a £lO fine. But he would worry a
good deal about three months’ imprisonment
without the option.

A STRANGER TO DECEIT.
THE JOHNNIE: “To he quite plain with you,
Miss Pert ”
MISS PERT (gushingly): “ Oh, my dear Mr.
Mugg, I’m sure you couldn’t he otherwise.”
Steadily this poor old world flattens out.
The recent great fall of rock at South Head,
Sydney, is one more item in the process —a very
small item, but enough to serve as a reminder.
Ma State has been robbed again. The sea eats
away the coast. The rains and the rivers wash
down the mountains, and cast them into the great
deep. The Bondi sewer helps to carry solid
matter into the sea. The throwing of ashes,
potato skins, bones, and empty bottles overboard
from ships is a steady throwing of our all-too-
scarce land into the all-too-plentiful ocean. The
sinking of ships hurries on the process. So does
burial at sea. When the land is flattened out
there is enough ocean to cover the whole globe at
least half-a-mile deep, and though half-a-mile of
water may not look much on top of 7000 odd
miles of land, it will be more than enough.
Already the bit of land which once joined Aus-
tralia to Asia is gone. Likewise, the bit which
joined England to France. The North Sea and
the Baltic are both recent encroachments.
Atlantis is a lost continent. The Polynesian
isles are the relics of another. The strait between
Australia and Tasmania is a bite which the sea has
taken out of the land. A time may yet come when
man will try to keep the sea off the last dead-flat
continent, by erecting a barricade of gin cases and
sand bags round the coast, and when that goes the
human race will retire from business, and “ a
shoreless ocean tumble round the globe.”

An old identity writes :
Sydney University threatens to become notorious for
its imported eccentricities. It began with a Lieut-Col.
of Royal Engineers, who made himself illustrious by
stating in the public press that the battle-value of good
rifle-shooting is not worth considering. Then it imported
the immortal Jordan, and Anally it brought along
MacMillan Brown, from Maoriland, with a cranky theory
about the evolution of a race of blacks in Australia. Here
it is as the S.M. Herald interprets it: —
“ The Northern Territory cannot be occupied by the
present race of whites, because the violet rays of the sun
have been shown to destroy the nerves. The violet rays
are used by Finsen and others for the eradication of lupus
and other diseases. If the rays are so powerful they will
destroy the nerve-tissue. Therefore, it is not only the
heat, but also the light that makes the white race in the
Northern Territory impossible.”
If that is Prof. Brown’s reasoning, or anything like a fair
summary of it, it sets forth not only that the white race
but also the black is impossible in the Territory, and
more than that, no land animal with a brain and a spinal
cord can live there. Why the robust white man, well-
protected with clothing, should not have more chance of
surviving the action of the alleged “violet rays” than
the weakly and unclothed black, the professor does not
state, nor does he give his authority for the alleged in-
fluence of the violet rays.
And his yarn overlooks the quite surprising fact that
all the time the white man, “ who can’t live in the Terri-
tory,” is living there, and has been doing it for ever so
many years. There may not be many of him, but 4000
healthy white men are as good for purposes of proof as
4,000,000. Moreover, the white man is, and has been for
many years, on each side of the Territory, in Queensland
and Westralia, and north of it in Java ; and he has hung
out for centuries in exactly the same latitudes in South
Africa and South America. And in all these places
he has been under the same sun and in the same kind of
light. Surely a paper or a professor that thinks the sun
shines with a kind of light in the Northern Territory that
is quite different from the kind of light it uses in the same
latitudes in Queensland and Westralia and South Africa
and Bolivia and Peru, doesn’t expect to be taken seriously.

Whooping cough is a very dangerous dis-
ease unless treated properly. Statistics show that
there are more deaths from it than from scarlet
fever. You cannot mistake the symptoms—pro-
tracted coughing, worse at night—peculiar sound
or whoop—labored expectoration of mucus—appar-
ent suffocation after coughing. Give Chamber-
lain’s Cough Remedy at once, repeating the dose
frequently. The quick relief afforded by Cham-
berlain’s Cough Remedy makes it a favorite with
mothers of small children. It liquefies the tough
mucus, making it easier to expectorate, keeps the
cough loose, and counteracts any tendency toward
pneumonia. This remedy has been used in many
epidemics of whooping cough, and always with
perfect success. There is no danger whatever
from the disease when it is freely given. It
contains nothing injurious, and may be given as
confidently to a baby as to an adult.*
“ Coalopolis ” : Was confronted with the
following horrifying E. News placard in George-
street (22/7/’O7): —
FRIGHTFUL RAILWAY ACCIDENT.
EXCURSION TRAINS IN COLLISION.
MANY KILLED AND INJURED.
AT SYDNEY RAILWAY STATION.
NEARLY A PANIC.
Then I ran, like the fresh, spring antelope, for
the nearest tram going stationwards, and duly
arrived at the scene of carnage. Considering
the alleged extensive death-roll, there were
very few persons about. Looked everywhere,
but saw no sign of bloodshed, so thought
I’d buy a paper and get details. Then I found
that the poster referred to two separate occur-
rences—the one a railway accident in U.S.A.,
the other a comparatively small row, disturbance,
or scrap at Sydney railway station. Discovered,
on close inspection, that the News had salved
what does duty for its conscience by scratching a
thin, black line between “Many Killed and In-
jured ” and “At Sydney Railway Station.”
Would be interested to learn if the News regards
this sort of thing as business or just plain robbery,
* * * <
Melbourne Argus publishes a list of dead
City Churches with details of their fate. The
Independent Church (Thomas O’Dell’s) is now a
tinsmith’s factory. The Presbyterian Church
(Rev J. Ballantyne’s) in the same street, opposite
the Hospital, was vacated in 1874, was for a time
used as the Insolvency Court, and is now the
Masonic Hall. St Enoch’s, Collins-street (Rev.
A. M. Ramsay’s), was sold by the mortgagees, and
converted into an Assembly Hall. The Congrega-
tional Church, Victoria-parade, has become apartof
St. Vincent’s Hospital. Chalmers Church (Adam
Cairns’), on Eastern Hill, is the site of an R.C.
educational institution. St. James’ old cathedral
is now surrounded by massive piles of buildings,
and can only be found by the worshipper who
seeks it with all his heart. Respecting the
Chalmers Church, the sale to the Roman Catholics
was achieved by stratagem. When it was found
that the Church would have to go, the remnant
of the congregation declared that the R.C.’s should
not have the property—Adam Cairns would not
rest in his grave if it were so disposed of, So the
place was sold to an earnest Protestant from
Sydney (he was a colonel among other things)
and he promptly re-sold to Archbishop Carr,
And whether Adam Cairns rests in his grave or
not no man can tell. The grave hasn’t been
opened.

The drapery man isn’t the only sweater.
The N.S.W. Bankruptcy Court lately elicited the
fact that the Borough of West Narrabri (the
wealthy Buchanan’s Killarney Estate hangs there-
abouts) gave its combined council clerk, inspector
of nuisances, and maintenance man the sumptuous
salary of thirty whole shillings weekly. Up to a
few months ago, the screw was twelve munificent
shillings more, but the Council became shocked
at the way the money was going out, and the
amount was reduced. The officer went broke —it
is hard to see what more the archangel Gabriel
could have done under like conditions ; and the
official assignee said that the only asset in sight
was “household furniture £3 175.” Seventy-
seven shillings’ worth of household furniture isn’t
much, but when a man with seven young children
is toiling for a crowd of hog-like aldermen at a
remuneration of 30s. a week it is as much as could
be expected.

Although the Associated Colliers managed
to force the union coal lumpers of Sydney
to agree to work with the blacklegs, no
one is foolish enough to suppose that the empty-
ing out of the dirty water will not be resumed imme-
diately the clean “is available. And the employer
is not wholly to blame for it either. It is one of
the natural results of blacklegging, and as nature
will take its course, the blacklegs must be emptied
out when the strike is over. It is only the fact
that the unionists are the better men that makes
the employers want them back again ; while the
first-class unionist who will return a bigger profit
is available, the employer is not going to he
foolish enough to employ the indifferent blackleg,
and thereby cut down his own profits. So the
non-unionist is petted while the row is on; then
he is tolerated ; then he is glared at; then he is
cold-shouldered ; and, if he hasn’t taken the hint
when that last stage is readied, he is plain sacked,
and goes forth to make a fresh start blacklegging
at some other business.

A Melbourne man discovered, the other
day, that if his neighbor’s poultry trespassed on
his garden, he might lawfully destroy the goose,
but not the duck. The law apparently regards
the goose as a rival in some way.
THE DRUM.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The sound of the trumpets was muffled, sobbing
Through eddies of fog that rose and curled —
Down in the mist a drum was throbbing
To the saddest time in the whole wide world.
Down in the roadway we heard the gravel
Crunch ’neath the tramp of pacing men,
The stamp of hoofs and a tossing snaffle,
And then the sob of the drum again.
The grey mist covered them well in the valley ;
Like a level sea its surface showed,
Hiding the heroes of sortie and sally
And that which they carried along the road.
The grieving music that Time has hallowed
Set the pace of the marching men.
The trumpets quavered ; and then there followed
The solemn beat of the drum again.
The sun rose over the mists, and glittered
On windows set on the highest slope ;
Birds through the clear air called and twittered ;
The blue sky spoke of a deathless hope.
The sunlight reddened with sweet persistence
Rimu and punga, wattle and gum.
Was thg rest but a dream ? Away in the distance
Numbing and slow went the sobbing drum.
MAORILAND.
WILL LAWSON.

Concerning the picturesque “Big Hole”
swindle, the Australian Star (Sydney) made
a ton of copy out of the case, and at time of
writing had published 10 articles giving the
details of the take-down. Exactly where the
Star obtained all the letters and so forth is
not clear, but it made the case so notorious
that Fraser’s two grown-up sons, who held
rather good positions in Sydney, and were
able to assist their mother a little to support
her eight young children, lost their billets. They
were thrown out on account of their celebrated
father. Rumor has not accused the Star of
having sent the family even the value of the 10
articles published up to date. It is now
generally understood that the detective genius
who reported Fraser as having got between
£BOOO and £lO,OOO out of the swindle had
his leg pulled pretty hard. The cheerful
readiness with which a man will magnify his
misfortunes is one of the worst features of human
vanity. He likes to pose, in Shakspearian style,
as “ a fellow that hath had losses.” Walter Besant
struck the same tendency when he made Mrs.
Bormalack, of the boarding-house, introduce the
miserable elderly junior clerk as Mr. Josephus
Coppin, “who has been unfortunate.” Fraser
seems to have been a charming letter writer, and
contrived to make his dissertations on mining
quite interesting. Apparently he knew his
people fairly well, and they seem by the stuff he
ladled out to them to have been mostly Deformers.

English, but which, being interpreted, means a form that,
having first been burned by the earth’s internal fires, and

finally forced through fissures made by enormous pressure
through the earth’s crust.. .. Mr. Stangar assures us that

politics generally, and N.S.W. politics in particular. At
this distance it looks certain that your Labor members
could alter the whole face of your statute books in an
afternoon’s sitting. ...A Socialistic Parliament, as the
outcome of your next elections, would shut down every
British-worked mine on the Australian continent. You
must not wonder at the uneasiness which some of ours
feel at the contemplation of such a possibility, and you
must own there is color in the possibility.

boshers who were in the net, and who were nicely

they were being advised to keep a sharp watch

“Vulcan” writes with a shade of irri-
tation :—•
The illuminating thing about “ Itan ” (B. 11/7/’O7) is his
brilliantly susta.ned inaccuracy. I am connected with
members of the Bagmen’s Association, and know many
Democratic sympathisers therein, and, as a whole,
haven t found them more solidly Anti-Sosh than any
other not-directly-interested class. True, bagmen get
sea-travelling concessions-—so do sportsmen, actors,
school teachers, hospital nurses, turf spielers, and other
persons who travel regularly or in quantity. Allegation
that bagmen get special railway reductions is ridiculous.
Without his season ticket, he pays the ordinary fare, and
“Itan” himself can get a yearly ticket for the same
price as the most favored bagman. Also, “ Itan ” may
have sleeping berths at reduced prices by purchasing them
by the dozen, as the Ass. does for its members. Hotel
concessions apply to members and outsiders alike—
regularity and quantity again ! Statement that
the big warehouses will not employ outsiders,” and
that “it is hopeless to trade outside the ranks,”
is pure drivel. Some of the biggest men on the
lines don’t belong to the „Association, and hun-
dreds of others successfully work outside it. Judging by
the fluency of “ Itan’s” remarks, getting “inside” cer-
tainly isn’t “as easy as telling lies,” but the sole condi-
tions of entry are that a candidate must be at least 21
years old, have represented a wholesale house for six
months immediately before application, and be proposed
and seconded by a member. The “exclusive union”
admitted 3-13 new members on these conditions during
the first half of 1907 (Jan.-June report). The Ass. endows
bursaries, scholarships, etc., for members’ children, has
a free mortuary fund (£75 allotment last year), sick and
indigence funds, and old-age pensions for members and
members’widow's, and, naturally isn’t inviting an indis-
criminate rush to share these benefits. That’s w'hy it has
to define a “traveller,” but the definition is "simple
enough.
* * * »
Judkins ought to rejoice over this Papuan
regulation:—
Any native w'ho plays cards for money or money’s worth,
or who gambles in any other way, commits a forbidden
act, and shall be liable as follows For the first offence,
to a fine of not more than £l, or imprisonment with hard
labor for not more than one month. For a second offence,
to a fine of not less than £l, and not more than £3, or
imprisonment with hard labor for not less than one
month, and not more than three months. For a third,
or any subsequent offence, to a fine of not less than £3,
and not more than £5, or imprisonment with hard labor
for not less than three months, and not more than six
months.
It was the holy saw-millers and other native-labor
employers who steered that regulation into the
code ; for Tommy Papua would, as a rule, rather
play euchre under a shady palm than hump tim-
ber or tinned dog any-day. So it is provided that
the fines imposed on “laborers under contract”
may be deducted from their wages, and the size of
the hole that a £5 fine makes in a third-offender’s
annual wages is so big that what is left only makes
a rim round the cavern.
Maoriland at last makes a move to com-
plete its famous Midland Railway—Christchurch
to the West Coast. A tender to construct 5J
miles of tunnel under Arthur’s Pass has been
accepted, and the price is £599,794. The con-
tractors are all Maorilanders. At present, the
railway plods up from Greymouth to Otira,
struggling along for miles in the shingle of a river
bed. Then, after the traveller has filled himself
with victuals at Otira, it is an afternoon’s drive
to bleak, lone, wind-blown Bealey, where yon
shiver through the night. Next day, you take a
header for about six hours down into the
Canterbury Plains, and strike bottom| in time
to catch the Christchurch train. If your vehicle
bounces when it strikes bottom the train
waits till it finishes bouncing. It is a splen-
didly picturesque drive, and even the joy of
being stifled in 5| miles of tunnel isn’t likeiy to
attract the tourist in any large quantities away
from the old coach-track. The route is seen
at its best going from east to west. Then you
take the real big grade—the one where the coach
goes down the wall of the mountain like a fly
going down a hotel —from the top, and the plunge
is wonderful and exhilarating. The coach is said
to usually turn two somersaults going down, but
that is somewhat of an exaggeration.
“Andante”: Be the alleged doubtful
morality of the N.S.W. State school teacher, the
fatherly Department goes even farther than stated
(B. 4/7/’O7). In the case of small country schools,
where the nearest resident may be half-a-mile or
more away, it sometimes happens that there is no
attendance on very wet days. When this hap-
pens the teacher is instructed to forward a report,
stating at what hour he arrived at his post, and at
what hour he left; and, as plain evidence that
the teacher’s unsupported word will not be taken,
the report must be certified by the signatures of
at least two responsible residents.

The smart Yankee “organising” secretary
of the Victorian Y.M.C. A. is growing monotonous
with his glorious schemes for collecting cash. A
report of his recent catchpenny proceedings at
Bendigo and elsewhere disgusted a number of
Melbourne capitalists who have given handsome
subscriptions privately towards a building fund
that seems to have no end. The Y.M.C.A. is
regarded by many people—especially merchants
and other employers of “genteel” labor—as a
very good cause, provided it keeps the mem-
bers from sinful manipulation of the ledgers, etc.
But it isn’t good enough to justify the eternal
importunities of a begging secretary, who pre-
sumably draws commission on his “orders” in
addition to a fat salary. American methods of
raising Australian money for highly moral pur-
poses are not more deserving than Ada Ward’s
devices for raising a controversy. For that
matter, the method in Ada’s madness is the most
amusing methodism on the market, and The
Bulletin would like to hear her propounding the
theory that no Young Man Christian can be saved
from Sin until he has given it a fair chance to
enchain him.

The Melbourne thief has an alert profes-
sional mind. He misses few opportunities. The
other day there was a collision in Elizabeth-street,
and a buggy was wrecked. When the busy helpers
had cleared the horse from the ruins, and while
they were still spraying the damaged owner, a
kindly soul undertook to hold the nag. He
is still holding it; the trouble is that no one seems
to know what county he is holding it in. The
horse left no address ; neither did the man. It is
firmly believed that the buggy would also have
been stolen if it hadn’t been an unsaleable wreck.
