Moved by Dr. Springthorpe’s charges of
gross and continued neglect, Vic. Chief Secretary
Peacock threatens to inquire deeply into the
management of the Bent State’s lunatic asylums.
For a start, he has learnt that attendants at the
big metropolitan asylum at Kew work 74 and 65
hours onalternateweeks, besidesdoingreserveduty
of three hours on each of two nights. Out of this
145 hours a fortnight, however, they get time off
for meals when on duty—viz., half-an-hour for
breakfast, the same for dinner, and 15 minutes for
tea. What a nice, placid, forbearing, nerve-
soothing attendant for the insane that attendant
must be who is just finishing his 13J hours day
and has inside him three bolted meals !
Edward VII. is about to establish a new
decoration for “underground workers.” This
doesn’t mean that the rabbit, or the subterranean
political engineer, or the fellow who burrows
under another man’s job will be decorated. The
statement refers to a medal Edward proposes to
hang on individuals who drag people out of holes.
Soon Edward VII. will have tacked portions of
the alphabet, or metal discs like the thing you
get as a guarantee that your luggage will turn up
at the other end of the journey, on the majority
of his subjects. Even the public servants who
attend to the sewers, if they stick to the business
long enough, are liable to become 1.5.0. (not Inde-
fatigable Sewerage Officer). When the King is not
opening shows and champagne, or trying to pick
the winner, he retires to his office and thinks out
a new decoration, medal, certificate, or button.
Balmain has committed the sublime
stupidity of deciding for an electric lighting
plant of its own, instead of arranging to take its
current from the Central Power House of the
Sydney City Council. While it is a step in the
right direction to substitute electricity for the
mixture delivered through the mains of that big
cuttle-fish, the Australian Gaslight Co., Balmain
should have had a little more sense, and realised
the obvious fact that electricity can be generated
at far less cost in one big central power-house
than in a multiplicity of small scattered plants.
The fatuous policy of erecting seventeen power-
houses where one would do the work arises
partly from municipal vanity. Every petty
suburb wants to have its own works,
with its own staff to bully, and its own
opening ceremony, and its own mayor, tricked
out in a ’possum rug, posing in the limelight.
Also, the policy arises in part from the loan of
the municipal ear to the pushing young men who
represent the importers of electrical machinery.
These bagmen never cease to breathe into the
bowl at the side of the municipal head that it
would be much more satisfactory to be entirely
independent of the city, and that the way to
arrive at that state of disunion and disorganisa-
tion is to buy Jubble’s latest plant. The result
of having all these needless plants to keep up is
that electricity costs Id. a unit more than it need
do. It also means that the suburbs totter under a
needless debt. But by rigidly refusing to have any •
thing to do with Sydney, the suburban alderme .
postpone the day of municipal union and of the .■
own extinction. It is no wonder that Sydney is
the home of such Anti-Federalism as is floating
about. It is itself split up into more than 40
petty municipal hatreds and disorganisations, and
when its aldermen can’t even see the advantages
of a united Sydney, it is no wonder that the class
from which they are drawn doesn’t rise to the
much bigger idea of a united Australia.

“ Melb.” writes :
The Protestant decrepitude in the inner square mile of
this city is evinced by the revelations about Dr. Bevan’s
church. This, with Dr. Marshall’s and Pastor Carey’s,
forms the trio of fashionable Collins-street churches, all
more or less in the soup. The Anglican cathedral com-
pletes a quartette, all in utterly miserable dependence
on the Sabbath morning trains to fetch them some ghost
or semblance of a congregation. Outsiders did not com-
prehend the true inwardness of Bent’s declaration, a
couple of years ago, when the Sabbatarians worried him,
that he would stop all the Sunday trains. That would
knock away the last props from the above four churches.
Some suburban preachers would be pleased, but the bulk of
the Sabbatarians were staggered at the idea of being taken
at theirword. The prospect of fourgreat churches closing
from complete lack of business broke them right up.
On a recent Sunday morning, Bevan’s church only held
80 people, whereas it seats 1200. It is, perhaps, the
most superb of all the Commonwealth conventicles in its
interior painting- and gilding, and it makes a brave show
outside with its campanile. Contrast this dreariness
with the Homan Catholic prosperity in Melbourne City.
My house isabout in the centre of the quadrilateral formed
by the churches of St. Patrick, St. Francis, St.
Mary and the Sacred Heart. I can reach any one of
them in ten minutes. The average Sunday'morning
attendance in St. Patrick’s is 2000 ; St. Francis’, 1000 ;
St. Mary’s, 1000; and the Sacred Heart, 700.
If Cardinal Moran preached at St. Patrick’s Mass next
Sunday, you might expect 4000, and, with a bigger
attraction, the congregation would rise to 6000. Moderate
people must sympathise with Dr. Bevan. His congrega-
tion wants a Judkins, or something like that—a Punch
and Juddy show. Bevan isn’t actual enough, with mere
Scripture exposition. It is a case of giving Brough and
Boucicault when the clamor is for William Anderson and
Bland Holt. The scholarly Lambley has just had to give
up the Unitarian fight after a decade of it, which is
another case in point.
« • * *
Melbourne seems to be producing a lot of
alleged charitable institutions that bestow a very
little charity for a whole lot of payment. The
other day nasty remarks were made about the-
Macedon Home for Consumptives ; and now it is;
discovered that the Talbot Colony for Epileptics,,
which has 50 beds and several hundred applicants
to start with, is admitting only those who can pay
a weekly sum, while “provision is being made in
the shape of more special accommodation for
applicants who are prepared to pay full fees.”
These full fees are understood to amount to £3 3s.
a week. It would be worth knowing whether the
generous donor of the 160 acres on which the
colony lives, and the smaller, but perhaps equally
generous, subscribers of guineas and half-guineas,
intended their benefactions for those who can
afford three guineas a week, to the exclusion of
sufferers who can scarcely raise as many shillings.

ENIGMA.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
You are so sweet.
You look at me with your wide, limpid eyes,
And my eyes search in them—as in far skies.
As in far skies my soul can never reach ;
I read in them an untranslated speech.
Sphinx-like, you smile away what my eyes ask,
Until I question, is it all a mask ?
Are you so sweet ?
You are so true.
Your feet stray not in byways damp with moss,
No shadows fling their waverings across
The high, white throning of your maiden state—
A thing serene, unmoved, inviolate.
Yet —sometimes when a reckless word I speak,
Old mem’ries seem to redden on your cheek —
Are you so true ?
QUEENSLAND.
M. FORREST.

“ Falder It is true that the in uch-adver-
tised gohanua oil is good and penetrating (it eats
leather away), but few people know how to get it
properly. This is how : In the autumn (he is
nice and plump then) shoot your “ go,” preferably
the biggest one you can find. Turn him on liis
back, cut him open and pull out the fat, which
you will find clinging loosely to the intestines,
and looking like a bunch of coarse yellow grapes.
Put this into a sound tin or very thick glass
bottle, and put it in the sun—not on a fire on any
account, as that takes all the strength out of it.
As it melts, dilute it with half its size of turpen-
tine, and put the result in a thick jar or bottle,
and stand it in a cool place till wanted. When
rubbing it in for rheumatism, use a glove or rag,
as the “go” oil has a tendency to penetrate into
the finger bones and make them brittle.
“G-.5.K.”: The country along the stock
route from Pack saddle to Milparinka (N.8.W.)
is full of traces of early Australian explorers. At
Mount Poole, near Milparinka, there are a lot of
places mentioned in Australian annals. The
Mount Poole homestead overlooks Sturt’s Water-
hole (Rocky Glen Dep6t), which is merely a soak
in Evelyn Creek. On the banks of this creek
stands the pyramidal headstone over Surveyor
Poole’s grave. This grave is sheltered by a
tree, on the trunk of which remains the
inscription—“J.P.lB4s”—which was cut by
Poole’s comrades. Mr. Lang, owner of Mount
Poole station, had the existing headstone placed
over the grave. Within a few yards of Poole’s
resting-place, an indentation in the ground marks
the spot where Sturt had his underground room.
About half a mile further away is Rocky Glen.
This glen is particularly beautiful, the precipi-
tous rocks that form the banks of the creek just
there being of most fantastic formation. On the
summit of Mount Poole there remains a large
rocky cairn, several feet square at the base, and
rising to a point. This cairn was built by Sturt’s
men, probably to pass away the time. At the
homestead there is an interesting relic —the side
of one of the boats which the explorers dragged
with them, being under the impression that there
were large water tracts or even an inland sea to
be crossed. This relic now forms part of the
front verandah of the homestead.

“ Janor ” writes (this controversy stops
somewhere hereabouts)
Re “Jeff” and the jackass that laughs (B. 27/6/’O7).
The man who obscures the landscape with his chest and
says loudly “ once and for all ” in a Johnsonian voice, is
mostly an advocate, not a witness. Years ago I held the
same opinion as “Jeff ” regarding the kookaburran menu,
but the smiler’s many thefts of fish which I had thrown
carelessly on the river bank while fishing gave me doubts
on the point, for those fish were as dead as they ever
would be. Also they are still dead. Later, I saw scores
of jackasses lying dead on the Morass station (V.) through
feeding on carcases of kangaroo poisoned for the dis-
couragement of dingoes. Not long ago, being plagued
by crows, I made an attempt to poison them by
placing junks of medicated wallaby on the garden
fence, The crow wasn’t taking any, but the jackass
was, and finding two of him stiff in the garden I re-
moved the baits. Therefore lam quite sure that “ Jack ”
doesn’t always kill his own tucker. As to the bird carry-
ing-snakes aloft, as “Jeff” relates, it is strange that
everyone who spins that yarn gives the show away just
as “ Jeff” does by emphasising the extraordinary num-
ber of times the bird drops the reptile. How many times
must a snake fall before it is quite undone? Let “ Jeff ”
put one in his pocket, climb a 100 ft. tree and drop it (not
the tree), and he’ll find that a broom would be re-
quired to gather up the fragments. Once saw a
hunted snake fall down a shaft 110 ft. deep, and descending
shortly afterwards found but a smudge on the shaft-
bottom. The lifting power of birds is but a matter of
wing-area, and in that respect “ Jack ” is a good few
feathers behind the hawk or eagle. Asa matter of fact,
his rising flight is slow and labored. Have filled half-
tame laughers so full of frogs that they couldn’t rise at
all. Therefore, if they ever do lift snakes they must be
dashed small ones. Observation impresses me with the
belief that the jackass has as true an appreciation of the
snake as the human has, and does not laugh too close to
the long, footless sneak. Even then he falls in at times,
for on two occasions I've found full-grown kookaburras
inside of carpet snakes. Come to think of it, there’s a
demnition lot of difference between a snake’s lunch and a
snake-laden airship.

“Jimmie Pannikin”: “G.S.K.” shows
more accuracy in discussing the Afghans’ .adher-
ence to their religious rites than he does when he
discourses about the gates West-o’-Darlinc.
Abdid Sooghee Bhag, Ram Jam Tin and Co. are
whales for the careful
observance of reli-
gious rites. Over
and over again, I
tried to induce
Afghans to be
photographed
with their
camels; but
they almost
invariably
declined to
be snapped,
saying that
their reli-
gion forbade
such a pro-
ceeding.
They did not
mind how often
their camels were
photographed. They
would even caparison the king
camel most gorgeously in order to have
his picture “took” ; but they would not be “in
the picture.” One cute amateur artist at Billv-
cannia successfully snapped some Afghans at
dinner, and again during loading operations. He
could not resist the temptation to show these
photographs to the Asiatics, and thereupon he had
to sprint back to the township to save his hide.
He was never allowed to visit the camel camp
afterwards.

“Yarrum”: Talk about horse-sense! I
once owned a moke ending in a bob-tail, and with
it he could open anything. He used to back him-
self up to the gate, slip the brief tail in where he
couldn’t get his nose, and up would go the latch.
Then I had to go after him. If the latch were
low down, he would sit down to get at it.
Once I put a heavy iron hoop over a gate, high up
where I thought lie couldn’t get his bit of tail.
He got away, arid when I came upon him, feed-
ing peacefully with his behind turned my way, 1
failed at first to recognise him. He had the hoop
jammed between his joint of tail and his rump.
His tail was, under certain circumstances, the
hardest to lift ever I struck. No boy could raise
it to put the crupper on, but a girl-infant could
hoist that tail to take the crupper off. I got rid
of him to a Chinaman.

Inventory taken by a Koroit (Vic.) fanner
of the contents of the stomach of a young cow :
Four pounds of quartz, a quantity of large bones,
numerous pieces of glass of different hades, a number of
fclie brass parts of cartridge-cases, nails, fencing stapies,
brass, lead, sandstone, seashells, clothes buttons, and
plated sleeve-links.
After this, goats, emus, city aldermen, and such-
like trash should retire to a back seat when the
eating prize is being awarded.

THE “ ANCHOR ” STROKE.
Years back, when the railway was being
pushed on northwards from Armidale (N.S.W.),
the navvies, as usual, boasted some “scrappers.”
One in particular made himself so famous as a
mixed slogger and drinker that when he died Ids
mates erected a tablet over his grave, using what
had at one time been a publican’s signboard, i t
was daubed over with whitewash, and bore the
simple legend—‘ 1 Sacred to the Memory of John
Smith.” But the bleak winds of the north, and
the winter rains of many years have swept off the
whitewash, and anyone passing Dundee Cemetery
may now make out a weatherworn notification at
a grave head : “ Licensed to sell fermented and
spirituous liquors.”
“ Cross Nibs ” tells a tale of fraud :
“ Lor’blime, ’e is a bonza, there’s no kid.” I was
addressed by the gentleman sitting; alongside of me. He
carried fur upon his coat and a brace of moribund rabbits
in his band. We occupied the rear seat of a tram, and
our tickets permitted a view of the terminus. The fore-
going- remark concerned the tram-conductor. “Y’r
wouldn’t think us two wuz comrades in misforchin wonce,
would ’yer? Well, we wuz. That’s Skaler —’n’ the
toughest bib ov ’ide I’ve travelled with. I ’umped th’
bundle with ’im down th’ Darlin’, ’n’ there wuz no cliarnce
of a perish when ’e wuz with y’r. ’E uster carry three
sets o’ bags f’r tucker, ’n’ always ’ad ’em occupied.
Carryin’ too many glad character bills, faked by ’imself,
nearly eroded ’im wonce. ’E passed one to a jumbuck
grower, ’ummin’ ’im up as a special food boiler, ’n’ scored
a job as wool ’awk’s cook. A bag o’ flour ’n’ a stack ov
vegetables got mucked up, ’n’ a big ginger cook put it all
over ’im ’aid ’n’ ’eavy. Skaler passed ’im th’ lid ov a
camp oven, ’n’ Ginger slumbered as ’e fell.
“After that me ’n’ ’im 'ad a contract ringbarkin’ten
acres ov timber. After a week’s chip scatterin’, ’e went
t’ tli’ boss ’n’ chinned ’im f’r a draw. When ’e came out t'
see our graft, ’e run ’is measurin’ chain over it ’n’ threw
us a couple ov dollars. Next day, me ’n’ Skaler went up
t’ tli’ ’omestead t’ give our axes a feed ov grindstone. We
got back t’ th’ camp at dark, ’n’ in Skater’s pocket wuz
th’old pot’s measurin’ chain. After tea,’e examined ifc
’n’ said it wanted alterin’ t’ suit th’ workin’man. ’E then
nipped off ten feet V fixed t’ ring oil again like a trades-
man. When we settled up with th’ old bloke we gw*
good silver f’r bark we didn’t disturb. As we wue leaviiM’’
’e told us t’call in again about June, as ’e’d'ave frstms
scrub-clearin’ t’ do. Skaler informed ’iin ’e ’ad a little
job ov that sort to attend to at wonce.
“ Game across a bullocky down th' rivers nrarrth after,,
’n’ ’e told us th’ old bloke ’ad been inquirin' about us..
Skaler give ’im th’ leary gaze, ’n’ remarked that ring
barkin’ didn't agree with ’im—made ’im giddy walkin
round trees. Et Ewebulla we called in f’r tucker. V wuz:
shown th’ wood’eap V a seven-pound axe. Skater wired
me t’ keep th’ cook listenin’ t’ a taLe or woe, V then
dodged round th’ back ov th’ kitchen. The bricks came
out ov th’ hack ov th’ oven, ’n’ with ’em a batch of
brownie’n’bread. Skaler reckoned a piece of ’bop-iron
wuz lighter than ’n’ axe b’ ’.uidle when graftin' f’r a crust..
Yes, ’e is dead up t’ date. Y’r see, Vs on a good wicket,
now foot-boardin’ these jiggers- Why, spare me days,
’ere's me street. S’long ! See y’r again some day !!’”
“ -Tanor ” : A bush scene on which I hap-
pened recently would have made a. subject for a
•picture. Said the selector to me: “-You’re just
in time ; come and see if I’ve caught any fish. 1 *
As we scrambled through the scrub, he explained
that one of his calves had died the previous day,
and he had loaded it up with strychnine,, in
hopes of netting a dingo. We emerged suddenly
oil a small, open space, green-carpeted, sunlit,
and embayed in the swirling waters of an elbow
of the creek. It was walled in by a closely-woven
screen of foliage 100 ft. high. In the centre of the
opening lay the torn and mangled calf, and
grouped round it four dingoes and five eagles
stretched stark and lifeless in the shade-barred
sunlight. On the edge of the water a sixth eagle,
with great beak gaping and dulled eyes, sup-
ported himself on widespread grounded wings,
making vicious struggles to rise, and battling with
the death near by. The man on the land chortled
with joy. “ What do you think of that, old
man ?” he whooped. “ Damn it! Why can’t we
all be artists ? ” replied I, with a great regret.
THE CHRISTENIN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
On the plains of Tooraneedin, where the rabbits keep on
breedin’,
Where the sheep break through the fences, and the cows
are fierce and wild,
In a farmhouse near the “ river ” (which was flowing
hardly ever),
In the family of Maloney had arrived the seventh child.
There was Pathrick and Honora(’twas her mother’s name
before her),
There was Bridget, Kate, and Edward, and that Murty
rogue as well;
And with Number Seven squealing, old Maloney said,
with feeling—
“ Begobs, ’tis I’m the man desarves to ind his days in
- h 1.
"For ’tis sinful an’ surprisin’ that there’sdivil a baptisin’
Has anj 7 av thim childher iver had from anj 7 praste ;
Faith, if Father Dooley knew ut (him that married me to
rue ut),
Av purgatorial pinance I wud get a fearful taste.
“ Now, I hear that at the station there’s a praste av
reputation—
A jolly bhoy who’d christen thim an’ niver turn a hair ;
Oi’ll sind for him to-morrow ; for ’tis shame to me an’
sorrow
That I’ve niver sought His Riverince all th’ toime that
lie’s been there.”
So Maloney in the morning at the station gave his
warning,
And Father M’lnulty heard his story with a grin ;
“ You muster up the siven and I’ll help them all to Hiven
This evenin’ after sunsit, just as fast as they’ll come in.”
That night the nine Maloneys, with their neighbors and
their cronies,
Sat waiting for the Father, who arrived a little late ;
There were lights and water handy (and maybe a drop of
brandy)
When Fatlier M'lnulty’s trap pulled up beside the gate.
Their thirst they had abated in the hour that they had
waited,
Fora christenin’ needs the comfort of a drop of good
potheen ;
And Maloney’s nose was reddened, and his eyes a trifle
deadened,
But ’twas dark within the parlor, and it couldn’t well be
seen.
Inside Maloney’s kitchen all the kids with fun were
twitchin’,
For to be baptised was something they had never known
before ;
And a list of names was handed to the Father, who com-
manded
The evening’s operations like a general at the war.
They put the blessed candle in a bottle—’twas a scandal,
But no other thing to hold it in the farmhouse could lie
found ;
knd from Murty up to Paddy all were dragged in by then-
daddy—
The Holy Church received them ; and the baby’s turn
came round.
Then behold, there was a scuffling, and a grunting, and a
snuffling, *
And a sudden gust of wind came in and put the candle
out.
“Where the divil is that baby?” “ Och, he’s on the
flagstones, maybe ! ”
And Maloney picked up something. “ Call him James ! ”
they heard him shout.
And the Latin words went racing, while Maloney stood
embracing
The baby ; but he muttered, “By me sowl, he's pretty
big 1 ”
Till Bridget struck a light, and screeched out loud, in
accents frightened,
“ God save us, holy Father ! Ye’re baptisin’ Murty’s pig !”
And the pig it kicked and wriggled, while the spalpeen
Murty giggled
Till Maloney bent above him, and he gripped him bv the
ear.
But says Father Mac.: “Don’t bate him ; faith, ’tis not
yourself should hate him,
For the pig’s as good a Christian as you’ve been this
fourteen year ! ”
Now up at Tooraneedin there’s a pig of Berkshire breedin
Whom people come to gaze at, from districts far and wide ;
They point at him, declaring you can gather from his
bearing
He’s the one and only Christian pig in all the countryside.

• * * *
“ Hamer ” : On that Friday night when
the news of the defeat of Squires had cast a
minor-poet's gloom over Sydney, I met a man
with a broken heart. He was leaning against a
verandah post, moaning softly ; and, thinking lie
was ill, I asked him what the trouble was. He
plaintively begged me to give him a drink.
Hoping to save his life, I carried him into an
adjacent bar. He clutched his liquor firmly, and,
in the voice of one prepared to hear the worst,
asked who had won the tight. I told him. He
swept a glass off the bar, and announced that it
was a lie. Also, he called me a “ stinking fish,”
and said “ Pah ! ” as if he smelt one. He added that
he had met Squires in Pitt-street, and Squires had
assured him that his defeat was an impure fabri-
cation. Then suddenly his fortitude deserted
him, and he leaned his head on my bosom and
wept aloud. I hurriedly suggested another drink,
and he raised bis head and wept into his beer
instead of on to my waistcoat. Then he inquired,
with sobs, what bis mother —presumably Squires’s
mother—would say, and remarked that a mother
was the saving grace of a man’s life. He men-
tioned, as a curious fact, that no man had two
mothers, and said it was a pity, and the world
would be much purer if there were more mothers.
Then, his beer being finished, be again leaned his
head on my bosom. I removed my bosom
hurriedly, and put him in a tram, and the last I
saw of him he was leaning his head on the con-
ductor’s shoulder, and asking who won the fight.

“Cic”: In Tumbarumba (N.S.W.) dis-
trict, among the tunnels and sluicing claims, is a
small spring, the waters of which are so heavily
impregnated with minerals that a piece of wood
deposited in same will, in the course of a few
months —the time required, of course, varying in
proportion to the cubical contents of the hunk of
fuel —become thoroughly petrified. I saw a stone
t’other day in the possession of one of the local
tunnellers, and used by him to grind his razor on.
The grain of the wood was plainly visible. The
miners simply square and plane a piece of tree to
the desired length and thickness, and having
securely tethered it in the spring, go on hunting
gold. Providing the other fellow up the creek
hasn’t commandeered it, the rightful owner, in
a season or two, is possessed of a whet stone that
will force an edge on anything, barring perhaps a
maul.

“ The Prooshan ” : The subject was floods
and alleged coaching experiences. Billy chip-
ped in: “ Tork about floods, yorter bin with
me on Urangeline Crick, on the IT ran a run, in the
early ’nineties. Flood ! Be crimes, there wuz a
bloomin’ sea er water fer miles, ’n’ there’s the
’orses up ’n’ down, floppin’, ’n’ reefin’, ’n’ corfin’,
’n’ gruntin’ like the dooce, ’n’ th’ old coach
threatenin’ to go outer bloomin’ sight any minit.
An’ there’s me, carnt see tli’ ’orses arf me time,
’n’ got to flog at where I sees the bubbles, that’s
all. Larsfc I spots a swagman scrooged up on a
bit of a knob, and I lets him and his swag in.
’Bout a mile on we comes to shallerer country,
and I notices the old ship wogglin a bit, ’n’ gits
the swaggie to git out V see what’s up, and
blode if one ’ind wheel ain’t gone! So we
gets a saplin’ and fixes it on fer a drag. ‘We’ll
part ’ere, mate,’ says I. ‘ Blode if I can lug you
and yer bun with that dead wheel.’ And the
swagman sez nothin’ but heaves his swag out,
end-on like, and tork about boggy ! Well, that
there swag bogged that tight the two of us could
har’ly shift it. And when we gits it, the bloomin’
thing comes away with a reg’lar ‘ sock ’ —like a
orse drorin’ his foot out of a bog.”
“Ouss” ! “F.R.” (B. 27/6/’O7) puts in a
plea with bushmen on behalf of the white goshawk,
and so does the writer—a strong plea, loaded with
a charge of No. 6 shot. A pair of these lovely
critters lately took up their residence in a tree
about a mile away from my bush emporium, and
every alternate day for some six weeks they dined
sumptuously on young buff Orpington chicks that
had been hatched under the personal supervision
of this pilgrim and his mate. On off-days these
gentle and harmless birds used to casually drop
in on a neighbor, who lives some two miles down
the creek, and sample the ordinary variety of
chicken stocked there. The sum total of then-
depredations has put two “on the land” en-
thusiasts out of business, and turned one of the
pair into a skilled criminal, as he now “raises”
what fowls he requires by means of an empty
chaff bag. As I write, the skin of the goshawk
indicted hangs on the front verandah of the
shanty, well cured with arsenical soap. 1 want
to see the bird preserved with the above-named
soap. As a general nuisance to bush dwellers he
runs the dingo mighty close on the N.S.W. North
Coast.
* • * *
“The Wearier”: Re. “ Quan ” and his
camel lore (B. 20/6/ 07). I have had something to
do with the walking and humpy bone-mill, and
while I quite agree with the statement that
horses work wide from the camel, I do not believe
that they forget the ugly beast in a short time.
They just couldn’t. The smell of ages is round
the camel, and to disremember it implies a
capacity for forgetting that beats creation. As for
cattle, they don’t charge the beast that hums,
nor do they retreat in a violent hurry when
the guano-ship of the desert gets to windward of
them. Cattle treat the camel with utter con-
tempt, and assume that it is dead. I have seen a
mob of real scrubbers in one yard and a bull-
camel on season in the next, and he hummed, too.
Also, he bubbled, howled, and roared, so that five
horses tied to a rail fully half-a-mile off pulled
away and departed thence with great haste and
sundry saddles and swags. But the micks in the
adjoining yard simply ignored the startling per-
formance. They had front seats and an uninter-
rupted smell of the drama, but they weren’t in-
terested worth a peanut.

A bush idyll from “ Mouse ” :
It was the usual concertina-type dance of the Cow
regions. I stood for a minute or two seeking a “ nimble
heifer,” and finally, As the dance ended, I sidled over to my
choice and asked her for the honor of the next. She stared
at me a moment, and then giggled until her girl friend
poked her severely in the lower regions of her blouse and
informed her, in a thiiJling whisper, ‘ ’E wants a darnce.”
Then she straightened up, and told me I’d “ have to ask
her bloke.” Upon my presenting my petition, he replied,
“ Well, yairs ; s’poseyeroan have one,." I had one accord-
ingly, and was about half through, when the lady was
suddenly twisted out of my arms by her ardent suitor,
with “ Let ’er rip, blokey ; ’er old man’s waitin’ ter start
milkin’,”—and behold she was gone! And her parting
utterance to her consort still blooms in my memory:
“ Lawks ! Joe ; yer ought ter feel ’is ’ands. Lorng, thin
fingers; just the sort ter make ther juice fly outer ole
Yallerteats at ’ome.”
“ Walfre ” : “My word, this Glory Halle-
lujah brigade has got a hide,” said a bandsman of
my acquaintance in a tone of disgust. “ You
know we have a job playing for an open-air show
every night, and part of our work consists of a
march along the main street. The Starvation
Army comes into the street to hold its services,
and our band has to pass it on the way to the
show. We marched last night, and this morning
the Salvarmy boss came to my shop and asked
for the bandmaster. I saw him, and he said, very
ungraciously, ‘ Don’t you think you show a great
lack of consideration?’ I said, ‘How?’ He
said, ‘The band ought to cease playing while
they pass our meeting in the street.’ I asked him
did he own the street, and be became very wroth
and said, ‘ You are only a common low heathen,
and an ignorant person.’ I fired up a bit and told
him he was only a common low loafer, and ought
to be made to work for his living. Also I asked
him why he didn't stop the trams running past
his blessed gathering. Likewise I said he ought
to stop making hideous rows in the streets at all
hours, irritating the ears of musically inclined
people who had to pass within range of his meet-
ing. He didn’t like it, and cleared. Oh, yes !
we'll knock off playing for that leather-lunged
cadger when we pass him. We’re more likelv to
stop marching and play all wc know just under
his nose.” And, ruminating on the “ossifer’s”
demand, and wondering who will win the
Ballarat contest, the bandsman went on his way.

CULTURE IN THE SLUMS,
MOTHER: “ You, Muriel Agnes, come in this minute and practise your agates."
“Barb”: Often wonder what effect a
heavy fall of snow would have on Sydneyites, if
they one day woke up to And their city white.
Would perfect strangers go about shaking hands
with each other, and the whole city become
friendly ? Here, where we have snow two or
three times in the winter, a “fall ” has a most
delightful effect. People who haven’t spoken to
you for months come down the road shouting
excitedly at you. The station men —some of
them deadly enemies—crowd round the blazing
log fire in the kitchen, and become cpiite friendly.
The Chinese cook, who would, in ordinary
weather, throw a saucepan at the head of any
man entering his kitchen, positively welcomes
them. Magpies crowd round the door. Little
blue wrens and red-breasts hop into the room in
the most engaging manner. Crows pick at the
skinned rabbit hanging from the verandah beams.
Pee-wits hunt in the snow-clad garden. Young-
sters, usually most respectful, think nothing of
smacking you in the eye with a stinging snowball.
Everyone is sloppy and uncomfortable and in
high good humor.
* ■» * *
“ Herrues ” : After some six years of city
life, I was suddenly stricken with an attack of
b'oodthirstiness, and went forth on a dapple grey
nag, armed with a sporting Snider, to slay wild
bulls on Cairn Station in Otago, Maoriland. There
were four men and a boy in the party, which left
the homestead while the stars were still sinning.
After a most infernal ride of about 12 miles the
cattle were sighted breakfasting on a bluff, at the
bottom of which grew a few gnarled, wind-smitten
trees. The boss sportsman devised the plan of
campaign, and ordered one man and the boy to
make a detour to the tup of the bluff, and by
opening fire stampede the cattle towards where
we would be waiting for them. It was a noble
idea. When the stampeders arrived at the
top of the bluff they opened tire, but the
bulls took no notice. The boy, who was proud
of his surefootedness, offered to climb down
and attack them at close quarters. He did so,
and about an hour later the man returned alone,
and reported that the bulls had the boy treed,
and that he had signalled he was short of food
and water. We decided to relieve the boy by
approaching in loose order, half-moon formation,
and the boss gave vigorous instructions, which he
hoped would prevent any of us shooting men
instead of bulls. Arriving within range, we found
the bulls were all cows. Smothering our liner
feelings, we opened fire, but our fingers were cold
and our nerves upset by the long, rough ride, and
consequently our practice was horrible. Half-a-*
dozen volleys produced no effect, except to scare
the life out of the boy in the tree, who was nearly
shot on several occasions. The cows ran back and
forwards, and finally took shelter among the
timber. Suddenly someone plunked a bullet into
a dun-colored brute. With a snort and a bellow
she flung up her head, and then, elevating her
heels, came right at us. What a scatter there
was ! Not one man stood his ground. The
noise brought out another couple of lady
bulls, and they started after the leader of our
expedition. We all fled up trees, and then, with
awful language, opened fire in every direction.
Seven cows were shot in as m'Hrv minutes, and an
equal number sent scamperivp ff badly wounded.
Then we came down arm congratulated one
another. While we were dining, an impudent
caitiff rode up, and, with curses, demanded what
the devil we meant by shooting his cattle. He
chased us seven miles, and then we dropped him.
Another hour's hard riding brought us to the
boundary fence, and we knew then that we had
strayed right off the Cairn territory on to that of
Kuriwao cattle station.

“ Drifter It is dangerous to chaff a man
whose chest is full of beer and sentiment when
the emotions of his soul well up into sad maudlin
song. At such a time you may steal his
beer, call him bad names, or kick his dog
and he will forgive you ; but scoff at his
harmony, and you are a remains. We were in the
side dive of a suburban pub one night. Two
rough-looking customers, who had reached the
stage when all.this sad world is a rose-tinted para-
dise, were hanging on to each other in the bar-
solemuly singing, in thick husk} 7 tones, “A
Violet What I. Plucked from Mother’s Grive.”
They were oblivious of all the sordid surround-
ings, and clung manfully to their pewter pots
whilst battling to keep their perpendicular.
Great salt tears rolled down their grimy cheeks,
and splashed heavily into the foaming beer. It
was a picture to melt a heart of stone. Suddenly,
during a particularly pathetic stanza, a fool friend
of mine threw back his head and emitted a long,
mournful dog-howl. Like a flash the singers
turned towards the sound. We saw the glint of
murder in their eyes, and skipped nimbly. Three
seconds later the infuriated vocalists surged round
into the passage. They saw no one, so they
started to tear up the premises. There was a
savage rush, a thud, and for the next two minutes
it seemed as if hell had broken loose and was
kicking the world to death along the pub floor.
I believe the remains of the building fell down
next day. No ! never poke borak at the drunk
who weeps and sings about poor ole mother. It
might cost you your life.

“ Gippsland East ” : A few rabbit notes :
Have shot seventeen rabbits in my green-pea
patch during the last three weeks, and found each
one tube a buck. In the bush I have killed many
more, and examination showed that fully 90 per
cent, were males, the exceptions, generally, being
those caught by dogs. These facts have led me
to the conclusion that the buck rabbit is braver,
fleeter, and more venturesome than the doe. In
this district, if a shooter surprise a dozen rabbits
in a fairly open spot, he will see nine or ten make
for cover and immediately disappear. Two or three,
having first stopped to “knock,” will make for cover,
but before reaching it stop to knock again. These
pauses give opportunity for effective shooting, and
in each case where I have been successful in potting
these guardians I have found them to be bucks.
The bucks, too, are generally first out of the
burrows, and last into them, which accounts for
the high tallies of bucks taken by trappers at the
evening catch at the mouths of burrows, and of
does in the morning. Old rabbit-dogs putting up
rabbits in the open will invariably chase the doe,
and they are always keener on a she-seent, even
though the balance of rabbitness is distinctly on
the side of bucks —so far as the human nose can
tell. Another item is that although, a few years
ago, trappers took dozens of colored rabbits about
here, to-day none but greys are caught. They
have grown as uniform in color as the bandicoots,
and, if more numerous than they were, are
rapidly decreasing in adult size. A four pound
rabbit is now an exception, where a few years ago
it was quite common.

THE UNIVERSAL DROUGHT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ If all the world were apple-pie
And all the sea were ink,
And all the trees
Were bread and cheese,
What should we have to drink?"
This nursery rhyme of olden time
Gives cause to pause to think
What liquid can
A cautious man
In safety find to drink ?
There’s poison in the whisky flask t
There’s fusel in the beer I
The water tract
At Cataract
Ts curiously queer 1
It’s full of infusoria
(Whatever that may be),
And divers germs
Expressed in terms
Far worse than Greek to me !
The morning milk’s analysis
Would make a person shrink 1
The tea we get,
Though warm and wet,
Is fitter for the sink 1
The coffee's choked with chicory 1
In fact we’re on the brink
Of death from thirst;
For all’s accurst !
There’s nothing tit to drink !
N.S.W,
W. T. GOODGE.

PERSONAL
ITEMS
The War Cry of July 20 publishes a
photo block of old General Booth in Japan,
seated in the centre of a score of dainty Geishas.
The old man, with his long white beard and bell-
topper, looks, in the centre of the small brown
damsels, for all the world like a character in some
musical comedy.
Edward George Wilton, just dead at 82,
in addition to being the most versatile of N.S.W.
country pressmen, deserves to be remembered as
the man who supplied the grammar of the old
Empire. Parkes’s own grammar was like the
winds of heaven, and blew where it listed ; and
his relative didn’t know its own antecedent when
the two met in the street. Henry the Great
always professed to regard Wilton’s repairs as
personal insults, but he allowed them to pass.
He assumed, however, that he did so only out of
a desire to amuse an inferior mind.
THE LATE FRANK MYERS.
Sydney’s new City Coroner, John Leth-
bridge King, is a grandson of N. S. Wales’ third
Governor, Rear-Admiral Philip Parker King.
The Admiral’s wife had been a Miss Lethbridge;
hence that part of the monniker of the man who
will now have the messy job of sitting on the
city’s remains. A. N. Barnett, his predecessor,
is promoted to the job of fining the city’s drunks.
Barnett is having a look-in at all the offices. He
began with the Lands, then went to the Mines,
and later on followed “Rolf Boldrewood” as
Police Magistrate at Albury.
Pedlar Palmer, the English pugilistic has-
been, got a well-deserved five years for killing
with a single blow a drunken train passenger who
persisted in singing when Pedlar told him to stop.
This rotter was for some years the bantam
champion of the world, and made over £50,000 out
of bruising. He spent it in characteristic style,
and when “ Terrible Terry” McGovern ended
his career in one whirling round he hadn't the
proverbial bean left, and his tandem, which was
one of the sights of London streets, went up the
spout. Now Pedlar himself is in the jug.
Journalist Edward George Wilton, who
died at Ryde (N.S.W.) recently, aged 83, was a
son of the late Frederick Charles Wilton, who
was for 30 years stage manager of the Britannia
Theatre, Hoxton, London, locally known as
“the Britanniar’oxton.” Edward founded the
Bathurst Times, Lachlan Observer, and Hill
End Observer, was an old-time manager of the
Wagga Advertiser and Newcastle Herald, a
S.M. Herald reporter, and an Empire man
during Henry Parkes’s proprietorship. He was
an enthusiatic musician, and wrote a short opera
and a couple of plays.
What is suspected of being a genuine cen-
tenarian has turned up in Victoria. A Mount
Noorah man recently took a ’cello, from which he
has been sawing disturbance for a number of years
past, to pieces to see where the noise came from,
and found in it a label bearing the name Nicolo
Amanti, and the date 1624. Further exploration
in the cavernous interior of the monster revealed
an inscription alleging that it had been repaired
by Louis Dandeh, of Versailles, for King Louis
XVI., in 1781. Apparently, like Nero, Louis
’celloed while his kingdom did most horribly
eOmbust.
Andrew Lang, who is quite the most
sporting literary man living, has just discovered
a 15th century treatise in Latin (on the Perfect
Parson) by a German calling himself Albertus
Trottus. Prom which he concludes that Albert
Trott, of Melbourne and Middlesex, is of German
extraction, and ought to be styled “Albrecht.”
“Albert” is a German name, anyway, and was
hardly known in England till the youthful Vic-
toria married a princelet so called, just about the
time the other Victoria first saw Batman on the
Yarra banks.
Mr. Augustus Brown Abraham, barrister-
at-law, whose death was announced recently in
England at the age of 95 years, was the oldest
member of the British Bar. He was born on
August 7, 1812, was called to the Bar at the
Middle Temple (London) in January, 1835, was
admitted to the Victorian Bar in 1853, and was a
leader in Equity until 1862, when he returned to
England. And his name was Brown Abraham,
which was almost as remarkable as if he had been
called Smith Moses.
Died, a few days ago, Mrs. Goddard, an
old resident of Waterfall, near Sydney, the
mother of one-time prominent pugilists Joe and
Herb Goddard, each of whom predeceased her.
It is doubtful if Joe had an equal in modern
pugilism as a natural fighter. He first shaped
(billed as “ The Illawarra line navvy”) in Sydney,
when someone undertook to knock him out in
four rounds and did it. Then Joe opened a small
store, and supplied the navvies engaged in build-
ing the Illawarra railway line. He laid the
foundation of his subsequent fame at Broken Hill,
by defeating Owen Sullivan, Charlie Dunn, and
others, and followed this up with successes in
Sydney and Melbourne over Mick Dooley and
the American Joe Choynski. The younger God-
dard (much better-looking than Joe, and well
built) hadn’t the “devil” of his brother. He
failed more frequently than he succeeded, and
succumbed to consumption while yet a young
man.
John Watsford, the oldest Wesleyan
Methodist minister in Australia, who died at
Kew, Melbourne, on July 24, at the age of
87 years, was born at Parramatta (N.S.W.) on
December 5, 1820, during Governor Macquarie’s
time. Hewasoneofthefirsthalf-dozen day scholars
who went to the King’s School, Parramatta, in
1832. Watsford, senior, was a member of the
firm of Watsford and King, proprietors of the
Globe Tavern, S.E. corner of Market and Castle-
reagh streets, and owners of the line of coaches
which ran in those primitive days between
Sydney and Parramatta. John Watsford was for
two years one of the teachers at King’s School,
and became a Methodist minister in 1843. For
seven or eight years he was in Fiji; then for
three years in Sydney, three in Goulburn, two in
Maitland, six years in South Australia, and the
balance of his time in Melbourne. He thus sin-
shifted over a large area.
From the Oldest Inhabitant :
Mr. E. A. L. Sharpe, police magistrate at Maitland
(N.S.W.), who was born in 1843, retired on the 31st July.
He is a son of Canon Sharpe, for many years located in
Bathurst, and was one of the very few police magistrates
who could boast of being barristers-at-law. He was for
some years at Forbes, and was a terror to duck-shooters
out of season. Once a few of the “ pot shots ” succeeded
in getting a bag in the close season, and sent the duck to
old Levi Vandenberg, the innkeeper, of Court-street, to be
cooked and served up for lunch. Mr. Sharpe heard of the
event, and he also lunched at Vandenberg’s. A hurried
order was issued that the ducks should not be produced
till the legal embodiment was gone. Unfortunately, it
seemed as if he would never go. In fact he lunched more
or less for two hours. It was the record long-distance
lunch of Forbes. The duck men had a half-inclination to
go and feed in the kitchen, but feared lest the magistrate
might come too, so they stood their ground and hun-
gered for half-an-hour. The magistrate was still con-
sidering a chop. They waited another half-hour; the
magistrate was wrestling with a small bit of cheese, and
had been on it for 25 minutes. A smell of burned duck
pervaded the premises. Finally, the party fell
back on the ordinary bill of fare, and found that
everything was off save corned beef. Sharpe saw them
eat the corned beef, and they tried to look as if a cold
remnant of salt-horse was what they had come'for. Also,
he saw them carefully and unobtrusively off the premises,
and as he went out behind them, he stopped in the hall
to sniff inquiringly at the smell of burnt duck ; and
the way he did it made the spine of Levi Vandenberg run
cold. These parties reckoned that they knew then what
was meant by the eye of the law. If it wasn’t the genuine
article they had seen, they considered that- a magistrate’s
eye, looking for two hours at a crowd of illegal duck-
shooters, was a fair substitute.
A. B. POSTLE, CHAMPION
RUNNER.
Three times running the
leadership of the Queensland
Labor party has been followed by
a seat in the Ministry. When
the Morgan Ministry was formed
W. H. Browne, then leader of
the Labor party, got a portfolio.
Upon his death Peter Airey, his
successor as leader, became Home
Secretary. Now George Kerr,
who took Airey’s place as leader,
has become Minister for Rail-
ways. This may inspire Dave Bowman, the new
leader, with a hope of one day becoming Premier.
Kidston, by the way, was never Labor leader,
though he acted once during Browne’s illness.
Found dead near Springfield station, in
the Windorah district (Queensland), Henry Fane
Becher, founder of the Grip (Grafton, N.S.W.).
Like many another man of his craft he deserved
better fortune than came his way.

William Timmins, of the Hawkesbury
(N.S.W.), lately deceased at the mature age of
98, may be quoted as evidence against the theory
that the Australian is a restless, flitting and un-
settled cuss, a rolling-stone and a will-o’-the-wisp.
William died in sight of the spot on which he was
born, and all the travels of his long life didn’t
extend further than a dozen miles. He never
got the globe-trotting craze, or fled from Pekin to
Paris in a motor-car, like Prince Bearsgrease, of
Italy; and he neglected Antarctic expeditions and
reckless explorations in the interior of Africa. The
wild ass of Persi a remain ed und istu rbed as far as any
pursuit on William’s part was concerned, and he
steadfastly refused to clamber like a fly up the
sides of the Matterhorn, or Kinchinjunga, or
Popocatepetl, or Cotopaxi, or Imhabura, or even
Adam’s Peak. Whale-fishing never attracted
him much, and he hadn’t time to study the art
treasures of the Vatican. He just lived seriously
on the old Hawkesbury, and died there at the age
of 98.
Died at Sydney, the other day, David
Scott Mitchell, Australian book-lover. He was
born in March, 1836. His father was Dr. James
Mitchell, a member of the first N.S.W. Legisla-
tive Council appointed by the British Govern-
ment, and afterwards an M.L.C. under Respon-
sible Government. David Mitchell was one of
the first Sydney University graduates, winning
his B.A. in ’56. He was admitted as a barrister
in 1858, but never practised. He spent his early
life managing the estate inherited from his
father on the Hunter River (N.S.W.). Then he
took to collecting early Australasian art and
literature, and spent half a century and the most
of his fortune in the task. The result is what is
considered the most complete library of Austral-
asian records in existence. There are pictures by
Oscar Brierley, Conrad Martens, Prout, Lycett,
and Chevalier; the original diaries of explorers
Leichhardt, Oxley, Mitchell, Sturt, Govett,
Evans, Bass, and King ; and autograph letters of
Vancouver, Cook, Phillip, King, Hunter, Bligh,
and other early notabilities; valuable MSS.
bearing on the transition stage of the State from
the “System” to Responsible Government; maps,
charts, sketches, books and pamphlets. All this
wealth of historical material becomes, in pursu-
ance of a promise made by Mr. Mitchell in 1898,
the property of N.S.W., together with a money
legacy to keep it in order, and add to it. Mr.
Mitchell’s only condition was that he should
see some evidence that the Government would
house his gift decently before he died, and
Premier Carruthers set about the new building in
Sydney Domain just in time.
Thus the Oldest Inhabitant :
Dr. James Mitchell, father of David Scott Mitchell,
recently deceased, was originally surgeon to the 48th
Regiment, with which corps he saw service in many
lands. In the ’twenties he was attached to the civil
hospital in Macquarie-street. The chief medical officer
then was Dr. James Bowman, who married a daughter of
John Macarthur, and lived in the house on the corner of
King and Macquarie streets, afterwards occupied by
Canon Allwood as “St. Janies’Rectory.” Dr. Mitchell
was plaintiff in a libel action seventy years ago, the
defendant being Dr. John V. Thompson, newly arrived as
Deputy-Inspector-General of Hospitals. The new official
was a martinet, and it was not long before his unpopu-
larity was larger than a herd of elephants. A quarrel
with Mitchell ended in the dismissal of the latter by
Governor Bourke, one of the charges against the sacked
official being that he refused duty at the usual morning
floggings at Hyde Park Barracks. Thompson took the
opportunity of making extracts from the general orders,
which reflected upon Mi tchell, and sent the extracts to the
papers as items of news. The action came before Chief Jus-
tice Dowling : and a jury consisting of J. Brown, J. Barker,
H. H. Bowerman, J. Betts, J. Byrne, VV. Cordeaux, J.
Chalmers, G. Cox, W. Cox, H. Cox, E. Cox, and Dawes.
The Coxes were- all of one family. Attorney-General
Plunkett put in an appearance to ascertain his'position.
Two of the counts concerned libels by a Government
officer in his official capacity; the third was for publica-
tion in the newspapers. Plunkett, after carefully strain-
ing the gnat, decided to officially appear in the two first
charges, but not in the third. Eventually it was agreed
by counsel to proceed on the newspaper publication
alone, and Plunkett withdrew with great dignity from
the scene. Foster and Windeyer appeared for Mitchell;
Roger Therry and William A’Beckett for Thompson. The
father of the lately deceased book-lover came out of the
scrimmage with a verdict of £lOO and costs. If it hadn’t
been that it was a serious offence for an official to refuse
to attend the morning floggings, he might have got more.
One day’s trial will convince you of the
soothing, cough-allaying, lung-healing power of

aids digestion. Send 4d. postage for a free
sample bottle to the Angier Chemical Co., Ltd.,

ie burly, red-headed, curly-bearded
Bishop of Wangaratta (Vic.) has the reputation
of being the biggest parson and the hardest
hitter in the Anglican Church of Aus-
tralia. Also he holds out the hun-
gnest collection-plate in all the
Commonwealth. It does not
matter what his text is, money
is his invariable subject.
On a recent Sunday he
preached two thunderous
sermons at his Arch-
deacon’s church. His
coming was heralded by
a handbill exhort ng
the congregation to
“ Give liberally. Give
gold if possible. Give
silver if you cannot
give gold. But please
give some special
offering. Ask Goi
to stir up your hearts
and wills to give
freely.” This Bishop
has a salary of £BOO
a year plus a fine
mansion ; but the
greater number of
the congregation to
whom these begging
appeals are ad-
dressed get much
less than £lOO a year.
And some of them
reckon that the Boss
Shepherd is too
ravenous, and that
he should take to
plain brigandage.
The Christchurch
(M.L.) synagogue
has for its president a
most _ comprehensive
man. His name is Percy
Herman, and he is set
down as ticket-seller, aue-
tioneer. money-lender, hotel-keeper, racehorse-
owner, hotel-manager, speculator, etc., etc.
The S. Australian Government is re-
organising its Statistical office—which in recent
years has consisted chiefly of dusty back numbers
and cobwebs. This “ reorganisation ” really
means that a statistical clerk has been employed.
Said clerk is “Jack” Lyons, the once mighty
hitter of Australian Elevens, who has tired of the
weary struggle to accumulate a million on the
Stock Exchange.
Probably the chief breaker-up of the
happy home, and also the unhappy one, in Aus-
tralia these times is ex-champion boxer Laur-
ence Foley, of Sydney. Foley now does busi-
ness on a big scale. He buys the happy home
when it is too old to stand any longer, and de-
molishes the domestic hearth, and sells the
materials. Lately he has been wrecking the one-
time athletic club of bis lamented pupil George
Seale. Probably some humorless Ladies’ League
or other will be told, one of these days, that Mr.
Foley is again breaking up the home, and will
denounce him fiercely without waiting for par-
ticulars.
S.G. HUTTON, the Labor and Anti-Borrowing
Candidate for Gordon (N.S.W.J.
Photo, by Creelman & Co., Sydney.
Some ancient reminiscences :
There lately died in Paris, aged 72, Theodore Tilton,
an American journalist who made a spacious fuss in his day
and generation. Some 35 years ago he was reckoned the
greatest editor in New York, or in the U.S. Likewise he
had wealth and a beautiful wife, and he accused Henry
Ward Beecher, the eloquent and most shrieky preacher,
brother of the “ Uncle Tom’s Cabin ” lady, of misconduct
with the wife. The row filled America with bi tterness. An
ecclesiastical council found Beecher not guilty; a plain,
secular jury didn’t acquit him —the best he got from it
was a disagreement. The man in the street and the bloke
upon the highway, being unregenerates, took it for
granted that Beecher was guilty. It was difficult to take
an3 r other view of a combustible parson who once com-
menced a sermon by wiping his brow and stating to a vast
congregation, “ It’sdamned hot.” Then he wiped his brow
once more, and again said, “It’s damned hot.” Finally
he explained that he had heard a man use the expression
as he came in, and that a worshipper shouldn’t employ
such language while entering the tabernacle. Theodore
Tilton never amounted to much after his row with
Beecher; he lost his wife and his job, and Beecher’s big
following pursued him with violent animosity. And
Beecher himself was somewhat of a draggled idol for the
rest of his days. He lived' and preached and collected a
big salary for 12 more years, but music-hall songs were
made about him, and swift girls tried to make appoint-
ments with him, and swift men called him “agay dog.
Chambers’ Encyclopaedia, which includes him in the same
volume with the articles headed “ Beetle ” and “ Bilge-
water,” whitewashes him heroically, but to little pur-
pose.

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
Many hundreds of people in Australia would
like to hear from the Marconi Wireless Telegraph Co. of
Canada re the shares offered for sale in this country by
.Munroe and Munroe, of New York, “agents for the
Marconi underwriters of America.” The Marconi W.T.
Co. of Canada exists by virtue of a special Act of the
Dominion farliament, is subsidised by, and is under con-
tract with, the Canadian Government, and may be said
to have had its character for fair dealing guaranteed by the
Government thatgave it life. Nevertheless, a few thousands
of peoplein the Common wealth who hold vouchersf or shares
in the co. are unable, after months of waiting, to get any
definite information as to whether they will ever receive
scrip for their money. The moral responsibility for this
is considerably divided, and at the back of it all stands
the Canadian Government, which has at least a senti-
mental, if not a political, interest in the following state-
ment of bare facts.
About a year ago a voluble Yankee person
named Horace G. Robinson arrived in Melbourne
from New York, with instructions to talk about wireless
telegraphy, give demonstrations of its working, and
induce the public to buy shares (or vouchers for shares)
in the Marconi Co. of Canada. Robinson talked
with great success. Before he left Melbourne, to repeat
his business programme in the other States, he had
received more than £7OOO on account of the shares he
professed to sell. The man’s representations of himself
and his business mission were absolutely honest. He was
Horace Robinson, the accredited representative of
Munroe and Munroe, of New York, who were agents for
the American underwriters of Marconi Co. stock, and
whose undertakings to deliver the scrip had hitherto been
fulfilled. Robinson offered the £1 shares at 255.; and, con-
sidering that they had been below par in Canada, there was
no reason to doubt that the underwriters would be glad
to get quit of 70,000 shares in Australia.
The total number of shares sold in the
Commonwealth has not been stated. Probably
Horace Robinson’s “takings” were not less than
£25,000 ; they have been estimated as high as £60,000.
The money was banked in Melbourne and Sydney
for a while with the Union, and duly drafted to
Munroe and Munroe in New York. The people who
parted with the money were told that the shares
would in due course be forwarded by Munroe and
Munroe to the Union Bank, for distribution among
the voucher-holders. They were hop.ful people at
that time and for months afterwards. But the
scrip is long overdue. Information received, by private
inquirers, from the secretary of the co., gave the
recipients some reason to hope that the delay had been
unavoidable, and the scrip might come to hand not later
than the middle of May. It is now August, but no scrip,
nor any apology for its non-appearance, has reached Mel-
bourne. A few shares are said to have been delivered in
Sydney, but this vague assertion needs confirmation.
The people who paid for scrip would now, presumably,
jump at a chance to get their money back, or even some
of it. Anyhow, they want some sort of satisfaction, and
it should be somebody’s official business to see that they
get it.

There are three lots of shares —preference, deferred pre-
ference and ordinary. All now get 5 per cent. But for
the deferred preference and ordinary shares it means
very little ; for in 1905, at a final cleaning up, the former
were written down from £2 10s. to ss. and the latter from
£5 to Is. ;at the same time also the pref shares came
down from £2 10s. to £1 10s. The one cheerful feature
about it is that the realisation of the written-down
securities is panning out a shade better than had been
provided for ; so that the special reserve, formed of the
surpluses, and earmarked for the benefit of the deferred
preference and ordinary shares, is mounting up. As
follows :
Profit on
Total Sales Special
Sales. Completed. Reserve.
June, 1905 .. £13,994 £794 £794
Dec., 1905 .. 20,193 1340 2134
June, 1906 .. 33,218 1366 3500
Dec., 1906 .. 52,343 9989 13,489
June, 1907 .. 17,427 2496 15,985
There were, at the end of the half-year after the writing-
down, £316,857 of properties, and the sales have amounted
to £137,175. The special reserve as shown above amounts
to £15,985. But that only includes the'surplus on the
transactions actually completed ; the surplus on sales on
time-payment is not reckoned in until the last pay-
ment is made ; and unfortunately the book value of
the properties absolutely got rid of is not given. The
new business is not progressing very fast, notwith-
standing that the position how looks solid enough. In 12
months the deposits have come back from £358,742 to
£349,664 ; though, mainly on account of the completed
sales, it has been possible to increase the advances (apart
from balances of purchase money, £117,187) from £124,513
to £158,689. The cash position is not strong; cash,
deposit receipts, and other liquid assets represent only
12 per cent, of the debts to the public ; but, as against
that, it has to be remembered that the current account
money amounts to only £12,652. A fairer way of looking
at it is that the shareholders’ interest in the business
amounts to £218,512 ; and that would allow of the whole
of the non-liquid assets being further written down 10s.
6d. in the £ ; so that new business is very well covered.
Because it faced the situation and cleaned up thoroughly
(as far as can be seen), the concern deserves well; but it
will probably not do as well as it deserves until it can
flash more coin and indisputably liquid assets in the eye
of the public.

June, 1906.. 56,237 15 ~ „ 59,971 117,278
Deo., 1906 .. 65,461 15,, „ 59,996 5465 122,743
June, 1907 .. 55,140 15 „ „ 60,000 117,883
To which reserves £232,227 of premium capital and
£10,652 representing meter-renewal reserve may be
added ; and there are also suspense accounts, probably
to a great extent reserves also, amounting to £66,681.
This co.’s accounts are mere skeletons ; the profit and
loss account simply bristles with “etc.,” and “etc.”
covers a multitude of sins. Thus : “Revenue: sale of
gas, etc" ; “ expenditure : manufacture, distribution,
maintenance, etc.”; “miscellaneous charges, interest,
bad debts, etc." The real point to be reached is—What
provision is being made for depreciation, and are there
any hidden reserves? One of the “etc.’s” may cover
outlay in extending the plant, and that would mean a
reserve which the accounts don’t divulge. On the eo.’s
own showing it barely pays its dividend ; there are not
as much reserves shown as there were five and a half
years ago. But the directors don’t seem to be gentlemen
of that sort at all. Chairman George Judah Cohen, for
instance, is chairman also of the Commercial Banking
Co., of Sydney, whose reserves have been increased by
about £40,000 a year. As at December, 1901, the sales of
gas, “ etc.,” were said to be £199,791 ; the profits were said
to be £56,818. Last half-year “ sales of gas, etc.”—the co.
still sells “ etc.”—were £226,991; but the profit was alleged
to be only £55,140. Yet gas is still the old 4s. From
time to time large sums of capital have been got in. At
December, 1896, the capital and reserves were £981,099;
10 years later they were £1,288,882, or £307,783 more.
Yet the fixed investments —buildings, plant, etc.—into
which the capital would almost certainly be put, increased
by only £215,321. That seems to point in the same
direction—to profits being used to write down plant.
The one solid fact that can be laid hold of definitely is
that the disclosed profits for the past 12 months were at
the rate of £lO 7s. 9d. per cent, on capital and reserves
of all kinds as at July Ist, 1906. The State-owned rail-
ways, supplying freight, had a remarkably good year ;
yet for 1906 they only' charged the public enough to show
£4 Bs. 6d. per cent., and for 1905 £3 9s. 3d. per cent.
Certainly the Gas Co. owes in temporary loans, deben-
tures, etc.” (good old “etc.” !), £349,950, and to con-
sumers (deposits) £18,830; but the interest on all this is
deducted from the profits. Counting that in as capital,
the return might be reduced to £9 10s. And even then
we are still left in the dark as to how much profit (if any)
was put into plant, etc., and not disclosed. If there were
any such expenditure the real profits are higher than
that. On the other hand, if there were none, then the
dividend was not justified. Either way, it is an unsatis-
factory balance-sheet. The object plainly is to keep the
public in the dark; and to do that shareholders and in-
vestors have to be kept in the dark too.

The reserves consist of premium capital £466,535, formal
reserve fund £82,717, meter reserve £20,977, and undivided
profit £2803. Of the amount shown above as written off
for the half-year, £2667 represents the sum charged to
the meter renewal reserve, and £2500 was voted out of
profits to cover the co.’s loss on the hiring and repair of
gas stoves; which latter means that, in order to
encourage people to use gas for domestic fuel, the co.
puts up with a loss of £2500 on hiring out the machines
that consume the fuel. Besides this amount written off,
£29,231 was paid direct out of revenue to cover ordinary
maintenance and repairs. The co.’s share capital (apart
from premiums) is £857,210; the debenture debt is
£848,000. The whole capital is therefore £2,171,745, of
which £2,149,842 has been expended. The net profits for
thehalf-year before payinginterest were £80,617; and that
show's a return at the rate of £7 9s. lid. per cent. For the
corresponding half of 1906 the return on the same net
revenue basis was £6 17s. 9d. per cent. ; for the ditto of
1900, £6 14s. 4d. per cent. This steady but emphatic
increase is largely due to the fact tha additions to works
are being made out of profits instead of being charged to
capital account; in other words, a large reserve is being
accumulated which is not shown as a reserve. In the
past five years the amount thus spent has totalled
£94,167 ; and that means something like £6500 a year
added to the co.’s earnings. Apart, however, from that,
the undertaking is more profitable than it was. At
June, 1902 (five years ago), the return on total capital out-
lay was £6 10s. 7d. per cent. The capital outlay then was
£2,144,363. Add to that the £94,167 since paid for works
out of profit, and the £5479 paid for works out of
capital, and the present return is still at the rate
of £7 3s. 9d. per cent, on the total outlay. Thus the
co. is not only making a large reserve provision not shown
in the reserves, hut the earning power of the whole
undertaking, whether provided out- of capital or revenue,
has advanced just about 10 per cent, in the past five
years. And there is a further advantage for the co. in
sight; for of the £848,000 of debentures £560,000 carrying
the high rate of 5 per cent, fall due 1908-1912. These
debentures are nearly all held outside Australia ; there
has not been a single reported sale of them on the Mel-
bourne Exchange this year. There is therefore a chance
ahead to get rid of some foreign debt, and let Australia
keep the interest. Unfortunately, only £lOO,OOO of the
debentures fall duo next year ; the bulk are dated 1910.
Here, by the way, are some comparisons, with
proportionate increases shown :
June, 1900. June, 1907. Increases.

been in the wages at the works and the cost of purifying
the gas. This may be explainable—labor-saving devices
may have been introduced, and purification may have
been simplified ; but on the figures the record isn’t one
that the co. has any right to be proud of.

On all shares.
May, 1907 17,234 20 p.c. =£12,500 3690 34,177
All shares (125,000) are now fully paid to £1 each. The
50,000 contributing shares were issued in 1898, and 6s. Bd.
was paid on them. In eight years that 6s. Bd. has
brought in 245. 9d. ; 13s. 4d. of that has been capitalised,
and the balance divided. It has been something gorgeous.
The present dividend on these shares on the amount
actually paid is 60 per cent. Naturally Perth begins to
wonder whether it ought to provide 60 per cent, divi-
dends to a co. which supplies it with a public necessity
like lighting. The city council therefore lately asked
the co. what it would sell out for (the council has the
right to buy, price to be fixed by arbitrators). The
co. said £400,000. The council said something else—a
much shorter word. Also it proceeded to get in an
engineer to prepare plans for an electric-light scheme ;
and there the matter stands. Meantime, however, Perth
has passed on a swag of its shares to the credulous
t’othersider, who only knew what he saw in the balance-
sheet ; while the co., in view of the threatening opposi-
tion, has promptly brought down its charge for electric
light (it supplies both gas and electricity) to a maximum
of 6Jd. and a minimum of 2Jd. per unit. (The mini-
mum charged by the Melbourne municipality is 2d.
for light and Jd. for power.) On the capital and reserves
the net profits for the past year were at the rate of
£2O Bs. percent. The present charges are so high, and
the profits so gorgeous, that, even on the £400,000 asked
by the co., last year’s net profit amounted to £7 16s. per
cent.—that is, after writing about 5 per cent, off capital
in the electric supply department, plus its proportion of
general assets. It looks, then, as though the munici-
pality could afford to pay the £400,000 and still get in-
terest and sinking fund, provided it were content to go
on making- the same charges as the co. But that is no
doubt just what it isn’t prepared to do : if the public has
to pay over 20 per cent, on the actual outlay, it might
just as well pay it to Peter as to Paul. So there are
changes ahead. And the co. may be thankful that its
innings has lasted as long as it has.

Going back only seven years, when the first report of the
new co. was issued, the capital was then £l2o,ooo(and that,
by the way, was a £1 for £1 watering). Then £20,000
was called up at par. Then £20,000 was paid up out of
profits. Now the other 40,000 authorised shares have
been issued, but onlv 10s. per share has yet been called
up. This shows the movement in that and other
respects:—
June, June, Dec., June,
1900. 1906. 1906. 1907.
Capital .. £120,000 £160,000 £160,000 £179,986
Creditors .. 2,156 2,450 11,978 2,745
Ships .. 37,324 53,3u8 60,687 66,805
Land, etc. .. 81,024 109,425 120,350 124,486

There were 28 of the new shares unapplied for. The
present postion is something like this : A man had, say,
12 shares ; so far as this co. is concerned they cost him
£l2. Then he got two for £2. Tnen two more for
nothing. Now he gets four more (half paid up) for £2.
So for £l6 he has £lB in shares, which bring in 11J per
cent, on his outlay ; and there is £2 yet to be called up.
If it is called up it will no doubt carry 10
per cent. ; but it may be paid up out of pro-
fits. For there is no doubt that all the pro-
fits are not disclosed. In the past 12 months the
Kai-Kai and the Kookooburra have both been built and
launched ; yet the value of ship property has only been
added to by £13,497. The balance of the cost has been ,
paid out of hidden profits. That sort of thing has been
going on all the time. The book-value of the ferries ,
was actually less at December, 1906 (£60,687), (
than it was at December, 1902 (£64,642), though the
Kulgoa, Kareela and Una were meantime added to the
fleet, and the Kai-Kai and Kookooburra partly paid for.
Of course, it is perfectly right to wipe off something for
depreciation ; yet, after all, these ferries are long-livers-*—
witness some of the old Parramatta tubs, for instance.
How long this hugely profitable business is going to last
there is no telling ; the North Shore Bridge is again in
the air, and may get itself built this time ; and if it is,
the co.’s past will be one thing and its future will be an-
other. On its actual capital and apparent reserves
(£203,430) it may still be profitable ; but it is one thing
to be profitable on that basis and another to be ditto on
the £585,000 at which the market capitalises the.concern.
Port Jackson Co-operative Steamship Co.
(Sydney), which has grown affluent with great rapidity
and now wants to alter its form and change the name
under which it has done so well

And yet the skeleton of a profit and loss account pub-
lished contains but the one item—“ Net profit for half-
year, after providing insurances, new steamer, etc.,
£2757.” It is an amazing profit and loss account that
only tells shareholders and the investor what the net
profit is after providing for such a trifleas “ new steamer,
etc.” A comparison of some balance-sheet items will,
however, make things clearer :
Dec., 1906. June, 1907.
Insurance fund .. .. £5,000 £lO,OOO
Plant renewal account .. 21,000 21,000
New steamer account .. nil 8,000
Employers’liability fund .. 400 400
Public risk fund .. .. 400 301
Undivided profit .. .. 2,551 5,308
Total .. .. £29,351 £45,069
The differencebetween these totals (£15,718) represents the
profit for the half-year ; but something has to be written
off that to provide for depreciation. Also most of the
reserves mentioned are not true reserves : they are the
cover for partly-expired risks. How much, then, was
actual profit it is impossible to say ; for instance, nobody
knows what the actual wear and tear on the boats
amounted to. There is no doubt, however, that either
the provision hitherto has been grotesquely inadequate,
or the statement as to the alleged net profit (£2757) is a
joke. The cash position can be ascertained. Six
months ago the co. owed its bankers and other credi-
tors £10,683, and had £1477 of cash, etc., leaving £9206
net; now it has a liquid surplus of £5037, showing an
improvement of £14,243.
Some premiums on new shares are mentioned
above. The present co., when it took over an almost
moribund competitor (it wasn’t very much alive itself)
paid in part with £8745 of 5 per cent, interminable
debentures: and the men who got this first charge on
the profits, this sure 5 per cent., chuckled. Now, how-
ever, they have been glad to accept in payment 3468
shares paid to 255. ; and thus the co. gets rid of a debt of
£8745 at a cost of £4335 of new capital. The other £4335
for the present has to be treated as premium capital
received by the co. (The £75 unaccounted for represents
unredeemed debentures.) Now it is proposed to recon-
struct the co., add the name Manly to the title, and pro-
vide a little scope for present and future “ watering.” It
makes directors nervous in these days to see 14 per cent,
dividends paid (“This Socialistic Labor party, you
know !”), and “ water” is the handiest substance to use
in order to make a fat 20 per cent, masquerade as a
moderate 6 per cent.

Through the long drought—which affected its district
very severely—this co. continued to pay a small dividend.
While the State was expected to carry fodder and starvin'g
stock for nothing, Private Enterprise still pointed a
stiff finger at its schedule of rates. There has been a
partial recovery in local conditions since then ; and, as
expenses are less than they were, the net results are
again fairly satisfactory. Here are some details: —

Maintenance includes renewals ; even during the drought
and the depression this work was not neglected—the
rates were kept high enough to pay for it, and leave a
bit over. Therefore now, in the present better times,
there are no back repairs to pick up and pay for, as is
the case with almost every State railway system, in con-
nection with which so many concessions were demanded
that something had to be cut out of the expenditure,
wherefore mostly the stitch in time that saves nine was
postponed. This co., besides its share capital of
£lOO,OOO, has £23,500 of debenture capital. It has paid
off £6500 of bonds in the past six months, and, after pay-
ing the present dividend, will still have about £2OOO cash
in hand. The reserves, apart from that, are used in the
business.

In view of the Beer Combine of Melbourne, there has
been a cleaning-up here ; and this shows some of the
results. But not all of them. The profit and loss account
charge for bad debts is lumped with the management
charges ; and as apparently advances have been written
down severely, what might possibly have been a small
profit has been turned into a heavy loss. In 1906 the
composite management-bad debts charge amounted to
£6137, and the year before it was £7077. This year it is
£18,520 ; so probably about £11,500 more than usual has
been provided for bad and doubtful debts —which would
account for the whole of the loss and £l9B besides. Still,
such losses have to be provided for sometime ; and if
they come in a lump now' it is only because they haven’t
been gradually provided for hitherto. Some more
details :
1900. 1906. 1907.
Debenture debt .. .. £lOO,OOO £lOO,OOO £lOO,OOO
Other dbbts to public .. 55,838 29,020 24,104
Breweries, etc 83,569 84,174 82,174
Hotels, etc 107,686 123,984 122,952
Advances, trade debtors .. 58,162 32,255 15,637
Stock and plant .. .. 34,777 14,991 10,300
Cash, etc 6,261 3,428 9,154
This co.’s brewery w'ill be closed under the Combine
scheme ; but the hotels will not be necessarily taken
over, though the Combine holds certain rights of pur-
chase, and meantime the properties may not be sold to
anybody else. The Combine takes over “ the whole
goodwill and business connections and stock-in-trade
and movable plant ” ; and the Castlemaine gets 12 per
cent, of the stock in the new’ co. as payment. These
things, however, are not mentioned in the Castlemaine
directors’report; altogether, in fact, as little as possible
has been said anywhere about the Combine’s arrange-
ments.

Swallow and Ariell, Ltd. (Melbourne), manu

Loss.
