1905 .. 10,658 Nil. 10,658 36,262
1906 .. 12,140 1J per cent. =£1824 10,316 46,578
1907 .. 12,209 2£ „ „ 3648 8,561 55,139
This co. got into trouble four or five years ago through
the sudden changes in the markets. It bought its raw
products during the drought at high prices, not knowing
how long the drought was going to last; and then the
plentiful harvests came, with their low’ prices, and the
stocks had to be written down. The high prices even while
the drought lasted had cut down the profits ; sb the co.
was hit both W’ays. Now, though the later figures above
look very cheerful indeed, there are similar risks ahead.
The trouble —or part of the trouble—is that the co. is at
the mercy of market fluctuations; and just now’it has
unusually heavy stocks. At April, 1905, they were dowm
for £54,542 ;in 1906, £56,968 ; now, £76,328. And this
increase of nearly £20,000 is for the most part represented
by borrowed money. It was like that before the last
trouble. Between 1901 and 1903 (both inclusive) the
mortgages and overdrafts ran up from £78,270 to £94,293.
Then, by the reduction of stocks and by other means, they
were got down, in 1906, to £50,571. Now, they are up
again to £61,574, in spite of the large profits of the last
year or two. Things may come out ail right; if prices
don’t fall they no doubt will. There is really not enough
capital in the business, and there is too much
paper; the capital is only £145,953, while the
original purchase of freeholds, machinery, etc.,
represented £148,500. That leaves the whole of
the book debts (£30,835), additions made to plant, etc.,
since the original purchase (£31,341), and stock (£76,328),
besides smaller items, to be financed with borrowed
money, and reserves, or on open credit; and it is
dangerous. What provision for depreciation is made it
is impossible to say ; tbe item is shown every year as
additions less allowances for depreciation, so you only
get the net additions; the allowance for depreciation
may be much or little. Also the exact size of the good-
will still included (after 19 years) as an asset is unknown ;
but it apparently amounts to something considerable,
and is one of the reasons why, though the co; started off
gallantly in the boom times with 8 per cent, dividends, it
has not got past 3f per cent, for 12 or 15 years, and its £1
shares are still worth only about 9s. 6d. A few years ago
they were down to 2s.

This co., which still finds the cold shoulder of the non-
oo-operative concerns turned upon it, is nevertheless
bearing up very well under the affliction. This time it is
able to return no less than 30 per cent, of the wool-selling
commission to its clients, and that makes up for a good
deal of cold shoulder. Also, by the way, the rebate accounts
for the cold shoulder ; it would no doubt account for a
whole leg, with a heavy boot at the end of it, if it was
thought that that attention would help the co. out at all.
At present, however, judging by these figures, the co.
seems very like staying
1904. 1905. 1906. 1907.
Commissions, etc. ..£11,557 £13,167 £17,944 £20,190
Working Expenses .. 9,444 8,439 8,156 9,204
Expenses p.c. of corns. 81'8 64 T 45'5 45 5
Of course such growth of the commission figures can t
continue. Australia hasn’t left all its years of drought
behind it. But by limiting its dividends and keeping its
expenses well in hand, the co. has been able to build up
a tine reserve fund, which it has used to clear off the
debt from its stores and premises ; so that when the
lean years come it will have no appalling interest bill to
totter under. In 1903 its liabilities were £10,463 in excess
of its liquid assets : now, after providing for the bonus
to clients, the surplus of liquid assets over liabilities is
£1896; and all the new capital that has been got in to
help along this very fine Improvement is £126. There
are probably advantages from the wool-grower’s point of
view which the accounts cannot show ; at any rate, the
existence of a concern of this sort is certain to have
tended towards lower rather than higher charges, quite
apart from the bonus or refund.

From Mr. A. J. Brierley : “ Generally I agree
with The Bulletin criticisms on those people who evince
a disinclination to invest in Australian industrial con-
cerns—a disinclination which I am certain is becoming
less and less pronounced now that the policy of the
Federation is fixed. But apropos the Oriental Timber
Corporation (B. 18/7/’O7) The Bulletin is barking up the
wrong tree. The duty on dressed timber will enable the
new concern to give employment with a direct wages
sheet of probably £20,000 per annum, and a large indirect
expenditure which will go into the pockets of Australian
workmen instead of those of Norway and Sweden as at
present. The movement is really an exploitation of
foreign raw material, to be worked up into the finished
article by Australians, and will place this country for
once in the same position as that held by foreign coun-
tries in respect to the other industry mentioned by you
—the woollen industry. The facetious remark as to
nosing under the snow and ice shows that you are aware
that it freezes sometimes in the vicinity of Vladivostock ;
but unless the fall of snow in that region approaches the
one mentioned by Baron Munchausen when he tied his
horse’s bridle to the top of a church steeple, the only
tethering post above the snow, it will still be possible to
obtain logs growing eighty to one hundred feet above the
snow line.
“Seriously, however, it is my experience that
there is plenty of capital in Australia waiting for invest-
ment in concerns which have the ‘ right people ’ in charge
of them, and which can show that there is a fair chance of
the capital being safe, and where the promoters have
such faith in their own ventures that they are willing to
share the risk fairly with the capitalist. Many of the
persons seeking money capitalise their brains at such
figures that there is little chance of dividends for the
capitalist, forgetting that the brains are not a realisable
asset when the affair goes into liquidation, and that,
when that time arrives, they take over the brains at
a large depreciation so far as the company’s balance-sheet
is concerned.”

Re City Bank of Sydney (B. 25/7/’O7). In the

* * * *
The Zinc Corporation was to have held a meeting
in London on Tuesday to get authority to issue 182,000
pref. shares of £1 each. The situation is so desperate
that the new shares are to be offered all the profits until £1
has been paid back per share, and then they are to carry
a 20 per cent, preference, and to share proportionately in
surplus profits beyond that. Present shareholders can
take two of the 'new issue for every five now held.
Naturally, in view of the big bite to which pref. share-
holders are to be entitled, the ordinary shares have
tumbled headlong to 4s. Even as late as December last
they were 38s. It has been a colossal case of self-
deception in spite of obvious facts and repeated warnings.
* * » *
North Extended, Broken Hill, is trying hard
to pick up two things—(l) thecontinuation of the North
mine’s lode ; (2) a little cash to keep the diamond drill
o-oing. After all the development work and prospec ing
that has been done by the present co., the lost-lode
problem remains as much of a problem as ever. The
drill has proved a very useful addition to the equipment
of the Broken Hill manager ; but it has its limitations.
It has been known to go through a lode without the core
showing any trace of .mineral, as the drill may bend and
follow a fault. It is also liable to pass within a foot
of a lode without whistling to the driver to stop. The
North mine is following the lode northward towards the
Victoria shaft, but where it goes after it crosses the
North’s boundary, no man can tell. Perhaps it doesn’t
go anywhere ; for every lode stops at some point or other.
* * * *
Of all the concentrating mills along the
Broken Hill line of lode the one at the Junction
North seems to come in for the severest criticism.
Its alteration has certainly been strung out for a pro-
voking length of time, and the perplexing part of it is
that the management has nothing to gain by pro-
crastination. The presence of large "quantities of rhodo-
nite in Junction North is no doubt to a great extent
responsible for the low grade of its concentrates, as com-
pared with those of the North and South mines. The
hio-h specific gravity of rhodonite renders concentration
difficult, and in the case of the Junction North the
gangue is so heavy that there is only a small working
difference between it and the metallic product. As show-
ins what rhodonite means to a mill not specially adjusted
to deal with it, the South Block’s mill, when a block of
the red terror from the Junction North was put thiough
the ore-breaker to test the strength of the machine pro-
duced a concentrate consisting almost entuelj oi
rhodonite! „

The little copper-producing mines of N. S.
Wales, which have to struggle in order to live up to the
reputations given to them during the boom, are begin
ning to groan under the smelting and other charges.
There is the Budgery, for example. A report states that
the principal working shows ore up to 10 per cent. , ana
even that is a long drop from the value of the stuft
found in the enriched area first struck. It is a tuithei
drop to the 6’86 per cent, which the last consignment
was reported to be worth. Still at £9O a tc >"’ J P®*
cent, ore is worth £6 3s. sd. per ton. The actual return
got for the lot was £3 10s. lOd. per ton. After paying
charges off the mine, therefore, the ore for practical pur-
poses w'as worth 3’93 per cent. And that is another neck-
breaking drop.

New Hillgrove’s new shaft is down 1258 ft.,
which is below’ the old workings ; a chamber at No. 11
level is nearly completed. A quantity of electrical
machinery, including copper cables and transformers,
has arrived —another sample of the craze for importing
is this idea of bringing copper cables to a copper-producing
country. High-tension electricity (brought from the
o-enerating station, where water-turbines drive dynamos;
will haul the ore and drive the Eleanora and N ew Hillgrove
crushing plants. # #
A discovery of some importance has taken
place six miles west of Yalgogrin (N.S. W.), where a gold-
bearing reef, 3ft. w'ide, has been struck near the surface.
It is on State land, held under special lease by G. Iroy.
A crushing of 15 tons yielded 250 z. The discovery has
given an impetus to prospecting work at Yalgogrin.
Blayney new co. (N.S.W.) is still pumping out
the water, which conies up as green as grass, and it is
saving the copper contents while manager Eiddington and
staff make extensive preparations for a smelting campaign.
Two furnaces are ready, hut the supply of ore on hand is
small. The co. is pushing its custom business, but no doubt
finds it difficult to get ore forwarded or bonded to works
that are not in blast; hence the hurry. Still, without a
mine to fall back upon in case the custom ore fails, the
co. has to take the pretty large risk of a shut-down
before long.
* * * *
Great Cobar new shaft is 15ft. long by 7Jft.
wide, a big hole to sink 1000 ft. deep. It is, for all that,
progressing at the rate of 140 ft. a month. Much is made
of the fact that ore reserves are 1,530,000 tons, or about
eight years’ supply at present rate of extraction. But if
the output is to be doubled (and £lOO,OOO worth of new
plant and machinery is being installed with that object),
it comes down to only four years. As against that, the
bottom levels, .particularly on the northern shoot of ore,
are showing up remarkably well. And at No. 4 level in the
Ohesney is ore worth 2oz. gold per ton ; but how much
there is of it has not been proved.
So far, Melbourne Cope’s Creek—a sluicing
proposition—is the bright star among the N. S. Wales
tin ventures in James Mackay’s Melbourne office. The
Elliott and the Stannum Lode have not had time yet to
get into their stride, perhaps. True, they were born in
November last year, while the Melb. Cope’s Creek was
born in July—only five months before —but the lode pro-
positions call for more preliminaries. The actual capital
put into Cope’s was £6500, and of that £lOOO went to the
vendors ; they also got £5OOO in shares, making the pre-
sent paid-up capital £11,500. In the 12 months, £5500
has been paid in divs., and at the end of June there was
a liquid surplus of £2995. Last half-year, the co. got
£9850 for its tin at a cost of £3725, leaving £6125 profit,
less £464 for depreciation. The ground sluiced yielded
an almost imperceptible shade under 21b. tin to the cubic
yard, and the cost, including management, was B‘43d.
per yard; excluding management, 8 - 02 d. By w .y of
comparing costs of successful dredging and sluicing enter-
prises : tiie No. 1 Tewksbury dredge, working (for gold)
near Bright (Vic.), last half-year worked at l'36d. per yard.
The old Nymagee (N.S.W.), bought a while
back by an English co. from the Great Cobar people, is
bein°- fitted up with a new plant as elaborate as the old
one was ramshackle. B. and W. boilers of 500 h.p., to
drive 20 machine drills and serve an engine capable of
lifting four tons from 2000 ft., are to go in ; and that gives
some idea of the new equipment. Meantime the new
owners are busy underground, and they have already
opened up enough ore to last the furnaces two years.
Nymagee in all its days—and it has seen some good
ol i e g —was never struck by such energy before.

The record of Mt. Bischoff (Tas.) recently
Dec., ’O5. June, ’O6. Dec., ’O6. June, ’O7.
Tin sold .. £42,'79 £50,023 £48,190 £25,182
Dividends.. 30,000 36,000 27,000 18,000
A bad fall, considering the extraordinary heights at which
tin has stood. The worst has happened during the past
half-year. The dividend list consequently went back from
£27 000 to £18,000; or, to go further into ancient
history, from £36,000 to half that figure. Liquid assets
stick at about £73,000, of which £40,000 is fixed deposit
and £13,000 Treasury bills. The smelting return tells
the tale of the co.’s work. During the six months
52 000 tons of ore were treated, and 232 J tons of tin were
produced. Costs last half-year were ss. l'6d. per ton-
cheap enough for anything. General-manager Kayser
for the first time gives an estimate of the ore in sight: —
Gossan, 83,704 tons; porphyry, 951,330 tons; Queen
lode, 2031 tons; pyritic, 2485 tons; total, 1,039,550.
He works that out at 1,000,000 tons of “crush dirt.” On
the past half-year’s results, there should be about 4460
tons of tin in that; hut there is no proof at all that the
past half-year’s 52,000 tons were an average of the lot.
And there is less ground to expect that the past half-
year’s tin prices will be maintained. To estimate quantity
was not difficult; it would be much harder to estimate
the worth of the ore, and that Kayser has left severely
alone. The mine, however, looks better than it did six
months ago, and the directors think that “ a similar-
output can still be secured without unfairly calling upon
the mine’s resources.” This is a negative way of putting
things, and the Board takes care to point out that the
profits of last half-year have been dependent greatly on
the price of tin. Therefore, it can be quite understood
directors are still on the look out for another mine.

Crown Lyell Copper Co. (Tas.), according to
the balance-sheet dated June 30, has a fixed deposit of
£lOOO. Judging from the thrifty way in which affairs
move nowadays, this will last the best part of a year. No
longer are the co.’s office rents and directors’ fees prizes
to be hankered after, for £23 10s. was all that was cut up
among five men during six months, and £4O represented
office rent and manager’s salary. Petty cash seems to be
absent, or we might look for some escape in that direc-
tion. Indeed, it would be hard to find in the wide world
a £250,000 co. run with so much modesty and so little
expense. The co.’s aim is to pick up the North Lyell ore
body, or wait till it makes into the Grown Lyell's ground.
But the formation sticks to the North Lyell with great
tenacity. The co., however, is doing the most sensible
work it has undertaken—driving along the contact of the
consrlomerates and the schist.
* * * *
Boulder Perseverance scrip rose to 95., the
other day, on the strength of a “ bull” cable. Next day
there was a rush to get out, and shares sagged to 7s.
Said cable alleged, among other things, that new develop-
ments in the south end promise a continuance of present
returns ; but if the few dabs of good ore just exposed in
that locality are all the mine has to depend on, it must
be in a worse way than even the most pessimistic of its
critics imagine. When the last estimate of reserves was
made up, the dividend value of the property ran out at
about 2s. 6d. per share. That ought to be a pretty stood
guide.
Ivanhoe east lode cut at 1500 ft. in the Boulder
co.’s north block, is one of the best and biggest joints in
the backbone of Kalgoorlie. It traverses the entire length
of the Ivanhoe lease, and then extends north across
Boulder territory, and south through the Horseshoe.
It is payable for a length of over 300 ft. ; and, like the
Boulder main ore-shoot, it is persistent in depth. Some-
one has said that the Boulder, Ivanhoe and Horseshoe
will be producing gold long after the moss has grown
green over the graves of the other big mines of the field,
and probably time will prove it so.

recent test crushing from the Callion (W.A.) was reported

about half that amount. The management may explain

which the concern is viewed.

A Perth shareholder in the wound-up Bayley’s
Mines, Ltd., is anxious to know how he stands. Some
months ago the leases, machinery and plant were
auctioned. It was alleged that the co. bad never re-
ceived a transfer from its predecessor, Bayley’s Gold
Mines, Ltd. What the aggrieved shareholder particularly
desires to be enlightened on is : (L) Does he hold scrip in
a co. that never possessed any property? (2) Was the
sale legal? (3) Has he any redress against the co. for
keeping him in the dark as to what was going on until
after the sale had taken place? Other Australian share-
holders want to know much the same things.
The forfeiture of the Paringa leases was recom-
mended by the Kalgoorlie mining warden the other day.
As the application was undefended, it is evident that the
co. wants to be relieved of its burden. B.,M.andCo.
had charge of the Paringa group for some time, but
finally gave up in despair. Tributors have since followed
suit. The co.’s best-positioned block adjoins the Brown
Hill on the west and the Associated Northern on the
north. However, position isn’t gold.
* » •* *
The Vivien Co,, one of 8., M. and Co.’s West-
ralian group, the other day issued a statement of
accounts covering the past two years. At the time
the co. was almost on its uppers as regards ready cash ;
but as a profit of £7OO per month is now being earned
from ore-treatment, the next balance-sheet should be
better. During the two years 75,552 tons of ore were
opened up, making (after allowing for 45,055 tons
extracted in the interim) a total reserve of 81,743 tons,
worth 325. per ton. These are the official figures. On
the basis of present output, the reserves are equal to
about two years’ supplies. The co. has been working for
some years, but shareholders have received no return.

Queensland Menzies Co. is having a bleak time
with the Goodenough lease, and its hopes about the
value of this show must be gone by now. The manager is
off to Brisbane to confer with the directors, but what
good that is going to do is a mystery. It is interesting
to note that of the 30 odd English and Australian flota-
tions in the Menzies district, only two or three have kept
alive. The rest are dead and buried, or lying around
loose. #
The shoot has “gone poor” in the north
bottom level of the Fingall (W.A.), and it looks as if the
limit of payable ore has been reached at that point.
Further back in the drive the No. 3 winze has now been
sunk 150 feet in ore averaging a shade under 30s. per ton.
There is, however, a blank of about 300 feet between the
two Shoots. Word comes from London that the decision
of the Fingall directors to add a sixpenny bonus to the
half-crown dividend just paid, is in accordance with the
views held and expressed by some members of the Board,
that the management has carried caution too far in
estimating the value of the ore reserves.

Boulder No. 1, being satisfied with its sampling
of the St. George, Mount "Magnet (W.A.), exercises its
option, and pays £lO,OOO to get in.
At the safe distance of London, C. A. Moreing,
head of Bewick, Moreing and Co., has had a fling at
Stanley Hunter, the deep-lead geologist on the Victorian
Mines Department staff. Hunter no doubt erred in
saying that you must have 14dwt. to make deep-lead
mining pay, for the Great Southern and the Ohiltern
Valley, the two big Victorian mines, show to the con-
trary. But 8., M. and 00. have not only to prove that
they can make Bdwt. dirt profitable, but also to show
that they have even that much. Of course, if prior
expenditure is not to be counted in, then, with the lead
pretty well drained, Bdwt. ought to cover exes. With the
wash green, and pumping still needed, another 4dwt. may
have to go on to the total. It is idle, then, for Moreing to
tell London that alluvial mining can be carried on for
gdwt. Even Burnt Creek, at Maryborough, the cheapest
worked mine in Victoria, can’t do that; and the little
Cathcart is not putting up a record, though it is in
shallow ground. Moreing’s speech was made at the
meetings of the Consolidated Deep Leads and the Aus-
tralian Commonwealth Trust, where be and Chairman
Gell talked an appalling amount of crudities. There was
the old gabble about borehole prospects, though every-
one on this side knows bow unreliable they are. More-
in", nevertheless, laid it down as axiomatic that where
the’boreholes were richer the wash was richer. There was
talk of appointing a committee to investigate the affairs
of the group. That committee might also find out the
names of the parties who shot out scrip early. Apropos
that the Commonwealth Companies Act should pro-
vide’that all promoting and share-dealing corporations
shall show their shareholdings at regular intervals. With
such concerns in the field, the garden variety of share-
holder needs safeguarding. And publicity is the best
safeguard available. „ » *
New Loch Fyne, Matlock (Vic.), which
“Johnnie” Ripper tried to tempt London with, isn’t
panning out good enough even with Victorian manage-
ment The idea was that the show could be made a
second Alaska-Treadwell—a big dyke formation carrying
a low percentage of gold. But the work, so far, is not
profitable, due to the hardness of the dyke and the low-
ness of the contents. Still, the margin is narrow enough
to tempt further effort.

The collapse of the market in Catherine Reef
United six months back hit a lot of Victorians harder
than they have cared to admit. Old ex-manager Coakes
was so positive he was on a good wicket that
caution was kicked out of the way, and shares were
taken right and left at above 80s. Since then
they have been back towards 205., Coakes has
gone, and generally things have turned topsy-turvey.
Bend’igonians looked for a rise when the 2300 ft. stone was
developed. But the new manager, Langdon, decided to
try if the shoot worked at 2200 ft. lived up. So
he crosscut east away from the lava at 2100 ft.,
and picked up sft. of gold-bearing stone. Straight-
way shares jumped up 235. ; so if Langdon is
as lucky at 2300 ft., it is certain that Catherines
will spring high again. What looks well in this quarter
is that the winze going down on the lava from 2200 ft.
carriesgold-bearingstoneasfar assinkinghasgone. George
Lansell looked to making heaps of divs. out of the present
level, for he held the paramount interest in the mine.
Now’ his heirs have a chance of proving the old veteran’s
judgment. What makes Catherines such a delicious
gamble is that Williams United, next door, hit upon gold
in the continuation of the same formation south. The
quartz here is 100 ft. wide, and it would not take much
leaven to make such a mass pay thumping divs.

The new Mt. Elliott Co. is registered—the
English co. that has taken over the Cloncurry property.
And in J. Bull’s large, expansive way, the co. has been
capitalised at £7so,ooo—for a show which, though rich
and promising, isn’t so far proved to be much more than
a surface deposit. Also the five London directors of this
as vet non-productive concern, besides their £lOOO a year
fees are to have 2£ per cent, of the distributed profits in
any year when 10 per cent, is paid to shareholders. It is
a bad system : it offers a premium to the management to
rip out the rich ore and let development slide ; and that
means booms and bursts.

Cannindah (Q.), states Bewick Moreing have started to

be under the old regime.

South Glanmire and Monkland, Gympie (Q.),
is not providing big profits ; but an output of 1500 tons

* * * *
A correspondent of the New York Mining and
Engineering Journal alleges that at one period of its
history the Boulder Perseverance nearly came to grief
through the carelessness of its controllers. This brings
out Ralph Nichols, who emphatically denies the asser-
tion, but admits (1) that he was in charge of the mine at
the time mentioned, and (2) that he was responsible for
the gaudy report which caused all the mischief, although
the sampling and measurements were not his own. The
fact that this report estimated the reserves at about four
times their actual value does not disconcert him in the
least. In airy fashion he merely sums it up as a mistake,
and points to the annual reports of certain Kalgoorlie
mining cos. to prove that there were others. True ; but
the Perseverance was by far the worst case, as for some
time cream ore was gouged out wholesale, and the skim-
millc stuff was left for posterity to deal with.

The Aerial Tramways which have been erected
for the British and Sulphide Corporation (Central) Mines
at Broken Hill are fine examples of present-day skill and
technical knowledge. The Mines will effect considerable
saving by means of these Aerialways as compared with
any other known mechanical contrivance for i andling-
ores on a large scale. The British Mine will economise
to over 2s. per ton. These two plants have already been
subjected to severe tests, and have amply demonstrated;
their worth and ability. Both Aerialways were supplied
by VV. & J. Lampriere, of Melbourne, who represent A.
Bleichert & Co., of London, the largest firm in the world
for the manufacture of transport appliances.*

Two patent portable traction scoop engines
are being built to the order of Messrs. McLelland and
Cooper for cutting the main irrigation channels in the
Waranga Basin District of Victoria. The engines will be
placed on opposite sides of the channels and will pull and
tip the scoops in their own direction, and so make the
channel and form the embankment. There are two
engines of the vertical compound type, and, along with
winding drums, friction clutches, worm gearing, and
other appliances, are mounted on a mild steel framework
carriage which projects above and forward over a portion
of the locomotive boiler. The boiler is mounted on
driving and steering wheels. The main haulage engine
is 160 I.H.P. and the smaller one 35 I.H.P. The Austral
Otis Engineering Company Ltd., South Melbourne, are
the manufacturers.* [D.]

Auckland’s New Water Supply.—Opening
Ceremony by the Mayor.—Speaking at the opening
ceremony in connection with the recently completed ex-
tensive water supply contract, the Mayor of Auckland,
Mr. A. M. Myers, said “It was only right to state
The Mephan Ferguson Steel Pipe Co. should have credit
for the splendid way in which they had carried out their
contract for the supply of steel mains. The Council much
appreciated what the Company had done. Although the

“A sensation in the American market
recently was the offering of 5000 tons of basic pig
iron from China.” Truly the Yellow Man stirs,
and it isn’t a football stir.
A handful of State Frights in Sydney have
talked secesh from the Commonwealth to such an
extent that they have made the word altogether too
familiar. Harmless old Minister Lee was quite
flabbergasted recently to meet his own chief’s
spectre wandering about loose in northern N.S. W.
A deputation there wanted a railway or some-
thing, and it explained to Lee that if the article
wasn’t forthcoming New England might secede
from N.S.W. at any moment.

our shores its volume should steadily increase —Daily
Paper .
P'raps it would be as well to remove the party
with the piece of spouting first, so as to keep

What is infant life compared with the
dignity of a suburban alderman ? Recently Mavor
Hughes and the Sydney City Council composed a
piece of milk legislation under which the chalk-
and-water brigade—the old sin which kills
off young children wholesale with its nefarious
wares—would either have to abate the iniquity
of its ways or else argue the point in the
police court. It was proposed that the
milk legislation should apply to Sydney and
suburbs, but the suburban councils have first
to accept it, and the Local Rights alderman is as
mad about Sydney as the State Rights fool is
about Victoria and t Federal encroachments.”
And some of him shakes an empty head with an
air of great wisdom, and fancies that, as the new
idea comes from the City Council, it may be the
first instalment of a Greater Sydney movement
and if that came to pass, where would Alderman
Pumpkin be, and what room would be left for
Alderman Blowhard ?
“Sailor”: The new
Navigation Bill should
stipulate for a winter
load-line, especially for
those ships that run round
the South Coast. It is as
necessary as on the At-
lantic, where it is en-
forced. We get gales that
are as heavy and seas that
are as bad as anywhere in
the world. I speak of
these things from bitter
practical experience. The
late bad weather has been
very hard on us, and 3in.
or 4in. of additional free-
board would have added
greatly to our safety, to
say nothing of our com-
fort. Many of our colliers
are like half-tide rocks in
a heavy sea. We talk
largely of the way workers
are looked after here, but
our sailors—except in
the matter of wages and
hours are worse off than in England, where
accommodation and food are strictly supervised.
There, too, the old ships have to be brought up to
date m such things as ventilation and covering
I VUI ? P| aces - Here I have never seen the
matter touched upon. There are boats on this
coast 20 and 30 years old, that have had nothing
one to them since they first appeared in Aus-
tralian waters. Ihe new ones are not so bad,
naving been built to modern requirements.

.There is a good deal to be said for allowing
every candidate for the Senate or Reps, to use
the post-office to send one statement of his poli-
tics to every elector ; but the thing, if done at all
ought to be done openly. Nowadays the tricky
candidate gets there, or thereabouts, but he
crawls up the backstairs to do it, and the Common-
wealth shouldn’t encourage people to crawl up
the backstairs. Just after the Echuca (Vic.)
writ was issued, the Economist was registered at
Melbourne Gr.P.O. as a newspaper; and, under
cover of that registration, Echuca was flooded
with Mrs. Janet’s portrait, an Argos leading
article, and a whoop for Palmer at the rate o'!
20oz. for Id. Now that Palmer’s in, the Econo-
mist will die ; but while it lived it allowed of 1(1
electioneering circulars being sent out for Id.,
whereas if the circulars had been called plain
circulars, and not newspapers, they would have
cost a ha’penny a piece.
Moaning over the Factories and similar
Acts, the Argus, the other day, dished up the
decomposed tarradiddle about the “old and
slow worker, just as if it had discovered some
novelty which it had half a mind to patent.
Anyone who has not been dead the last few years
knows quite well that, in his annual reports for
1901 and 1902, Vic. Chief Inspector of Factories
Ord first doubted, and then denied, the existence
of this alleged aged and gradual worker. Then
in 1905 he openly challenged anyone to produce
an instance of any such person who had been in-
jured by factory legislation ; and not a single Tory
°l Anti-Soshite accepted that challenge. Can’t
the blessed paper invent some new lie, or turn up
Munchausen? There are a lot of people who
know the Argus’s ancestor by name, but have
never read him, and he would ’be something of a
change anyway.

After all, the tarradiddle that the Labor
party encourages irreligion and the bursting-up

“ Num ” : It is not necessary to go further
than Victoria to find instances of notes issued by
private banks that failed to materialise 20s. in the
£. When the. big smash of 1893 occurred, there were
one or two institutions which paid only 15s. or
17s. 6d. in the £, while others, like Jimmy
Munro’s unspeakable Federal Bank, succeeded in
meeting their note obligations only after theholders
of the paper had, in many instances, disposed of
it to speculators at a loss. Yet the S.M. Herald
doesn’t know of any case in which the note of a
private bank has proved worth less than its face
value in gold. Ye gods !
“ Dingo ” writes :
I notice that the Rev. J. B. Ronald—ex-Federal mem-
ber for Southern Melbourne—has been talking about the
want of gratitude” shown towards him hy the Vic
Labor Leagues, and about his “faithful work and endless
sacrifices ” on their account. Now, it's time that a word
was said on the other side. Rev. Ronald must not forget
that, the Labor Leagues first placed him in Parliament,
and that they cannot see, as plainly as he can the
faithful work and endless sacrifices.” They complain
(and somewhat justly) that as soon as he commenced to
draw his £4OO a year, etc., he took little further interest
m their movements, and almost entirely forsook their
meetings. Rev. J.B.R. does not mention why he
did not stand again for Southern Melbourne—the
seat he occupied up to last election. He must
have been aware that his chance of selection for Mel-
bourne Ports was poor when he stood against Mathews
who was eventually chosen, and who won the seat for
Labor. Mathews had given Sam Mauger a close run
three years before, and was well known right throuoh
the electorate, while Ronald was known in only a part of
it. And, though he found the Labor League rules good
enough for six years, he broke, when it suited him the
rule which prohibits any Laborite contesting a seat for
which another Laborite has been chosen to fio-ht Bv
breaking this rule, Ronald split the Liberal vote’with
Mathews; and it was only the immense strength of
Labor and Protection in this electorate, which 'polled
enough votes to carry Mathews in. So, when “grati-
tude ” is talked of, the boot is on the other foot.
Growler : I want land to make a living
on, so I am writing to ask The Bulletin to open
a subscription list for me, to, get funds to pay
my passage to England, from which place I
feel sure I would be able to settle on land
in Victoria, and might, perhaps, even get it
already growing crop. I have given up all
hope of getting any land while I remain in
V ictoria, but I see that the Bashed Govern-
ment is setting aside areas for settlers from
England, and I want to go to England so
that I can come from England and settle in
my own country.
State Attorney-General Wade, of N.S.W
assures the community, at great length, that there
is no need at all for Senator Neild’s Bill, allowing
for appeal from all parts of Australia to the High
Court in criminal cases ; the system already exist-
ing -in New South Wales being so perfect that an
Appeal Court is quite superfluous. Some people
when they consider such ghastly miscarriages of
justice as happened in the case of Suffield, have
about the perfection of the system in
■ro in i P ass * Wade’s point is that
it IN.S. VV. doesn t need an Appeal Court, then the
other States, which may need it, shouldn’t have
it. In fact if N.S.W. doesn’t require it, he
evidently holds that the rest of Australia shouldn’t
want it.
Thus “Red Tape, junior ” :
Another queer case under the Lands Acts has enn-P
before N.S.W. Full Court. In 1867 the VolunlX Land
Act was passed, and it provided that any person who
served five years with the Volunteers was entitled to
select 50 acres of any land thrown open to conditional
purchase Two brothers named Capel, in the New Eng-
Und d strict, held the necessary land order, and reached
out for 50 acres. The Land Board objected, and so did
the Land Appeal Court, and now the Full Court has
further sat on them, subject to review bv the Hhrh
Court. But the Full Court did not say the Capels were no
entitled to their 50 acres. It blew the brothers out
in a friendly judicial way. Twenty-five Land Acts have
been passed since 1867, said Darley, C. J., with the resmt
that he (Darley) has only a hazy idea if what'a wha
But one thing on which he staked his little bit was, that
the 25 Acts had quite forgotten the 1867 Volunteers’ and
their right to 50 acres was still intact. They were also
liable to ejectment from the 50 acres under certain cir
cumstances, about which the Court was none too clear'
The Loca Land Board had ejected them from this pa":
ticular allotment, though by the statute it had no power
to adjudicate on an application under a Volunteer
order. Still, the Full Court, being only aCourt of Anneal
could not decide whether the Board was right in decid’
ing whatit did decide, when decidedly it fad not the
right to decide anything at all. The Court, therefore
had no jurisdiction, nor did it have anything else tw
was likely to be of the slightest use to the Canels 1 It
couldn’t tell them what to do next, but it dismissed the
appeal with costs, and sort of died away in the distance
So two brothers are still clutching a Volunteer land ordeV
up in bleak New England,

The Australian Customs authorities have
decided not to enforce the Commerce Act regula-
tion—which requires foreign-made goods to bear
the name of the country of origin—in the case of
Chinese goods imported by Chinese for their own
use, and not for sale to Europeans. The idea
seems foolish. No doubt the Chow will enthu-
siastically declare that he isn’t going to sell his
imported stuff to Europeans, but the Chow is so
well-known as a law-breaker that he is always
open to suspicion. Anyhow, it is easier to enforce
the Commerce Act regulation than to discover
what becomes of the goods after they get out of
the hands of the Customs, and in any case it
wouldn t hurt the Chow importer to see his
countrymen’s brand upon his goods. A six
months return of the goods imported under this
Customs decision will be interesting; possibly
also the amount will be something 1 surprising.
As one of the small current devices for
baiting the Deakin Government, some member or
other has been rising every day to ask if that
forfeited bond for £25,000 in connection with the
ocean mail contract has been paid yet. This is a
sort of game in which, for very shame, N.S.W.
and Queensland members shouldn’t take a hand!
If the Deakin Government has been a fortnight
or three weeks trying to collect £25,000, the
JN.b.W. State Government has been 48 years try-
ing to collect £49,856 which Queensland owes it,
and hasn t smelt a halfpenny of the money yet,
so Cornstalk Reps, should remember that there
are other inferior debt-collectors in the world.
And if the defunct mail syndicate takes a month
to pay up—well, Queensland has already taken
576 months ; and Queensland Reps, should avoid
as much as possible any references to the painful
subject of paying up.
A humorous incident befell in the Tas-
manian House of Assembly, recently. The last
year’s policy of Premier Jack Evans consisted of
compulsory resumption of large estates, taxation
of land values, and reform of the Council. All
this policy the Council threw down the stairs
with a hefty thud, while the Opposition railed at
Jack and said that he had “arranged for it.”
At this Jack waxed virtuously and sonorously
indignant, and roared that sincerity was his strong
point. He was an absolute whale and complete sea-
serpent for sincerity. This year, Opposition leader
Nicholls openly accused Jack’s Government of
having sent a private letter to each of the Coun-
cillors who voted for throwing out its entire
reform policy, thanking him for the assistance he
had rendered to Ministers. Evans tried to deny
it, faltered, turned purple, and said, “Oh, ves!
The Attorney-General did that; not Ministers,”
whereat even the dullest of his own supporters
grinned. The Attorney is the Broken Prop, who
has been looking very much ashamed of himself
for some time. Now, apparently, ex-master
mariner Evans is also ashamed of him. The facts
indicate that the renowned circular was really a
Ministerial act, but the whole responsibility was
put on to Broken Prop, because he is too mild to
resist.

“ Wait till the clouds roll by, Joseph .”
Tommy Bent, in London :
The Commonwealth cannot give you one acre of land.
After all the high-falutin’ we have heard about immigra-
tion, it is only the States that can provide any land. The
States are sovereign States. They are not like the pro-
vinces of Canada. These sovereign States will insist on
working out their destiny in such matters in their own
way.
‘‘ The Commonwealth cannot give you one acre of
land ” —that’s just where Thomas talks through
his plug hat. When the Commonwealth Parlia-
ment gets a good progressive land-tax going it
will give the people land quickly enough—or it
will force the pampered land-monopolists of the
“sovereign States,” which are not, and never
were, sovereign, to let the people have land,
which amounts to the same thing. Also, if the
Commonwealth makes haste, it may, within a
year, have about 300,000,000 acres of its own land
in the Northern Territory to offer to immigrants
without asking any permission from the blatant
Premier of the smallest State on the Australian
continent.
THE SUNSHINE SPREADER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
lam the sunshine spreader—l spread mv gorgeous
plumes ;
I am the glory shedder in common people’s rooms.
Ornate in furs and feathers, with diamonds all ablaze,
’Mid thunder-threatened weathers I bring celestial days.
Among these wretched creatures with stately step I
tread ;
My rouged and powdered features are humbly worshipped.
I take a sudden header in Upper Class intrigue,
lam the sunshine spreader—l am the Ladies’ League.
lam the fair fatiguer—l dazzle day by day,
A silver-backed intriguer who brushes care away.
I talk to each good woman, however rough and rude,
Till she, who seems quite human, drops down with
gratitude.
By washing-tub and boiler, in accents silvery,
I lecture each she-moiler on home-life’s purity.
I am, my dears, the one shine that through existence
roams :
I spread my brilliant sunshine in coarse, plebeian homes.
I am the boon and blessing—no humble house I pass,
For Life is most distressing among the Lower Class.
In three-roomed huts and hovels, where pamphlets I
dispense,
Each ragged creature grovels at my munificence.
I preach the joys of weakness, the gospel of the stromr,
The purity and meekness that should to them belong.
With arrogant devotion, class dykes are piled by me
To stem the sweeping ocean of loud Democracy.
lam the sunshine spreader—l flash my gaudy prism ;
I am the angel shedder of Anti-Socialism.
Industrious promoter of Law and Loyalty,
I castigate the voter who has no time for me.
O’er them I cast our Boss spell, with feather-flaunting
grace,
That drudges learn the gospel of keeping in their place.
I am the glory shedder of Society intrigue—
I am the sunshine spreader—l am the Ladies’ League.
VIC.
LOUIS ESSON.

SHILLING BOOK
ROWLANDSON’S SUCCESS
Note what the Sydney Morning Herald says about it
“ ‘ Rowlandson’s Success ’ is really the third issue of ‘ The Commonwealth Annual,” and it has its new
name partly because it is published between seasons, and is therefore not technically an annual, and partly
because a discerning' critic prophesied of it that, if it turned out anything like its predecessors, it would be a
success. This prophesy bids fair to be fulfilled, and certainly the publisher has done all that in him lies to
deserve what proverbially no one can command. There are short stories by such well-known writers as
Steele Rudd, Thomas E. Spencer, H. M. Somer, Ernest Favenc, Charles White, T. B. Clegg, and Ambrose
Pratt, and by others not so well known as they probably will be, if we may judge from the specimens of their
work here, such as Mary E. Leith, 0. D. Websdale, Rhoda Reynolds, and Robert Allan. It is really note-
worthy when one comes to think of it —the number of Australians who can turn out readable stories. Verses
are supplied by, amongst others, Henry Lawson, Roderic Quinn, Louisa Lawson, Grant Hervey, and E. F.
Christie. Lionel Lindsay contributes many of the illustrations, but we have also work by Percy Spence,
A. J. Fischer, Stuart Allan, A. Collingridge, and others. The Rev. Jas. Green, who was special representative
of the Herald at the Indian Coronation Durbar, writes an interesting article on ‘The Holy Ganges.’ Mr.
William A. Crawley talks with much knowledge about ‘The Stage in Australia’; and there are capital papers
on ‘ Hina Sama,’ by Zaida Ben-Yusuf, and on ‘Malay Plays and Players,’ by A. G. Plate and T. Dee.
‘ Rowlandson’s Success ’ is a credit to the publisher, and to all concerned in its production.”
Price, 11-; post free, 1/5. N.S.W. Bookstall Co., 476 George Street, Sydney, and all branches.

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, July 30, 1907.
My Dear Arini,—
Is any hat worth 20, or 14, or 12 guineas? A
group of Melbourne milliners —a man and two
women assistants—who are in Sydney with their
new season’s hat-show, would chorus “Yes.”
And to a further query, “Do women pay such
prices?” the (frio would probably reply in an
octave higher: “Yes, else how could we three
afford to stop at one grand hotel and hold our
hat-exhibition at another—to say nothing of
passages for three humans and several hundred
hats, and the leaving of our Melbourne business
for a week or more?” Personally speaking I have
not met a woman who would go as high as
ten guineas for a hat. Some of the smartest and
most artistic dressers in Sydney assert that not if
they had twice Miss Eadith Walker’s money
would they pay more than five guineas for a hat.
Then they qualify the absoluteness of the remark
with—“ For something unique I might be
tempted to seven guineas.” And there seems
then to transpire a claw or two to indicate that
there is a cat in the bag. For something a
shade better than the hat called unique, presum-
ably, they would pay the ten guineas.
In some obscure (woman’s) paper—the Home
Cat possibly—l read that some women will pay
twenty guineas for a hat if.the seller mentions the
price in the hearing of several other women. The
stare with which the buyer and her purchase are
favored has much the same effect on her as enthusi-
astic applause on a public performer.
The Melbourne millinery invasion was an amus-
ing circumstance, from one minor point of view. In
■every unknown woman who went to look at the
models the principals saw a Sydney milliner or
■one of her assistants come to copy them. I was
meditatively gazing at a little white chip-straw
thing, with an enormous blue-Alsatian bow (about
seven yards of wide ribbon, possibly). It was
just the straw, the ribbon, and (I suppose) the
style, and the price of the lot wits four guineas. I
suppose the raw material must have cost more
than 6d. The male petal of the trinity balanced
up to me, smiling the smile of the successful and
expensive dancing-master, and asked “if Madame
had made a selection yet.” He had the face, head
and shoulders of a man who would be of great
value to his side in a football scrum. I had
been in about 30 seconds, and said I hadn’t made
a selection. One garden of hats was all-blue.
Different shades of blue —Nattier, periwinkle,
powder, and those tones you see about —the blue
delphiniums. The trade has specialised them
with cunning names that none but the trade re-
members. And there were purple straws, green
straws, and luxurious hats of black crin, ostrich
feathers standing at attention, so to speak, and
plumes that drooped over the back of the hat and
trailed on to wearer’s neck and shoulder bones;
brick-red, green, and brown feathers in con-
spiracy ; hats that dipped in the brim; and
hats that soared from the rims. One model
was covered with many-colored acacia sprays.
Another was massed with sweet pea.
But the proprietor, bidding a significant and
persistent “Good morning” to a girl who was
hat-gazing, was the choicest exhibit. She is a
very rich Sydney girl, and was finely frocked and
furred. Perhap he mistook her for a milliner ;
perhaps his eighth sense (the born and trained
trader has an eighth sense) told him she was not
a buyer ; anyway, the inflections of his reiterated
“Good mornings,” the decision in his bowings
as he manoeuvred the heiress towards the door,
proved him to be a Napoleon in the art of getting
rid of the Woman who Wouldn’t.
In another ten seconds he had got rid of me
with the like firmness and tenderness.
No matter how hot the coming summer, Sydney-
sidersmay taketheir pleasure iced. The Glaciarium
—an artificially-frozen lake—has been made on
the site of the old Cyclorama, fairly close to Syd-
ney Railway Station. It was officially opened on
Thursday by State Premier Carruthers with a
few words—not an ice-axe—in the presence of
a crowd that was frocked as if for a swell
race meeting. It was the most comprehen-
sively Somebodyish and Anybodyish gathering
of 2000 persons that ever said “Yes” to
an invitation. It included some six or seven
Melbourne girl skaters who had been taught
by the management to cut capers on the
ice, as light and swift as if their skates had wings.
Sydney is mostly mistaken on the question of the
status of these ice-maidens. It thinks they are
paid, professional exponents of cutting ice, and
that they chase the glowing hours with flying
feet at so much per chased hour. But they
merely chanced to come to this smiling State at
the time when the frost set in, and rushed into
the frozen breach like the ardent little
sportswomen they are—for pure love of it.
After the Melbournians had done ringtailed
wonders, some of “those present” put on the
skates. Captain Leslie Wilson and his sister
showed by their, skill that they and ice-skating
are well acquainted. The morning after the
opening, Miss Rawson and several girl friends
went up to the Glaciarium and had a fly round.
A returned Australienne says the Glaciarium is
quite as fine as Princes Court in London. Skating
there, women mostly wear short accordeon-pleated
skirts and blouses.
Blanche Arral, with her wonderful dramatic
soprano and her songbird eyes, will brighten
and enlighten an audience at Sydney Town
Hall next Saturday night with grand opera-
tic selections. The famous “ Shadow Song ”
from “Dinorah,” and the entire mad
scene from “Hamlet,” will be included in
her programme. The brilliant foreigner de-
serves the support of all song-with-style lovers.
Apart from her natural and highly-trained gifts,
the Arral has an abiding personal charm,
and she’s one of the few women who
shake hands in a way that makes you want
to say, “Do it again.” It’s subtle' flattery.
A charming plump face smiles at you, and a hand
which is soft and round and caressing nestles in
your own for a fraction of a minute, and you feel
pleased all over. That’s the Arral shake. Some
otherwise charming women place in your paw a
flabby, cold bit of fish ; othex-s scratch at you with
a nervous sharp claw ; others wrestle with your
hand in Hackenschmidt style. The Blanche
Arral hand-shake suggests a gentle and dainty
compliment; and it’s the typical hand-shake of
the educated Frenchwoman. I don’t know whether
they have shake classes in their schools, but I
think the art must be taught very young.
The just bankrupt de Trafford baronet is elder
brother of the O. E. de Trafford, who visited this
hemisphere with a very smart spouse and a
cricket XI last summer, and while in Sydney put
up at “ Cranbrook.” The reckless one, who owns
to having spent half-a-million on racehorses, has
been famed for years as the possessor of the best
lot of heavy-weight polo ponies and one of the
prettiest wives in London. Why go bankrupt
buying things for mere racehorses when you can
do the job just as neatly buying other things for
a pretty wife? This to any well-x’egulated female
mind must ever remain a dark and insoluble
puzzle.
Roberto Hazon is going away in. two months
time, and Sydney is beginning to ponder as to who
can fill the shoes of melody which will thus be
vacated. By the way, you should see his material
shoes ! Awe-stricken observers say that they are
not made, and, of course, they can’t be born, so
all that can be said is that they just are. One
theory is that they are raised from seed,
A committee of Mosman ladies has arranged
for a conversazione to Dr. and Mrs. Arthur, in
the Mosman Town Hall, on the afternoon of the
13th of August. Mrs. Mayoress (of Mosman)
Leahy, Mrs. Moseley, of Neutral Bay, and Mrs.
D. H. Valentine, of Mosman, are of the execu-
tive. Last-mentioned lady is hon. sec. Admir-
ing supporters of the strenuous, patriotic Richard
Arthur and his clever and well-liked missus will
no doubt come in squadrons to tell Him and Her
that He will be hoisted to the top of the poll.
The Hospice Dance, at the Paddington Town
Hall, was such a phenomenal success—only a
dozen or so souls short of 500 were present —that
only the slow procession of the years will wear the
triumphant smile off the faces of Mrs. Allen
Taylor and Mrs. M. Deery and others of the
executive. Ex. Rawson and daughter were so
pleased with the frisk that he wanted to know why
he hadn’t been asked to all the previous Hospice
hops. And the flattered committee fell down
before him and said “ O king, live for ever,” which
he immediately did.
Arthur Mason, who sat in Wiegand’s capacious
seat at the Sydney organ for six years, has resigned,
immediately after re-appointment, and goes to
London as the representative of the Sydney
Morning Herald. Crowds rolled up to a fare-
well dinner at Baumann’s, when local musicians,
presided over by Signor Hazon, spoke strongly of
Arthur’s ability and energy. The organist was
at one time a clerk in the Education Department,
but there is a suspicion that he was a very poor
clerk, owing to the music that buzzed unceasingly
in his brain. The departing one gave several
organ recitals of a farewell nature to crowded
houses, and the Mayor assumed the perpendicular
at a meeting in the Town Hall, and handed over
a gold watch in the presence of many citizens.
Editor Ward, of Sydney Telegraph, has had a
bad tinje through an ear trouble. The drum of
one of his ears was shifted, the cause of the
trouble removed, and the drum restored. He is
now as good as new.
Mrs. Aliek Gordon (Margaret Thomas) is in a
private hospital. So is Mrs. Deery—Aggie
Thorne. But, for that matter, so are a crowd
of other well-known Sydney-siders, both men
and women. Influenza or appendicitis are
mostly the causes. Airs. Amy Sherwin has the
flu, but she has so far kept out of hospital.
A sapling of one of the families who have made
Maoriland history is in Sydney, and enthusing
spontaneously over the magic of our blue-and-
honey-colored winter, as he rushes through it in
his own gasolene cart. Mr. Percy Buller is at the
Bar in Wellington. All Australasians who have
walked hand in hand with Culture will remember
the writings of his father, the late Walter Buller.
Mrs. George Tallis’ latest and third package
left by the Stork arrived on a Sunday, and, partly
on that account, and partly through the success
of Miss Brune’s “Sunday,” the small girl has
been named after the Sabbath. Many girl babies
are now named after the months of their birth—
especially since Elizabeth of the German Garden
let it be known that she had infants called June
and April. But the days of the week are another
matter. They are not melodious, and suggest
washing, or fasting, or shop shutting. Sunday is
the best of them in the matter of euphony. But
it would be a gloomy thing to grow up and enter
society as Miss Friday Drinkwater.
Southwell and Sykes were having some causerie
at Paling’s. Former (who had just returned from
ALL.): “While I was at Rotorua a Maori girl
fell into one of the springs where her tribe was
boiling potatoes and doing other cooking. She
fell in up to her armpits ! I forget whether
I fainted or buried my head in a sand-drift,
but the next thing I clearly remepiber is her
people wailing around the boiled girl after they
had pulled her out. And, after that, her remark,
‘Don’t weep for me—l am not going to die.’
And she didn’t die. She is recovering.” “It’s
the staying power of the savage,” responded
pianist Sykes, as he patted his lily-of-the-valley
buttonhole. “When I was in England, and
watching the football contest between the All
Blacks and the Englishmen, I saw, and heard, the
collision between the heads of two players. One
was Warbrick, the half-caste Maorilander—a
cousin of Maggie Papakura, and later a guide
at Rotorua. Well, the two men’s heads met like
those of two colliding bulls, and the sound of the
impact turned the people white and sick. It
could be heard—anywhere ! The Englishman
went down like an axed ox, but the Maorilander
stood and looked apologetically at him as he was
carried unconscious from the field, and scratched
his own head as if a canary had tickled him.”
Mrs. Harry Lassetter will be on the outward-
bound list this year. She takes her son to school,
and will live in England while his young idea is
being trained on British principles.
A chummish contributor :
Forty members of a well-known yachting club resolved
the other evening to honor a fellow member, and for that
purpose arranged a dinner. Man’s idea of honoring his
fellow-man lies in the over-feeding of him. On the even-
ing of the dinner the ’phones were hard at it for an hour
or more telling the feeders that the function was off, as
he whom they delighted to honor was dead ! Most of
them wrote messages of condolence to the mourning
relatives, and sat down with their wives to the roast
mutton. But a mistake had been made. The first man
at the other end of telephone number one had received a
message that Mr. Guest would not be able to attend as he
was in bed. That was all ;he isn’t dead yet. A large,
bowl-like ear is sometimes an advantage. It collects
sound well.
Mr. Bertie Pilcher, barrister-at-law and elder
son of the K. 0., has abandoned the pursuit of the
elusive brief in this city, and left with his pretty
wife for Tasmania. In that blest isle (any isle
which is rather dead is understood on the “ de
mortuis ” principle to be blest) he proposes to
start farming and forget such profitless affairs as
Probates and other perks of the junior Bar.
Farming is, in the abstract, an idyllic pursuit
whereat large cheques are pouched monthly by
the simple process of sitting upon a rose-festooned
verandah or fence or other place of the sort, and
narrowly watching the kine browse upon the
mead, and the young crops shoot automatically
through the soil, and the young lamb skip on the
elevation. Hence it appeals to the weary urban
mind with much force. In sooth, it’s a pity it
doesn’t appeal more. There are enough youthful
barristers making the letter “O” per annum at
the overworked law-game in this State to people a
fair-sized agricultural college.
“Miss Lancashire, Limited,” will have the
Rawson Excellencies to her farce with music and
clog-dancing, on the opening at the Palace on
Saturday. Miss Florence Baines, who plays the
name rdle, brought letters of introduction to the
Rawsons. She is a large, golden-haired, and
infantile-complexioned lady, who knows her Lan-
cashire and its clog-wearing, factory-haunting
inhabitants from the pavement up. Singing and
dialect story-telling at various vaudeville houses
in London have been our visitor’s occupation for
some years. Her stories are got first-hand from
the originators. No matter how wretchedly a
Lancashire corpse has lived, it must be buried
with all the pomp and circumstance that its family
can afford. “ The fun’ral was done very poor—
very poor indeed. Not a bite o’ ’am ! My mother
buried four, and all with ’am,” one of Miss
Baines’ townsfolk told her.
One of Miss Baines’ company (it arrives by the
Runic) is Mr. Patrick Evans, who was Du
Maurier’s original of Little Billee. He has altered
scarcely at all, Miss Baines says, from the well-
dressed little dandy with the delicately curved
nose, who fooled and suffered so fascinatingly
through the pages of “Trilby.” Little Billee
plays like a magician on a one-stringed viola,
aged 200 years.
To Miss Baines the late Phil May once ex-
plained the kind of funeral he yearned for —for
himself. The artist and his wife and Miss
Baines and her husband were prowling about
London the night of the late Queen’s funeral, and
May, after decrying dismal processions, declared
that his should be led by a full band playing lively
airs. The bandsmen were to be dressed in red.
Twenty-four ballet girls would follow in short
frocks of scarlet. There would be another band and
another contingent of dancing ballet girls, and
then the hearse. “And me,” continued Phil,
“ sitting up in the coffin smoking a good cigar.”
The new entertainer would have brought out
Miss Kate Vesey—Emily Soldene’s niece—but
the girl’s mother did not smile upon the idea of
the separation.
Mrs. Masseran will materialise at the Hotel
Metropole from the seventh to the fifteenth of
next month. Her intentions are strictly artistic.
She will elongate the dumpy, and etherealise the
adipose, and give contours to the bony, and
generally bring the Sydney figure up to date,
according to the new fashions.
Mr. Robertson Clark, one of the ornaments of
the State Government House, has got back to
Sydney after a trip to Japan.
A Sydneysider, wandering amongst the amber-
and-coffee-skinned races, writes me that at Singa-
pore he ran across a recent visitor of ours—
Biscboff Van Ryck, the man who used to drive
such ripping black horses about Sydney. He was
seeing the King of Siam off to visit a brother
potentate, Edward of England. Van Ryck, who
intends to be back here in October, is an honorary
A.D.C. to the gentleman who reigns over the
country where the blue-eyed cats come from. As
the monarch had engaged the whole of the first-
class accommodation of a huge German mail
steamer, he evidently didn’t run short of staff.
He must have sold an awful lot of eats and white
elephants to raise the money for a trip on that
scale of magnificence. A learned person informs
me that the name of the potentate is Somdetch
Phra Paramindr Maha, but he seems to reign
under the alias of Chulalongkorn I. I hope he
will come back feeling better.
My Brisbane correspondent writes:—
The Chelmsford family has been busy of late, and has
sundry jobs still on hand. On Monday morning those of the
committee of the Exhibition of Women’s Work who were
not down with the flu assembled at Government House,
and after an hour’s good work had their pictures taken.
In the afternoon Lady Chelmsford presided at her third
hospital meeting in six days. That night her lord and
master accompanied her to the thinly patronised Exhi-
bition Hall, and together they handed out the Trinity
College prizes and certificates. Tuesday saw a dinner
party, Wednesday the opening of Parliament, and on
Thursday they gadded off to say the usual nice things at
the Laidley Show. Friday evening saw them at the
Charity Organisation Annual, and Saturday the worn vice-
regal male shadow viewed the final wiping out of Queens-
land by the All Blacks.
This week will include visits to the Deaf, Blind, and
Dumb Institute, Royal Geographical and Cruelty to
Animals meetings, the opening of the Art Societj’s show,
and then the Toowoomba Carnival visit. After that the
fainting wreck of a frail Governor will probably be put to
bed.
Parliament was opened by commission on Tuesday, and
the few womenfolk interested in matters political were
rewarded by a good deal of fun. The Opposition nominee
for the Speaker’s chair had his character read with a
frankness bordering upon intimacy, and the Govern-
ment’s pet fared no better. This searching inquiry was
spared the Labor aspirant—he was merely voted out
right away, and then a conference took place at the table
between Premier and Opposition chief, and a hungering
multitude went to lunch. Speculation ran riot as to the
Speaker to be, and Mesdames Bell and Macartney both
had a following who were ready to lay odds on their hus-
bands’chances. However, when the House reassembled
at 4 o’clock, burly John Leahy, Philp’s erstwhile bos’n,
had a walk-over, and assumed the lace tie and the added
dignity.
Next day, with the usual crowd of battling females
scrummaging for a seat, his Ex. appeared in full Windsor
uniform and read the speech. Of the women on the floor
of the sacred chamber, none looked smarter than Home
Secretary Hawthorne’s bouncing missus, in a heliotrope
silk, the top half exposing a waist of fancy point d’esprit,
and a top-storey picture erection of violet velvet, plumes,
and some wonderful old lace. Subsequent happenings
make it look as if the present Home Sec. will have but a
brief career, and, if this is so, it’s a shame, for both
husband and wife look the position to a nicety—-also, the
new landeau is a revelation.
The Nelson-Bird wedding will take t>lace early in
October.
Feminine interest is at present centred upon the new
A.D.G., Captain Verney, who, in a uniform that is a poem,
looks like a twin brother of the loved and lost Harman.
From “Rosna,” in Adelaide :
The Misses Boothby, who guide the little May Club the
way it should go, struck a shining novelty in their recent
Lavender and Old Lace ” afternoon. After the weariness
of the Novel Tea and Bird or Beast-Guessing Com-
petition, and the other worn-out events with which the
average hostess vexes the dull ear of her drowsy guest,
the thing was a joyous relief. The promoters scoured
the country in search of any stray persons who might be
concealing old lace about their persons, and altogether a
most costly collection was exhibited. There’s a nice
came-over-with-the-Conqueror and handed-down-for-
generatio-is appearance about the possession of old lace.
The exhibits were mounted on lavender paper, and hung
round the walls of the club rooms. A real live Devon-
shire lace-maker, Miss Hammelt, who happened to be in
Adelaide, was unearthed to give an exhibition of pillow
lace-making ; and the same authority—a demoiselle still in
the long-haired, short-frocked stage—wrote an excellent
little paper on the strange old legends connected with the
earliest patterns of lace. This was read to the assembled
horde by Miss Winifred Scott. I extract one gem—a
quotation from Fuller, who was evidently a wise old
party. “Let it not be condemned for wearing because
it doth neither heat nor hide, seeing it doth adorn.”
Her Ex., accompanied by Miss Clowes, graced the occa-
sion ; also Mrs. Way (who lent some gorgeous lace), Mrs..
Herbert Rymill, Mrs. Bray and her daughter, Mrs..
Duncan, and flocks and herds of other influential folk.
Bargain sales and dances punctuate this landscape, and
all the shes of the city rend each other’s garments by
day and characters by night in the fight for a yard of
ribbon and two yards of man. The exalted and be-shekeled
population rope in all the brass-buttoned young warriors
from the Challengah for their entertainments, and last
week the warrior- retaliated with a return dance or hop
or leap on their gallant tub. Special trains and other
kindly attentions were provided for the guests, and a
double line of bluejackets handed the land-lubbers up
the gangway, and saw that nobody fell into the salt
drink. Supper was a kind of eternal affair, and the
guests wandered in at their own sweet will just as the
spirit moved them. Also, they were all supplied with
hot soup before being sent home through the dank and
dreary midnight.
Elsie Jones, one of our numerous young nightin-
gales, returned to us by the Moldavia on Saturday. She
is a daughter of Mr. T. H. Jones, Mus. Bac, who teaches
the young skylark of the Conservatorium. Also her
mother was one of our finest dramatic sopranos of a few
years back. Miss Jones seems to have captured a good
many leaves from the laurel tree. Of late she has been
touring America and the provinces of Britain with much
success. She is about the first of our little exported
colony of singers to return to us, and, therefore, we are
falling upon her neck by relays, the neck not being a tenth
large enough to hold all of us who want to arrive there.
When the last of us have got off her neck she will have
time to sing us something, we hope.
News that South Oss rifleman, Walter Addison, won
the King’s prize for rifle-shooting at Bisley, set Adelaide
crowing pretty loudly last week. Welivein astateofper-
petual crow now-a-days(hence the term “crow-eater”).
Lieutenant Addison is a son of A. R. Addison, of the
State Legislature, and a native of Orroroo, which town
promptly went en fete at his success. It may not be a
very large place, but it shouted loud enough for Rome
and Babylon combined.
Yours Affectionately,
AKENEHI.
Chiozza Money, English Freetrade M.P.,
proves by pretty conclusive reasoning (in current
Contemporary Review) that 95 per cent, of the
entire wealth of the United Kingdom is owned by
about one-ninth of its population. And Splosh,
M.P., is one of those who assails Protection
because it “tends to create a monopoly of wealth ”!
If the Dalgety (Australia) site for the
Federal capital is defeated, then Australia will
have to thank the York-street Fatman, the State
Frights party, the Daily Telegraph, the Even-
ing News, G. Reid, Carruthers, and—J. C.
Watson. How does the Federal Labor leader
like being in that galley ?
Beauty. —There is no topic interests women more
and no possession they prize more highly than beauty. It
has been so through all the ages, and while human nature
remains unaltered will still he so. Hence the Valaze
Institute established by Mile. Helena Rubinstein here in
Sydney caters to a distinct want—and caters thoroughly.
All the advances in beauty culture made by the great
Complexion Salons of Paris and Vienna are practised at
the Institute, and there is scarce a blemish that mars the
human face divine but Mile. Rubinstein has a treatment
to successfully remedy it. Red noses, double chins,
superfluous hairs, flabbiness, wrinkles, crowsfeet, are but
a few of the unlovelinesses that are permanently removed,
and even the eradication of birth marks and such like
serious blemishes are successfully treated. Ladies should
call into the Valazelnstitute when in town—even a sitting
of face massage had there will result in benefit to one’s
looks. The address is 158 Pitt Street, on the second floor.
Ask to see Mile. Rubinstein herself.*
There is at present on a two months’
holiday visit to Brisbane Lieutenant W. P. Drury,
R.N., a member of the well-known Queensland
Drury family, who. since joining the Orlando in
Melbourne as a cadet 18 years ago, has seen a bit
of fighting, and has carved out a career for him-
self. Besides being a naval lieutenant, he is a
lieutenant-colonel in the Egyptian army, and was
for a time Governor of Fashoda. He was also in
control of Port Soudan, the terminus of the Nile-
Red Sea railway, during the construction of that
line. He is another instance of the Australian
who, going abroad, is valued at his worth, but
who, if he had remained at home, would have had
to play twenty-second fiddle to the imported
genius.
The result of the judging in the “Australia
To-Day £25 Cover Competition ” is now announced.
Many of the large number of competitors showed con-
siderable skill and merit. The winner turned up in
“ Ecnav,” which was, found to represent A. Vance Steel
and R. F. Steel, of The Block, Elizabeth-street, Mel-
bourne. People who are anxious to see the winning
design will take train to Melbourne, and tram or cab tc
the Metropolitan Gas Co.’s building, and then the lift
will land them at the top floor in which the office of the
Australasian Traveller is situated, far from the mad-
ding crowd’s ignoble yell.
For about the fiftieth time it is declared
by the Imperial War Lords that T. Atkins is
about to be armed with the best rifle in the world.
Curiously enough, there are at present in London
three Australian inventors trying to deal with
the Imperial War people. Two of the inventors
have a patent range-finder, for increasing the
accuracy of heavy-gun shooting, and the third is
offering a new style of rifle, with which it seems
almost impossible to miss. All three have
reported that they have received offers from the
Japanese and the Germans, and will entertain
them if the British Government doesn’t avail
itself of the first chance to buy the secret. The
“Victorian factories” produce some wonderful
men ! (Presumably they are from Victorian fac-
tories —as the Sydney press never mentions the
factories of N.S. W. or any other State.)

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, July 29, 1907.
Mr Dear Yarrie,—
The Australian girl has her charms chanted
by her brethren, her sisters even, and mere
acquaintances. But her brother is scarcely ever
mentioned. After seeing a number of Him
under favorable circumstances, I think he, too,
should have a pagan of praise. The other
evening, I viewed a masculine reunion for
a few minutes. It was in honor of some expert
amateur athlete, and about 40 cheerful allies
turned up to do him honor. All were in evening-
dress, close shaven, and cleanly shining. They
seemed as happy as children; and some were
beautiful, like faery princelings (aged cynics, and
ugly young ones may sneer, but it’s true !). And
the collection of black-and-white figures round a
sparkling dinner-table was as ornamental as a
fringe of arum lilies round a moonlit pool. They
showed a most interesting diversity of type, and
a most gratifying evenness of general attractive-
ness. There were tall boys with broad shoulders
and brave faces, and small boys with round faces
and sharp tongues. There were boys, black and
sinister as the historic Black Douglas whose race
went down in the end before the foxy red Douglas,
but with smiles like summer sunshine ; and there
were boys with Celtic phantasy written on their
foreheads and local catch-words on their lips.
And there were boys who bore no maps of ancient
countries upon them, and looked real Australian
products. Some looked like jockevs, some
like plough-boys or bank-clerks, and none
looked fools, and all looked virile and
wholesome. One was like a wood-fawn
in a starched shirt; one needed only a goatskin
drape, a reed, and an inspiration to play the role
of sweet Theocritus. And one looked horribly
like George Reid. But they couldn’t all be
beautiful.
Just now a promising doctorling is realising the
seriousness of sins of omission. The youth who
has been a resident in a hospital, wants promo-
tion. His medical qualifications gave him a fair
show, but his limited capacity for amoretting
seems to have put him out of the running A
ward sister reported him for “chucking” proba-
tioner nurses under their dear little chins, or
something of the sort, and now a sub-committee
is appointed to consider his misdemeanors. It is
notable that none of the kissed ones charge the
gay caresser. They seldom do! It is the un-
kissed sister who takes exception to the little
gallantries which the other girls enjoy.
At Melbourne Mansions, David Symes’s ela-
borate flattery, the flatters are subject to as many
restnctions as cheapboardersina religious famly.
Ihe gilded bachelors and grieving grass-widows
were lately warned that they must indulge no
more in vocal exercises and piano-thumping
before the brisk hour of 8 a.m., nor after the
giddy hour of II pm. And on Sundays secular
music is barred. They can’t even sing “There
was I, waiting at the church,” or “ Will de congre-
gation please stand up ? ”
Prof. Laurie lectured prettily upon “The
Meaning of Art to the Trinity Women*s Literary
t >M aSt i Tu f S u- ay ’ i And the learned don said
very little about his subject, and gave the igno-
minious lay-worn an the impression that he was
talking down to the corseted sex from an
immensely high altitude. By the way, the Trinity
girls commence to justify the unstaid phrase I’ve
used. I hey are no longer the greenery-yallery
gals of yester-year, with dank fringes and pre-
Kaphaehte contours of face and figure. They are
developing into as comely a coterie as a comic-
opera chorus, with pneumonia blouses and Gibson
bangs under then- gowns and mortar-boards.
After the Professor had concluded, Dame Archer
presided over a neat supper in the Hostel dining-
room. Altogether Trinity Hostel festivities go
way towards reconciling one to the
Learned Girl. Her digestion is good, and she
