In a hushed whisper comes the news of a blas<s
Sydney bachelor s engagement to an Australian
stage charmer in Fogtown. For years the wary one
paid nicely-considered attention to all the comely
dames and damsels of his acquaintance, though
1 f l i t Q e r fcain S d i a specml adoration for the lime-
iight lass. But it was a tentative form of worship
until the damsel got up steam for London and his
mother rushed into matrimony. One departure
or the other made him ill, and then sent him
scurrying over the ocean wave after health and
his touring happiness. Now he’s caught it, and
returns presently with his bride.
Miss Eugenia Stone has been the guest of the
ex-Senatonal A. P. Matheson clan at Maidenhead,
where the Westralians live in a quaint bungalow
what time they’re not gadding about the Scotch
end of England. Sundry smart little suppers and
other varieties of spree were given in the lady’s
honor, and congratulations and compliments were
the order of her hour.
“Tryphena” from London:
The pair of Denmark royalties have added to the pomp
and circumstance of the festive June. At Covent
Garden Opera a royal equerry dropped dead, and
was solemnly carried out as' the double flank of
Maiesties, ignorant of the tragedy, gaily processed in. The
show of neck and gem and gold braid was more absorbing
than the execution of Caruso’s opulent top C. And at
Olympia, the most superb mob of thoroughbreds that
ever four-stepped on the green globe were of second
importance to the pageant presented of four crowned
heads and a collection of half and quarter crowns, with a
gross of small change in the form of equerries and
guardians of the Royal Shoelace and attendants of the
Grand Sneeze.
But the Crowned Heads min us the great world’s grovel
or the colossal Bodyguard would be very humdrum,
ordinary mortals. King Ned, now looking ten years
ahead of the camera editions, and more sleek of beard and
thinner in the jowl than I imagined him, is simple in
manner and just German enough in speech to suggest a
common or garden foreigner of long residence in this
land. His gentle spouse is an artistic person with Age
strenuously discountenanced, and a long look of sweet
resigned patience (as is often the attribute of the deaf)
in her faintly colored face. “There are, after all,” says
Oscar Wilde, “ but two types of woman—the plain and
the colored.” The Queen is the colored.
t The Denmark couple are long and grave and elderly.
No dolly-dye about the she-rojalty—her thatch is
frankly grey. So is her rather wistful old face. The
King of Denmark reminds me of the Talbottle. He
doesn’t resemble his Royal sister of this throne in the
least.
. Tommy Bent has been accorded a dinner and recep-
tion by some officials and others at the Trocadero. Lady
Brassev, whitened and thinned by the intervening years,
was there with her husband, the Faller-off. He looked
astonishingly spry. Falling-off must be a healthy habit.
The Sydenham Clarkes were also there. Lady Clarke’s
pleasure at meeting' Toorak and Collins-street East again
bore a genuine ring. Of the footlighting chick Dame
Clarke was able to chat with a glad smile. Countess
Darnley had got her elongated self up in flowering silk
(popular ice-cream tones of pink and white) with lots of
diamonds frescoing her tall top. I noticed also Mrs.
Robert Harper, Mrs. Dunbar-Hooper, Mrs. Henry Butler,
Mrs. Spencer Brunton, Mrs. J. Tait, and Mrs. E. Keep.
At that sort of show you must come late and withdraw
earty, if you would be reckoned rushed and in strong
request. To dally is to write yourself down a social frost.
A snug little club in this noisy, nerve-racking centre is
that of the Women Writers. It’s just off the Strand, a
cosily-furnished establishment. On the committee is one
Melbourne girl—Miss Quinlan, daughter of the Victorian
judge, who died nearly two decades ago. Miss Quinlan
drifted to the Big Smoke several years since, arid is
working a paying literary reef on the magazines here.
She’s an aesthetic blonde with a most charming manner,
and an eye bright with imagination’s fire. All the Vic-
torians are emploiing a profitable “ J ” nib hereabouts.
Miss Elms looks bright and keeps busy. Sophie Osmond
is turning out with mechanical regularitv the Dook
and Governess brand of romance, and lurid tales of high
life, and hyperbolical yarns of Counts and factory belles
for the popular fireside journals. She laughs good-
naturedly over the ease with which her money is earned.
She knows she can do far better things, but wonders if
she should let go her hold of the tail of the devil she knows
when the tail of the devil she doesn’t know mav be too
slippery to be grasped. Some devils (alas!) wear their
tails buttered.
For dress styles here you don’t go to Ascot, or Covent
Garden, or the Savoy. You look to the stage. In every
show—from the classy comedy and musical farce to the
second-rate halls—the garbing of the women is an
immense factor. I’ve seen seventeen shows of all kinds,
and the superb dresses on each stage have dazzled me no
end. “The Merry Widow” alone has dozens of the
noblest fashion-plates.
The clever Pounds children (at present the
smaller. one is really not more than an ounce) have
a standing engagement for an item or two in the
short, sharp programme of the Chicago tea-gossip-
and-music frivols o’ Tuesdays. They shine
together in mimicry of scraps from Harcourt
Beatty and Nellie Stewart plays, and they have
the voices to the veriest shade.
West, of the moustaches, and his Brescians and
moving pictures have great houses here every
night. It’s such a fascinating programme for
Edwin and Angelina. There is the congenial
darkness of the pictures and the encouraging
sentimentalism of the musical interludes. Ange-
lina finds it a good hot-house in which to en-
courage the growth of Love’s young dream.
The usually sedate Joe Gunn—clever private
secretary to the Eairbairn Brothers—is turning
hand-springs just now in celebration of his newly-
made matrimonial engagement. The lady in his
case is Miss Renecke—the smart “ Girl from
Jay’s,” who came out for the frilliest Melbourne
froeking store, to tell them just how they set a
plume in a London hat. Evidently she set the
feather in her own hat with killing effect.
Widow Braham is cutting her year of mourning
off with a sharp snip on exactly its last day. The
memory of her former husband was much too
tragic to be treasured. 80 she weds a venerable
and wealthy suitor, who carefully nurses a shadow
of departed youth.
James Nevin Tait, of the numerous Tait
family, has entered into the preliminary of
matrimonial bondage, with Miss Bess Norris—
the black-eyed little painter of miniatures who
has made some name in London.
After a number of false starts, Doris Madden—
the serious young cousin of the vice-regals—made
a belated departure on last week’s mail boat. It
seems that the musical young lady of the studious
spectacles has finally decided to be a pupil of
Carreno when that artiste returns to Berlin.
Goathlands—one time the festive abode of the
McEacharns—has been turned by Drs. Spring-
thorpe and Maudsley into a private hospital
for nervy, brain-strain folk.
There is a mild clawing in Kew about the in-
vitations for the recent dance of the matrons.
There were nearly a dozen matron hostesses, and
the ball circ’ed in the Recreation Hall (which
has the smoothest floor and the most cosy-cornery
gardens in Melbourne). Although the there*
abouts offer much scope for the wander-
ing moon-gazer and the sitter-out, the hall
itself is very small, and each hostess had
her invitation list considerably chopped up.
Many scores of names had perforce to be
dropped into the waters of oblivion, and the
resultant wails and scratchings are turning the
district into a furious cattery. The question of
how many cats make five is in a fair way towards
solution. The dance, by the way, was largely
regimental and gold-laced. Mrs. Commander
lickell led the Dames, of whom pretty little Mrs.
Geoffrey (Age) Syme was the most youthful.
Talking of dances—the Pinschof daughters make
ready for a big frivol at Studley Hall in the near
anon.
Lieutenant Stephens, one of the most adored of
the officahs of the Katoomba during a fleet visit a
year or so ago, is coming back again. He is to be
stationed in Australia for some time on the
Pyramus. Unfortunately the giddy young man
is attached—firmly attached, and to a widow !
Pretty, buxom widow Buscombe of Westralia.
By the way, three of the Katoomba officers col-
lected Australian girls to share their joys and
sorrows and revenue and breakfast chop. Jolly
tan-faced Lieutenant Glasspoole annexed the
daughter of the Toorak McNealeys, and Surgeon
Lavertine married Ethel Gunn, the handsome
and petted beauty girl adored as lead in most
private theatricals. Mrs. Glasspoole and Mrs.
Lavertine dwell in cosy little contiguous flats at
Devonport, where their sailor husbands are sta-
tioned on the warship Theseus. The Lavertine
doctor man has just been gazetted Senior Staff
Surgeon.
Many St. Kilda girls play hockey, but in various
clubs. Now the enthusiastic seasiders want to
form an exclusive club of their very own, and one
excited player—proposed as captain—rushes
round to she-friends beating up recruits. The
new-chums catch the enthusiasm when they hear
of the fancy costume for the hockeyiste— very short
skirt and shirt of squirrel grey, knee-high tan boots,
and grey cap in blocked wool. The first wet
blanket on the enthusiasm aforementioned comes
with information about the practise rule—seven
to eight every morning ! Br-r-r-r ! Second draw-
back is the sight of the prospective captain’s
dissipated-looking nose. The girleen’s natural
profile is classic; but just now two hig bumpy
bruises add variety to the lines. “Oh, a mere
nothing 1 . Betty Crewe flung her hockey stick
into theair in a transport at our last win, and it
hit me.” (A hockey stick only weighs about 281 b.
or 301 b.) “But don’t worry. We’ve made
another new rule —JVo one must get excited if they
have a win ! ” Simplicity itself.
But the hockey club girls are the jolliest
fellows. Short skirts, blocked wool caps (in vivid
colors), startling complexions, and a tempestuous
rush. That’s a hockey club on the move.
Little Mrs. Harbinson—the wealthy and quiet
wife of the Hibernian doctor—has just battered
a law case to death, and made her opponents
pay the funeral expenses. It had to do with the
huge question of her marriage settlement. She
was a Laidlavv of the big Heidelberg family
before she annexed the sociable little medical
person. The settlement tied up her portion of
the Laidlaw properties, letting her enjoy the fat
income, but giving her no other control. With
her own beautiful eye she saw a loophole in the
deed, and has come triumphantly out of the case,
despite vigorous opposition from the trustees, her
father, her sister, and lawyer Trenchard. And
doesn’t she just congratulate herself 1
The last memories of an interesting family will
be scattered this week by the salesman’s hammer,
when the late Mrs. Loureiro’s possessions are
auctioned. Those who knew the woman and
her house have pleasant recollections of both.
Her personality was full of charm. The tragic
circumstances of her last years made her end a
very pathetic one.
. Archbishop Clarke goes a-voyaging to England
in February, or thereabouts. He’ll be abroad
until December, and takes his missus .and portion
of his family. The Prelate takes almost as many
months’ leave in a single year as the beatific Bevan
cleric, and, like that venerable performer, does it
on full pay. Archdeacon Hindley, the Irish
gentleman who objects to his country achieving
Home Rule, will play under-study to“ Dr. Clarke,
and administer the tender slap of confirmation to
the juniors of the diocese.
The other Archbishop goes to Rome anon
to interview Pope Pius, and takes Ireland as a
holiday stage in his itinerary. Before he departs
his flock is going to get him “done” in marble,
if funds permit; if not, in some more perishable
and cheaper stuff.
The purple-jerseyed Canadian lacrosse bovshad
a great old game with the green-clad Australians,
on Saturday afternoon,_ and the grand-stand
crowd was as frilly as if it were an English Test-
match and spring weather. There was tem-
pestuous delight when the Australians (who are
comparative novices) beat the Canadians at their
own pet game by two goals.
The famous “Besses o’ th’ Barn” were blown
down Coll ins-street from the Express, on Satur-
day, by the force of 600 extended cheeks and
every scrap of trumpet brass that could be col-
lected to do honor and advt. to the visitors.
The vibration made a minor earthquake;
one almost expected the buildings to totter!
It was an extraordinary crowd that filled the
Town Hall for the concert in the evening.
Band-haunting faces are faces that seem to belong
to the open air—to the band-blown sands of
favorite bay beaches. There was the extremely
swagger element also, led by the just-returned
Northcote lady, and a row of mayoresses or so ;
and the lacrosse boys made an exceedingly correct
line of black and white dinner dress along one
balcony, with nary a girl to break the even pattern
of shirtfront.
It’s easily believed by “ bachelor girls ” in Mel-
bourne that crimes great or little can be com-
mitted in this town without any police inter-
ference. A lass living in a city fiat makes bitter
plaint that on more than one oocasion. when she
and two or three flat-mates were walking to ur
from the theatre at night, they have ' been
most insolently accosted by civilians who
were sprawling at street corners, conversing
with the neighborhood’s custodians of order.
In each case the constable made no attempt
to restrain his pal’s impertinence, and, when
remonstrated with, contented himself with
declaring that the unchivalrous animal was “a
gentleman, and only joking.” A dozen such
trivial but significant examples of police in-
difference to the public’s comfort and security
could be cited. Some have been submitted to the
Commissioner, who replies with an indiscreet
silence.
Yours affectionately,
JOHANNA.

When the mail left, the United States papers
were full of frantic remarks on the downfall of gentleman
bruiser Ollagen-O’Brien. When the cable announced
his defeat by Tommy Burns it made some vague refer-
ences to a “ double cross.” Before entering the ring, and
while O’Brien was sitting in his corner fastening on his
gloves, Burns notified the referee that in order to get the
O’Brien into the ring he (Burns) had to agree to be
beaten—to “lay down,” as the Yankees sav. He an-
nounced that he intended to lay O’Brien down and was
fighting to win. He refrained from shocking Btien
with the news, but immediately Burns got going _ and
landed one on the napper, O Bnen understood. There is
a soul-affinity, a mental telepathy, between pugs
that lets them know when the other fellow is out
And O’Brien understood. He was in a hole, f behe\ii g
that Burns would keep to the arrangement, O linen had
omitted to train for an all-in scrap, and was qmte ut able
to stand up to his beefy antagonist. So he fled from
before his face, and the crowd reared on its hind legs and
hissed and cursed. O’Brien told it to ti e
went on racing for safety. As long as he
hiding he didn’t care a hang for public opinion. Burns
stood in the middle of the ring and holies eel to him,
beo-ffingr him “ to be a man and take his jTuel.
merely galloped further into a place of safety. In spite
of lack of condition, lower weight, inferior fighting
,lower, funk, and all the rest of it on the part of OBrien
Burns failed to utterly knock him out, and wept with
rage. However, the referee decided in his favor, and
Burns was cheered up somewhat. The referee also
announced that the fight was a double cross, and declared
all bets off.
Then O’Brien’s reputation, which had been look-
ing seamy for some time, suddenly cracked right np. y
the time the exposure was over, it seemed that O Brier
had never fought a genuine fight since he became a
front-ranker. They were all fakes, ni wluc li
backed himself to win, and paid the other f rf. l ®'
more than he would make out of the fight even it he
won it, to lose. Now they propose to remove his name
from the record book, and wipe the slate clean. A
O’Brien smiles cynically. “ 1 can take my
I’ll frankly admit I never went into the game for honor
or glory, or anything but the cash. And Ive got the
cash. I’m the richest prizefighter in the woild, and
nothing else matters. That’s all! ”

“Old ’Un” : Saw Bob Fitzsimmons at Foley’s
old White Horse gymnasium, in George-street, Sydney,
20 or more years ago, when he seemed quite dead.
Asthma had a strong grip of him. In his cor "6r, a fter
each round, he was a painful spectacle—gasping and
often on the verge of suffocation. It looked extreme
cruelty to allow the man to continue, but the call upon
the respiratory organs provided the cure. Twelve
months subsequently the trouble had almost disap-
peared. a »
Sydney will soon have a new racecourse—much
more complete than most others of its kind—on the Cooper
estate, south of Zetland. The situation is a great deal
handier to the city than that of any other suburban track.
Already £14,000 has been absorbed by the venture, and
£lO,OOO more, in £1 shares, is being raised to finish tne
work.

The publication of the weights for the Cups
and other spring handicaps has brought forth a thirty-
second edition of J. J. Miller’s Sporting Pamphlet and
Athletic Rfecord. There are 247 pages of information,
horsey and otherwise, in Miller’s pamphlet. Ihe most
interesting, for all admirers’ of Australia’s most remark-
able gee-gee, are pages 122 and 123, which set forth the
performances of Proceeder. The onetime buggy-horse,
entered again for the Caulfield and Melbourne Cups, has
run 100 races, winning 23 of them. May Ring has won ->
races in 87 tries.
*** . *
The South American can give some of the Aus-
tralians points in the management of races. Wherever
horse-racing is established in the leg-of-mutton continent
there is also the tote, and where two horses from the
same stable, no matter in whose names they are run,
start in the same race they are counted as one gee-gee on
the tote, and cannot be backed singly.
« * * *
“ H.G.” : There has been much denunciation of
vicious football in Melbourne recently, but all lovers of
true, sport should hail with glee the present tendency
towards stoush and laying out. Vicious football mean
clean football. Twelve years ago, when Victorian foot-
ball reached its zenith, it was the customary thing,
whenever a visiting team won a match, for the barrackers
of the home team to follow them, ten thousand strong,
peltino- their cabs with road metal; while every umpire
who knew his business used to carry a disguise to
enable him to escape with his life after the match.
Those were the palmy days of Melbourne football.
There was no professionalism worthy of the name,
and if suspicions of stiff running had ever entered the
head of anybody, he would only have needed to witness a
match to be convinced of his error. In every contest
willingness was the mainspring, and frequently the free
fights between the players were augmented by barrackers
lumping the fences and joining in ; while the lady
friends of the gladiators were wont to wait in the
pavilion with hat pins and puncture the legs of their
opponents as they came in. Then football became
'••entlemanly. The bookmaker stepped in, and two nice
well-behaved sets of young men went through a, number of
pre-arranged evolutions without any nasty spiteful work
or violence. The number of goals and behinds to be
kicked could be forecasted by those in the know with
actuarial accuracy. Barracking threatened to become a
lost art. There was no partisan feeling, and the South
barracker who met two Fitzroy barrackers in a lonely
street could pass them without fear of molestation.
Football became moribund : for years it lay like the
sleeping beauty. Then, by dint of hard work on the part
of some of the old-time enthusiasts, it was once more
••■alvanised into activity, and to-day, with profes-
sionalism controlled, if not stamped out, and the book-
maker entirely eliminated, it is itself again as a game.
,'* » *
The Council of South Melbourne, which has
some small say in the matter, lately asked for informa-
tion as to the management of Albert Park. It acquired
little, but what it did get threw considerable light on the
anxiety of certain well-to-do cliques to grab a slice of the
people’s property whenever they get the chance. The
boating clubs that row over the lagoon where the suicides
have to sit down to be drowned, pay from £3 3s. to £o os.
a vear for their shed sites. Thirteen junior football
clubs 49 ditto cricket clubs—two of them made up of.
ladies —and one hockey and one hurling club pay £1 Is.
a year each. These all play in the open, and must either
o-et out of the way of the casual meanderer, or knock him
nver and so if they pay but little, they get little
f n return. But South Melb. and St. Hilda Cricket
Clubs pay only£2s a year each for the exclusive use of
grounds that frequently during the winter bring them in
Wirt adav. The bowling clubs are different. They in-
quire onl’v * diminutive paddock; yet the Middle Park
Bowling ‘Club gets nearly half-ah-acre alongside the
station for £f. per annum, while the St. Hilda Club has the
use of a trifle more for £lO a year. Just across the road,
where the private landlord dwells, the same slice of the
earth would cost about £lOO a year, and as fast as the
club improved the paddock the landlord would raise the
rent-
“H.A.”: **F.C.” writes interestingly (B. 18/7/’O7)
about the finances of Melbourne football cluus, and their
connection with the cricket clubs that control the
grounds. “F. 0." knows a little about the matter, but
his remarks show the danger of a little knowledge. His
statement as to the division of profits is correct. But he
is astounded that the cricket clubs which “do nothing
and take no risk ” should get the largest share, and there
he makes his first big mistake. Except playing the actual
game the cricket clubs do everything, and take exactly
one-third of the risk. They provide and equip the
"round, keep it in order, supply dressing-room accom-
modation, water, light, etc., undertake the entire
management of all football matches (a matter involving
the organisation and supervision of a staff of 30 to 50 men
for big games), and attend to the whole business end of
the affair generally, involving an amount of work which
would surprise an outsider. Then “ F.C.” wonders what
the cricket clubs do with their money. All the money
made bv the cricket clubs in this manner goes towards
improving the grounds and making them fit to accommo-
date big crowds. Before this season St. Kilda had prac-
ticallv no revenue from football. South Melbourne has
splendidly improved its ground. Fitzroy has recently
built a fine new grandstand (to accommodate football
patrons), and made other improvements. East. Melbourne
has used the money to drag itself out of the nnre of debt
into which the club fell years ago. Melbourne—well the
M.C.C. has so big a revenue that it is impossible to
trace where its football money goes to. These are the
only grounds on which the cricket clubs get a share. At
Carlton and Collingw >od, the third share goes to a
ground committee, and the cricket elubsget nothing. On
all League grounds but St. Kilda the attendances are
checked by turnstiles, and no dispute is possible. It
is very evident from “F.C.’s” arguments that he
has not the remotest idea of the expense of
running’ a ground, and forgets that there are such
things as caretakers’ and assistants’ wages, rates and
taxes of different kinds running all the year round,
repairs, improvements, and alterations. I have no doubt
that a seat on the committee of one of the big metro-
politan cricket clubs for a season would be an eye-opener
for “F.C.” Finally, “F.C.” is “struck with amaze-
ment at the moderation of the football clubs in not
demanding full control of the grounds and treating the
cricket clubs as interlopers, who may graciously be per-
mitted to use the turf a month or two in the summer.
Well, if the football clubs are foolish enough not to
know when they are well off, they will start ‘ demand-
ing,” and go on' “demanding” till they are blue in the
face, hut the cricket clubs control the grounds—and that
ends it. , ,
Adelaide’s big school match—St. Peter’s r. Prince
Alfred—gave another example of the possibilities which
make the Australian code of football popular. There is
always scoring going on, or just going to happen, or just
ought to have happened but didn’t(grannnarcopyrighted),
and spectators love it accordingly. When S.A. last
played Victoria in Melbourne—the return match is due
in Adelaide next Saturday—S.A. scored 14 goals nine
behinds, and won by one point! The “college match
in Adelaide was a very similar event. The losing side
made a desperate effort in the last quarter, arid I mice
Alfred only got home by seven goals five behmds to six
and 10—a single point again.
* • • *
The governing bodies of Vic. football have, been
moved to action by the outcry against “ vicious ” play.
Week before last the League passed out a Melbourne club
’bailer for the rest of the season for striking an opponent.
The Wednesday League handed a similar sentence to a
South Melbournite for a like offence. The Association
went one better, and disqualified for life an ill-regulated
Port player who was adjudged guilty of “using threaten-
ing and obscene language and assaulting” an opponent.
Senior footballers are evidently persons of importance in
Melbourne. The Investigation Committee of the Ass.
had no sooner recommended this player’s disqualification
than his friends rolled up, hired the Port town
hall persuaded the local mayor, sundry councillors,
and’ politicians Sangster and Matthews on to
the platform, and passed a resolution asking
the Association “ not to go into the evidence or judge
the case, but merely to give the player justice. Ihe
Association, at its usual weekly meeting next day, ignored
this remarkable resolution, and, after hearing the accused
player’s advocates—the two Port delegates—endorsed the
recommendation ; wherefore the obstreperous young man
may presently be expected to seek some other sphere of
usefulness for his muscles. The Vic. Junior Association
this week inquires into an allegation that an insidious
elbow-jab bv a Footscra.v Junior player met with a retort
in the shape of a blow in the mouth so violent that five
stitches had to be inserted ; and the Queenschff Bench
is asked to adjudicate on a discussion between a
Oueenscliff ’bailer, and a military ditto, where again a
suro-eon had to repair the under-dog in the fight. As
usual, the good young men of the Metropolitan Churches
Association shine in fighting the bad fight that leadeth
to destruction. When Heidelberg' St. John s met
Brougham-street Methodists, on July 13, the barrackers
took a hand, and several willing fights w r ere only stopped
by the arrival of the police, who tore the combatants
apart. Numerous; players were laid out, but these
Christians were not quite hopeless—they only hooted
the umpire. South Australian barrackei s went one worse,
and “booted” him. Hahndorf and Mt. Barker (S.A.) clubs
had stumbled through a turbulent and unseemly scrnn-
mage, at the Mount, on July 13, till the last quarter,
when the former equalised the scores by kicking what
the goal umpire said was a goal. He had hardly time to
raise his flags before he was rushed, pummelled, and
kicked on the ground. Some of the players tried to
succor him, but Mt. Barker was on the warpath, and a
o-eneral attack was made on the Hahndorf men, who only
escaped, per van, to the accompaniment of showers of
stones and bushels of epithets.
* * *
The final deposit of £2OO in connection with
the Towns-Webb sculling match for the championship of
the world is due. Both men are in excellent
health, and training steadily. That double-scml facing
boat propelled by George Towns and Fogwell, is develop-
ing great speed powers in the champion-by-suceession.
Present appearances indicate that after he has rowed
Webb, Charles Towns will be a champion in reality. At
present it is hard to say what he is.
“Pick” : The Creswick (Vic.) v. Dean cricket
match, referred to by “ Blister ” (B. U/7/’O7), was not a
record In 1888 the Long Reach C. C., playing against
Towrang. near Goulburn (N.S.W.), scored one run in its
first innings. The solitary run was made by a casual
stranger who had joined in, the Long Reach team having
arrived on the ground one man short.

Humanitarians should note for reference at the
comin" election the following list of members of N.S.W.
Legislative Assembly. It contains the names and electo-
rates of those who voted to legalise gambling at coursing
matches for fear the inhuman, brutish and degrading
“sport ” should die out for want of gambling. The clause
was passed solely in the interests of a few.bookmakers
who bet at coursing matches, and a few ruffians who have
a vested interest in the cruel business, and who make a
useless living by supplying the grounds and the hares.

By a curious oversight on the part of I rovidenee
circumstance has been allowed to order things so that
when a man meets with an accident and has to spend
money on doctors and nurses, chemists and other neces-
sary evils, his income generally decreases just when it is
most wanted. Providence not having fixed things to meet
the case,man has to take a hand himself. The South British
Fire and Marine Insurance 00., of Maoriland, conies along
with an up-to-date Accident and Sickness Insurance
system. This, to a large extent, rectifies the oversight on
the part of Providence. Death by railway accident is
specially provided for, the compensation ranging over
three classes at .4500. £lOOO, and £2OOO. Death by any
other form of accident is also provided for. But the
great advantage of a policy is that it provides a regular
income for people who are for the time totally disabled,
not only by accidents but by certain specified fashionable
diseases, such as appendicitis, pluerisy, Pj ieun 1 ' o ?'‘J’
diabetes, and 13 others, equally pleasant, though less
popular. Total and permanent blindness, and permanent,
general paralysis that are not due fco accKlent, s are also
provided for, the three figures being £5OO, £250, and £l2.j.
And all these advantages are to be obtained for premiums
ranging from two to ten guineas per annum. The bouth
British is a solid concern, and has been so well managed
that its reserves have been built up to a sum times
greater than its capital. A good concern to do business
with ! *
Goldsbrough, Mort and Co., (Sydney) report
re wool Our catalogue comprised 997 bales, consisting
chiefly of Queensland clips. Considering the quantity,
the selection was a good one for the time of .war. There
was a representative attendance of buyers. The market,
however, was somewhat irregular, good greasy and
scoured ruling from par to 5 per cent, lower as compared
with last month’s rates, while faulty scoured showed a
decline of fully 5 per cent. By auction and privately we
sold 862 bales.*

I CURE FITS.
As a letter of introduction predisposes
one in favour of the bearer, so advertU.
ing may predispose one in favour of the
thing advertised; but, unless the article
advertised possesses in itself qualities
which the World values, no amount of
praise will insure its success-therefore
- I give you a bottle of my remedy, to try,
before purchasing any; it speaks for itself

The latest race between Messrs. Davies and
Relph for the speed supremacy of Port Jackson, and, ini -
dentally, that of Australia, on Saturday, proved clearly
that two Fairbanks engines can just about '" a , n , ln
long race to beat one Invincible. Of course, if the fepre
sentative of the foreign engine thought it worth while
taking such a fearful trip as Saturday’s to demonstrate
that —well, it is his own hearse. When the I " vl " clb ®
beat the Fairbanks, four weeks earlier, the owner ot tne
Fairbanks had a whole volume of excuses to make, ana
the only reason he didn’t give, while protesting he hdn t
want to make excuses, was that the InvinciMe was the
faster boat. But, apparently, when the sting of defeat
had left him he realised that all this sort of talk was mere
guff, for, instead of bringing exactly the same boat into
the deciding contest, he installed an extra engine, and
brought it into the race with exactly double the power.
The engines were coupled up tandem, and even with
double the power Davies, apparently, didn t feel too
sure about downing his rival, and got into the
press the morning of the race with a sort of half
excuse, tending to explain the situation in case
anything went wrong, and the double-poweied boat
should get beaten. This action has caused much com-
ment, although the Motor Boat Club decided that, as it
was a best-and-best race, Davies was within his rights.
All the same, general opinion as far as this scribe can
gauge it, is that it wasn’t good sport. Certainly it was a
full and unreserved admission that the boat, as formerly
powered, had no possible hope, bar accidents, with the
Australian flyer. It was an awkward position Davies
having to either submit to certain defeat or frankly ad-
mit that it takes two Fairbanks to down an Invincible,
and of the two uncomfortable horns of the dilemma he
chose the latter.
* * » *
The race was exciting. An ugly nor’-easter
blew the water into great heaps, and left yawning
ravines in the track of the two boats. That was bad
enough, but racing through the Heads was as fine an
assortment of white horses as ever came out of the south
Pacific. They fell on the boats as they climbed, the
watery hills and rushed down into holes through which
the bottom of the Harbor could be seen momentarily.
Both craft behaved splendidly, and tore on at full
tepeed, regardless of consequences. Once, just as
the Invincible sprang through the top of a kopje ot
foam, a great curler running cross ways crashed down on
the stern, which was tilted skywards with the pro-
peller racing in the air, and nearly stove it in. The speed
at which the boat was running broke the force ot the
blow, else there would have been no more Invincible.
One blade of the propeller was twisted and another
cracked, and the whole mechanism was badly strained.
Rushing round the buoy Relph jammed bis helm hard
over forgetful for the moment that his boat was halt
full ’of water which, as the vessel heeled to the
turn rushed to the side and forced it down. lor
the second time in a few minutes Relph and his
assistant looked into the face of David Jones ; but Relph
reversed his wheel in a flash, brought the boat up attain,
and swung wide. The race was lost, however, and the
championship had passed from the green boat. Relph
ran to the finish, and started in the second heat, but that
blow between the Heads had finished the Invincible for
that day. First, the shaft bracket broke, then one blade
of the propeller flew off, and, with a wobbling shaft and
bne of the remaining blades of the propeller twisted, the
Invincible crawled back to the starting - point.
As a matter of sober judgment, Relph was foolish
to race the Fairbanks under the conditions. Davies chal-
lenged Invincible on the ground that his alleged 16-20 h.p.
boat had not had a fair chance to prove what it could do.
then instead of showing what it could do, he brought
along’ a practically new boat of double power. This
question of power badly requires settling. Although
Fairbanks is advertised to have run as a 30-40 h.p., there
is more than a suspicion that the power shown on Satur-
flap was somewhere about 78. Anyhow, nobody this
writer knows takes seriously the statement that Fairbanks
is only 30-40 h.p. If it is, on Saturday’s running it holds
a world’s record. If Mort’s Dock retains the judgment
that made it what it is, it will let Relph loose on the con-
struction of a racing boat pure and simple, built specially
to |hold the speed supremacy of the Harbor for an
Australian engine. Admitting that racing boats cost
money, in the interests of the industry it is necessary
that the supremacy of Australian waters should be held
by an all-Australian boat. And Mort s Dock has the best
plant and the best man to build that boat.

The Queenslanders put up a great struggle
against the All Blacks in the return match at Brisbane on
Saturday, but the Maoriland back machine, combined
with the finesse of the black-clad gentlemen behind the
scrum, was too much for the Bananalanders. The
islanders took possession of the leather, and got about the
best backs on earth going full speed. The victors had
scored 17 points before Queensland broke its ice, and then
the war-waste began to tell, and, with two men short,
Maoriland was flung back on its goal line, and Queens-
land notched 11 points in short order. But it is doubtful
if the Queensland score would have reached double
figures but for the All Blacks being short-handed. The
home forwards played a sterling game with dogged pluck,
but the Maoriland rogue elephants juggled the leather
out of their hands, and let loose the prancing backs.
The result, was that when Queensland did get a show the
leewav was too much to make up in the time, and the
o-anie ended in favor of Maoriland by 17 points to 11.
» * * *
The chief difference between the Australian
and the Maoriland Rugbyite is that the former has some
idea:of fairness, while the Maorilander seems to be dead
to every feeling but a great and terrible thirst for points.
When an Australian Rugbyite finds himself off-side he
usually stops playing till he has regained the right posi-
tion—the Maorilander doesn’t. First of all he deliberately
gets off-side, and when he has managed that he plays on
till the referee whistles him down. And with the whole
forward and middle divisions of the team working on
those lines the referee has no chance, unless he likes to
take the unpleasant step of ordering a man off the
o-rbund after repeated warnings have failed to keep him
in place. But if Australia is to meet Maoriland on equal
terms in the Rugby paddock, that umpire has to be
found; and, further, the teams to meet the invaders will
have to be chosen earlier and made to practise together,
with special attention to the training of the scrum halves.
But that referee is the most necessary item.
* * * *
The Rugby competition resumed in Sydney on
Saturday, and, judging by the crowds that stood afar off
with the wind whistling round their nobility, the game
o-rows inore popular every week—as a spectacle, at any
rate. The interest of the day was centred on the
struggle between Glebe and South Sydney, at Sydney
Cricket Ground. The Glebe brought its absolutely best
team into the field, and attacked strenuously from the
kick off. South was equally willing, and, aided
by the breeze, drove the butchers back and com-
pelled them to force twice. Glebe came away
again, and one man, breaking through, careered at
a' o-iddy pace for the Southern’s goal. The full back
brought him down in great style, but had there been any-
one to pass to !! 1 South broke away from the scrum,
hemmed the Reds in on their own line, and struggled
vainly to get across. Glebe got the leather, brought off
a passing rush, and scored the first try amid tremendous
uproar. • After another exciting struggle South Sydney
equalised the score, and the half-time whistle brought a
breathing spell. With the wind behind them Glebe
raged like the heathen in David’s psalm, but only managed
to kick a goal. On the other hand, South only managed to
secure a try, and Glebe stood victorious on the stricken
field with 11 points to nine. This placed G! she at the head
of the competitir i list, with 16 points. Eastern Suburbs
was also out in all its might, with Messenger raging
as of old, and Western Suburbs, though struggling
-•amely, was pushed right over on to its back, trodden on,
and beaten by 29 points to six. A few minutes after St.
George started against University at the Urn. Oval, the
former began to get a dark suspicion that it was up
against the All Blacks. The Uni. men were very fast, and
the unfortunate Saints found the oval a regular Coliseum,
where they were butchered to the tune of 26 to ml. lhis
made Eastern Suburbs, South Sydney and University
dead level in the competition with 15 points each, just
one behind Glebe. Newtown and North Sydney put up
a wretcln-i exhibition, in which Newtown proved top
scruffier, with 10 points to six. Sydney had the bye.
The League (Austral rules) matches were post-
poned c .1 Saturday last to allow players and barracke-rs
to ro’lup to the match between N.S.W. and South Mel-
bourne at Sydney Agricultural Ground. It wm a . gr-at
game, and South Melbourne scored a well-deserved w,„
by the comparatively narrow margin (in the Australian
game) of 10 points, having piled up 70 to N.S.W. s 60.
The bustling nor’-east wind spoiled the kicking, which is
so fine a feature of the Australian game. A rather
numerous crowd accepted the Leagues invitation to
come along and see if the national game is not as
interesting as Rugby or Association. However, to
return to the struggle. N.S.W. made some great on-
slaughts, but the South Melbourne backs marked in an
astounding manner, and sent it back again. At one time
the Cornstalks were leading by a point, and the
N.S.W. tail obliterated the landscape. But South
came away again, and at quarter time it was level
pegging, with 14 points each. In the second quarter
N.sTw. handled the ball very badly, while the
visitors worked with the easy precision of a linotype, and
in spite of the head wind were practically the attacking
side right through the quarter. So well did they do it,
too, that the bell found them leading by 34 to 19. In the
next quarter, with the wind behind them, the visitors
carried disaster right home to the Cornstalks and ended
up with 61 points to N.S.W. s 40. Then the N.S.W. men
started a desperate struggle to make up the leeway of 21
points in the final quarter. South put up a great defence,
rushing the ball into N.S.W. territory over and over
again, and keeping the home team cn the defensive.
Meanwhile time was running out, and their tremendou.
efforts were beginning to tell on the Melbourne men.
while the Cornstalks, with the wind behind them,
struggled more furiously than ever. Just a few minutes
before the bell-ring, South Melbourne’s strength gave out,
and with a whoop the Walers fell on them, rushed the
ball into their territory, and reduced the Bad to 10. They
had victory almost in their hands when the bell sounded
their requiem, and the exhausted Melbourne team
staggered off the field victorious by 70 to 60. But it was
a very close thing. m # ,
In the Sydney Soccer struggle for the Rawson
Cup, Pyrmont still leads, and now has 21 competition
points to Glebe’s 18. Balmain, Roselle, and Ultimo—l 6,
14, and 14 respectively—follow. Petersham and Navy are
eight and five respectively.
By downing Petersham, with three goals to nil,
Pvrmont again secures the premiership. Even should a
miracle happen, and the Navy beat it next week, it will
still head the list, so the matter is practically settled.
G!«be met Rozelle, and for a time it looked as though
the butchers would have all their work cut out
to maintain their position, but) the Rozelle defence
was pierced at last, and half-time saw Glebe
leading by one to nil. The second half saw Rozelle take
a hand at pressing, and Glebe had several narrow escapes.
But soon it penned the Rozelles round their own goal and
commenced artillery practice. One shot got into the net,
and the game was over. Play continued for some time,
but both sides were worn out. Out at Leichhardt, Ultimo
placed several dark horses on the field, and shook up
Balmain in a startling manner. But the Balmainers
defence was sound, and when the attack weakened they
followed uo and scored the only goal of the game.
* * • *
Though the Vic. Football League suspended its
premiership matches to give the Canadian iacrossers a
show for a gate, the Association was less generous, and,
though its inter-State contest at Adelaide took away the
best of its players, it still poked round after the stray
local sprat by running on with its premiership games.
The only one that drew a decent attendance was the
Richmoiid-Williamstown discussion, where some 12,000
ill-regulated barrackers urged their respective teams to
deeds of violence. Their advice was hardly needed, any-
how, as was shown by the temporary laying-out of three
plav’frs—including Richmond’s captain who sustained a
most uncomely kick over the eye. The result once
again exemplified the value of tactics and system.
Williamstown scored freely with a wind that blew from
goal to goal, but could only notch one behind
against the wind; Richmond, playing the concerted
short-kick game against the wind, scored four goals four
behinds, and the result was 68 points to 53. The other
games resulted as anticipated. West downed Ports by 65
to 34; Essendon walked over Prahran by 51 to 29 ; and
Footscray made a painful exhibition of the unmention-
able team that cannot win a game. During the week the
Association mapped out a neat little scheme of district
football, in which a bare 12 months’ residence is to
qualify ; but as the League monopolises the chief metro-
politan districts, and as its matches draw three spectators
to the Association’s one, the scheme will probably before
long be for sale cheap.
* * * *
The casualness of the average footballer about
reached high-water mark in Melbourne, the ocher day,
when the Railways United Club played the Carlton
Trades. The discussion progressed more or less amicably
till three-quarter time, with the Railways winning hands
down, when someone discovered that they were playing
19 men, no less. Then the odd man was discharged from
duty, and that team’s score wiped off the sheet.
The Football League’s generosity in suspending
all local matches promised a fine crowd for the first inter-
national lacrosse match in Melbourne, in spite of dusty,
windy, rain-threatening weather; yet the 20,000 who sat
and stood around the M.C.C. ground were typical rather
of a cricket than a football crowd. There was no induce-
ment to stand up and crush forward, yelling one’s lungs
out the while ; and no one sits throughout a football
match—unless a lusty woman with a stout brolly happens
to be planted immediately in the rear, and it’s ten-to-one
she’ll stand and wave her gingham promiscuously like-
wise. Nevertheless the ’crossers gave a pretty, and at
times exciting, show. A few players shone out above
their mates, notably Campbell, MacDonald and Grace
for Canada, and Delves, Hughes and Bryning for
Australia. The scores at quarter-time were: Canada,
one goal ; Australia, nil. At half-time the Kan-
garoo was top dog by three goals to one, and stayed
so to the end, winning by five goals to three. It is not
easy to account for the Canucks’ defeat. They are short,
stocky men, of the British type, and the long, lean men
in the green and gold ran away from them time after
time. The stocky men should have lasted their oppo-
nents out —yet they were palpably tired at the finish ;
their captain averring that the terrific pace wore them
out. Nevertheless, they played with a coolness that
declined to be bustled about anything ; and their passing
and stick work generally was a trifle superior to that of
the Australians. Perhaps undue confidence in that
superiority and implicit reliance on their defence
led to their undoing—that and the one weak
feature of their play, their lack of persistence.
Having turned the ball from their goal, they
seemed quite content to let the others get it and to
rely on that impregnable phalanx of defenders to turn
it away again. The Australians vainly tried for one
quarter to break through—then their captain saw the
weak spot, and after that the day was his. Time after
time the Canucks were allowed to pass the ball down the
ground and try to shoot it through. Delves turned it
off, but then, instead of letting the Canadians pick it up
and shoot again, he whipped it up like lightning, tossed
it on to Graham or Mann, then to Humphries, to Taylor,
to Bryning, back and forth till a chance came to shoot-
all this with a speed that gave the visitors no chance to
re-erect that stonewall that had baffled the Aus-
tralians at first. To Delves is due a Urge slicei ofl the
credit of the victory, for 13 not only defended almost as
capably as Arens (of Canada), but he immediately changed
the scene of play, and made it possible fc . his mates to
score, which the other didn’t. As to whether this team
would be called first-class in the Dominion, cannot
say; but his memory goes back to his boyhood s a y ,
when he watched the Redskins, from the countrj of the
Great Lakes, playing the Whites in Nova ■-'otia, v. •
Island, and elsewhere ; and he remembers also the play
of the copper-colored team that made a holy show of the
clubs of the Cold Country in ’B3, and his idea is that
those lean brown men showed as great facility with tneir
crosses as our present visitors, and superior speed.

The victory of Brookes and Wilding, by which
they won the lawn tennis championship of tt 3 world and
the Davis Cup, is the more interesting to lot'al tennisitM
because the nextchaltenge for the honors mustbe decided
either in the Commonwealth or Maonland. Wilding, wno
played with Brookes, is at present a resident
Northern Hemisphere, and played only on his birthplace
qualification—he is a Maorilander. Tennis in Australia is
not a pastime of the very wealthy, and lack of money
prevented an absolute Australian representative going to
partner Brookes. Should the next match be played here,
Maoriland may want to be represented, seeing that its
rep. helped to win the cup. Brookes is expected out,
but Wilding is doubtful. His absence will not make
much difference, as Brookes and Dunlop (Vic.)i are a much
more successful combination in Doubles than Brookes and
Wilding. N.S.W. tennis people would much like to see
their champion, Horace Rice, pitted against Brookes.
Cabled that a crowd of Londoners had a burst
of anger because the wicket at Lords’ ground was tc > wet
for play. Having paid entrance money for an entertain-
ment which didn’t happen, they demanded the return of
their cash, and finally they “took it out” of the.wicket.
As the result of the damage to the. turf, Maclaren, the
Lancashire captain, refused to continue the game next
day. Of course, the howlers had a genuine grievance
against the management, which took their money under
false pretences. Cricket managers are the only gate-
money showmen who consider themselves entitled to
“bilk ” the public. A theatrical boss refunds the money
received on account of a show which is postponed or can-
celled, and Heaven only knows why the autocratic rule
of “ takings-keepings ” has become an established swindle
at cricket grounds.
*■***.
The insincerity and inconsistency of the anti-
betting law, and the partial enforcement of it by the police
and post-office authorities, is perhaps rather m ore glaring
in Victoria than in the other States. For instance, a
Melbourne agency for Tatt’s Sweep was lately prosecuted
and fined heavily, which was at least consistent with the
post-office boycott of Tatt’s in Tasmania. The address
formerly known as Wren’s City Tattersall’s Club is also
black listed at the G.P.0., and any letters posted to it
are duly confiscated. Advertising - bookmakers, if they
be unlicensed, are subject to prosecution, and, for that
matter, the mere newspaper announcement of a licensed
bookie being addressable at the Victorian Club is
seemingly tabooed —for the present. Meanwhile, the
Postal Department permits Jack Wren (taking a notorious
instance) to have private letter boxes at the G.P.O. Wren s
cards, stating that “ Melbourne” is a sufficient address, are
spread abroad, together with lists of betting prices on
forthcoming big events. The absurdity of permitting
G.P.O. private letter boxes to Wren (and, presumably, to
other well-known “books”), whilst the deli very of letters to
Tattersall is strictly prohibited, is not more palpable than
the utter dishonesty of this Government assistance of
the Gambling Evil. Again, the mere fact that these bet-
tino- lists bear no printer’s name is a matter for police
action ; but the wife of many a Melbourne policeman may
find the unlawful bit of paper in her husband’s pocket, if
she goes through his uniform when he is snoring the
sleep of the unjust. Common sense cries out for a fair
settlement upon a solid basis of the old, old question of
Gambling Control. For common sense, which favors
Tatt’s and a licensed totalisator, above all other outlets
for the inevitable Evil Spirit, finds the Law, the Police
and the Postal Department conspiring to leave undone
the things that ought to be done, and do the things that
the public in general doesn’t approve of.
* * * *
Theodore Andrea Cook discourses fluently on
the subjection of the neddy in his latest book, “ Eclipse
and O’Kelly.” Cook maintains that there is no record of
any horse having been ridden before 10008. C., so evidently
he attaches little importance to the song of Moses (about
1430 8.C.) re the overthrow of the Egyptian horsemen ;
but he points out that chariots have been traced back
almost 2000 years before that. The horse was originally
a much smaller animal than he is at present, and suitable
for driving- long before he was big and strong enough to
ride. Cook places the height of the earliest known rid-
ing horses at barely 14 hands. Although the Babylonians
drove horses according to their own records 1500 years
B C., the first jockey was the Greek who rode in the
Olympic Games 648 B.C. Of course, this is evidence that
men rode horses previous to his appearance. By 359 B.C.
the mode had grown in favor immensely, and a man on
horseback appeared on the coins of Philip 11. of Macedon.
When Caesar arrived in Britain he found the natives had
subjugated the horse, or rather pony, and drove him in
harness, and a coin of Cymbeline’s reign shows the
animal to have been a miniature edition ofthe Shire,
Clydesdale and Suffolk breeds of to-day. The emperor
Severus improved the stock by importing southern
horses, which were kept at Netherby, in Yorkshire - .
When the age of heavy armor came along it brought with
it a demand for heavy horses, and the result was an ele-
phantine brute much more fit to haul a plough than
carry a man. * * *
The Adelaide Racing Club has a record entry
of 297 nominations for its two-days Grand National
meeting. The previous record was 292 in 1898, and the
numbers have been far lower lately. The G. N. Steeple,
however, has the unusually small entry of 15.
* * * *
Sydney ponydom was very much ruffled during
the past week. Ascot and Kensington seized on al
Wednesdays (excepting one) up to December 4, ignoring
Rosebery Park as if it never existed, and Rosebery Park
naturally kicked hard and persistently, and threatened
an appeal to the newly-created (in accordance with the
Gaming Act) Advisory Board. This course might have
brought trouble to all three squabbling bodies, for it is
an open secret that the Board has little, if any, sympa-
thy with pony racing ; but the difficulty was overcome
on Monday, and all is quiet for the time being. A
peculiarity of this latest wrangle is that Kensington and
Ascot were combined against Rosebery Park, while in
that lengthy and fairly-recent disturbance which forced
special legislation for pony racing, Kensington and Rose-
bery Park were shaking fists at Ascot.
N.S.T7. Cyclists’ Association is desperately
anxious to amalgamate with the League of Wheelmen,
which it was founded to destroy. So far, its advances
have been met with sniffs, and it is now struggling to
work up an agitation in the hope of subjugating the
League as Joshua did Jericho—by making a great noise.
The truth of the matter is that the Ass. is in a bad way.
Financially, it is generally understood to be about broke,
and the best of its members are applying to the League
for re-instatement; so unless amalgamation comes, and
comes quickly, there will be no Ass. left. Of course, if
the League were foolish enough to agree to the proposal,
it would have to take back all the crooks and purse-
cutters it has biffed out during the last three years of
strenuous fighting, and start the good work all over
again. Which would be suicidal. The brutal truth is
that the Ass. is dying, and hopes to get a fresh lease of
life by fastening like a parasite on to the League. And
parasites are deadly. 4 , ,
The man who has decided to give up the idle
life and take to some form of manly exercise should
invest in Spalding’s Athletic Library, which not only
instructs the novice in the finer points of his game, but
o-ives him the history of it, the names of mighty cham-
pions, past and present, and the names of those who pro-
mise to be champions. Every game and pastime has its
separate number, and the work throw 3 a great light on
the subject, no matter which subject it may be. The
latest arrivals are the cricket and lawn tennis numbers,
and all enthusiasts of either game should procure copies.
* * * *
“ S.s.” : The officials of the Vic. Amateur
Athletic Ass. running the amateur boxing competition
are a quaint crew of sportsmen, who would make excel-
lent Sunday-school superintendents. One condition they
insisted on very noisily, and with an elderly and skittish
show of virtue, was that the boxers must go into the ring
wearing each a guernsey or sweater in addition to the
usual trunks, and must box in this grotesque garment.
The result was that the arm movements of the men were
ridiculously handicapped, and the display was poor. The
idea that athletes performing before an audience of men
would be guilty of gross depravity in showing the upper
half of them, is worthy of the nice-nasty type of elderly
lady with her eye glued to a keyhole. But the said
dainty officials forgot to insist on the wrestlers wearing
overcoats or tin armor, and thecompetitors in this branch
came out in the usual lone and scanty tog.
A horror that is peculiarly Amurkan is the
boxing woman, a type of female who gets her poses illus-
trated in the sporting papers over an announcement that
she is anxious to meet something her own weight.
Whether these contests ever take place, writer knoweth
not, but so far he has had the good luck to escape drop-
ping on an account of one. But women’s boxing matches
may be among the list of Amurkan luxuries that are
never mentioned in polite society, and which are re-
served specially for millionaires and oil, steel and pork
potentates.
* * * *
Billy Cooper, featherweight, of Sydney, jour-
neyed to Melbourne, where he was an unknown, to meet
Frank Thorn, of Westralia, and Billy demonstrated, in bis
first round with strong, block.v, and clever Frank, that
he had sadlv over-estimated his ability. The Westralian
simply swarmed all over the Sydneysider, hitting him
how, when, and where he pleased. Before two minutes
were used up Cooper was ditto. He was taking every-
thing and giving nothing. In the second round Cooper
was down twice, and neglecting to get. up in time on the
second occasion, the 'bus went away without him. Thorn
could account for ten a day of the like of Cooper, and
would probably be content with a reduction in purses on
taking a quantity.

Cabled that Barney Wrenolds is returning from
the U.S.A. immediately, leaving William Squires in the
land of hope and glory. What William intends to do has
not been officially announced, but The Bulletin learns
from a private source, more or less unreliable, that the
Australian champion has announced that he will stay in
the States till he has learned the Yankee method of
scrapping, and then once more proceed to climb the
ladder that leads to fame and dollars by the simple
process of killing a few “ suckers” and taking on the next
best man after each fight. Bruising, if well managed, is
a lucrative game in the States, and William does not
yearn to go back to the mine. Anyhow, there is no
reason why he should. Even the loser’s end of that gate
was a substantial sum, and when he left Australia Squires
was supposed to have the best part of £2OOO in his clothes.
He wasn’t searched, but that was the estimate.

INDIGESTION.
“Digestion is the Process op the Assimila-
tion op Pood, Whereby the Body is
Nourished.”
■ Indigestion, or dyspepsia, invariably arises
from improperly prepared food, hasty eating,
over-indulgence, fatigue, and exhaustion during
the hot weather, breathing foul air, excessive
brain work, and more frequently from cares and
worry, which exhaust the nervous force and
weaken the organs of digestion. Dr. Morses
Indian Root Pills are not simply a purgative
which forces the food out of the body, depriving
the stomach of its nourishment and thus starving
the system ; they contain the essential properties
that are temporarily lacking in the stomach
through weakness, and complete the digestion
and assimilation, and so restore the functions of
the digestive organs by obtaining the maximum
of nutriment from the food, which sustains the
body and assures good health.
“Twelve months ago,” writes Mrs. Benjamin
Spooner, Glen Innes, N.S.W., “ I was induced
by a friend to try Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills,
as I was suffering severely from indigestion and
bilious headaches. I had already tried various
remedies without any apparent results, so 1
followed my friend’s advice and bought two
bottles of Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills. They
gave me great relief. After a short course of
these pills the headaches left me, and I lost all
traces of the symptoms of indigestion.”
Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills are a. perfect
blood purifier, and a sure remedy for biliousness,
constipation, indigestion, headaches, sallow com-
plexion, liver and kidney troubles, piles, pimples,
boils, and for female ailments.’'

STECK PIANOS.
If you are considering the purchase of
a piano* and will but compare the
STECK with any other instrument you
have in mind as being of fine quality,
you will quickly learn the reason for
the enthusiasm of musicians over the
wonderful merits of the STECK.
The individuality of this Piano, so far as it can be described
in words, consists of a certain richness and mellowness of tone
—a sympathetic, singing voice, as distinguished from those
pianos characterised chiefly for brilliancy, but lacking in the
deeper and subtler qualities which art demands. Wherever
there are musicians of true culture, professional or amateur,
this piano is known for its enduring qualities, its generous
singing tone, its beautiful harmonious soul.
When the Grand Nibelungen Orchestra, at Beyreuth, desired to
tender Richard Wagner a special token of their affection, it was a
STECK PIANO that was selected for the gift. Wagner placed the
STECK PIANO in the first rank, his opinion being shared by his
friend Frank Liszt:
“ The fine STECK PIANO which / have obtained is
everywhere acknowledged to be excellent. My
great friend, Frank Liszt, expressed the liveliest
satisfaction after he had played upon it.
“(Sgd.) RICHARD WAGNER.’ 1
The STECK PIANO is manufactured by the PIANOLA CO., in
the largest piano-producing factories in the world, making last year
a little over 16,000 instruments. The prices range from £5B to £l2O.
We should be glad if you will call and see the STECK, ®r, failing
that, write for Catalogue “P.”
THE PIANOLA C 0„ 357 george-st., Sydney.
Propy. Ltd.
Branches at 185 Collins-St., Melbourne ; 311 Queen-St., Brisbane ; 38 KingWilliam-St., Adelaide.
The other day the steamer Den of Airlie
arrived at Port Adelaide, and, according to the
plans, it was to be followed at monthly intervals
by the Oro, Errol, and other vessels of the same
line. It was, in fact, going to be a new Anglo-
Australian cargo service, independent of the
English shipping ring. The people behind it were
big and powerful—they control the Mogul and
Plate lines—and they fixed up offices in many
places, and got promises of much support. But
then the ring got to work, and the support gradu-
ally faded away, until eventually even the first
steamer of the new line had to sneak off empty
to the Baltic and take in a timber cargo. That
ended the new line, it is understood. The ship-
ping ring has triumphed—the same ring that
Australia has to depend upon to carry its pro-
ducts to Europe in order to pay its great interest
bill. But still the idea of a Commonwealth ship-
ping service is socialism, and treason, and other
horrible things.

aperient medicines.”
HOWTO STIMULATE THE LIVER AND PROMOTE DIGESTION.
Kutnow’s Powder stimulates the digestive processes so perfectly that errors of diet are neutralised
thereby. It enables you to eat heartily without the dread of overfulness, flatulency, or acidity.
People of sedentary occupation require-periodical stimulation of the liver, which plays a most important
part in digestion. The bile should be discharged into the intestine to mix with the food from the
stomach, otherwise constipation will be very pronounced. Perfect digestion means perfect action of
the stomach, liver, arid bowels. Kutnow’s Powder acts concurrently on each of these forces of
digestion; hence its value in ensuring regularity. Another point of merit is its beneficial cleansing
properties ; a dose of Kutnow’s Powder cleanses the Stomach, rouses the Liver, frees the bile, and sets
the machinery in perfect working order.

Gentlemen, —I am sorry I have not written before to
state the result of the case I have been treating, but I
have been too happy in the great blessing which has be-
fallen, me. My was the patient to whom I gave the
powders, quite unknown to him, of course. At one time
•tl, seemed hopeless, being in the bar all day, as he was, in
the very midst of temptation ; but 1 kept in the bar with
him myself as much as possible, and did not let him stay
in it any more than I could possibly help, and I am now
thankful to see him constantly turn away from the spirit
bottles with a shudder. T have about a dozen powders
left. I shall recommend Eucrasy to anyone I know
troubled like I was.—Believe me to be, yours sincerely
grateful, (Mrs.) M. J. S.

The Imported Mischief-Maker.
The return of W. M. Hughes, M.P., from
London (where he so successfully represented
Australian seamen’s interests at the Navigation
Conference) was marked by the prompt settle-
ment of the Sydney coal-lumpers’ strike, which
had threatened to paralyse the port of Sydney
and put a serious check to the growing prosperity
of Australia. That strike suggests that an ad-
dition should be made to the list of imported evils.
Unto the rabbit, the fox, the thistle, the unsober
“Christmas Goose Club,” the habit of saying
“ blanky,” the stinkwort, sectarianism, phthisis,
and titular distinctions, should be joined the
imported British labor leader, who in our
climate has a tendency to become a dangerous
nuisance. The first British Labor leader was
probably imported into Australia for the same
reason as the rabbit. It was not that there was
any special reason for either. The native kan-
garoo was quite fit for the job of eating all the
grass that the sheep did not want. The local
Labor leader was capable of managing things so
that trades-unions and labor bodies generally got to
a height of influence and prestige not equalled
anywhere else in the world. But somebody felt
lonesome, and couldn’t say “haw” comfortably
without seeing an occasional lop-eared rabbit in
his paddo—we mean meadow. So Bunny
came. Somebody else felt lonesome without
a Trafalgar Square atmosphere of disfranchised
workmen listening dismally to vociferous orators
talking vainly against the existing order of things.
So Tom Mann came. In England, Bunny was all
right. In England, probably, Tom Mann was all
right. In Australia, they are both pests. This
coal-lumpers’ strike in Sydney was from beginning
to end an artificial trouble run by imported labor
leaders for the purpose of advertising their ideas
of “ industrial war ” and “ revolutionary trades-
unionism.” There was no real dispute between
employers and employed. So soon as Hughes,
M.P., got back from England with his common-
sense, the whole trouble vanished. Had the im-
ported labor leaders had another month’s undis-
turbed possession of the field, they would probably
have involved the wharf-laborers and possibly the
coal-miners, and the country would then have
drifted fast towards another Maritime Strike. It
is not necessary to convict Tom Mann and his
type of an intention to work to that end. Proba-
bly it is rather a case of “not knowing.” But
that doesn’t make the nuisance less. Could not
British Labor leaders be stopped at the Customs ?
Ruskin.
“ 0.K.” writes : A while ago a Bulletin woman-
writer uttered a cry of aesthetic pain at the publica-
tion of “ Penny Buskins ” for the People. The
works of that one-time exclusive author, she said,
were being profaned by penny publication. But
whereas the name of Ruskin is commonly identi-
fied, thusfar, with books on Art, his future fame
(if he is to achieve it) will be founded on those
writings which he particularly intended for the
instruction of the public that wants good
literature in the cheapest possible form.
Ruskin’s essays in some cases were written as
letters to “a working cork-cutter of Sunder-
land,” whilst Great Britain was in a state of
Reform Bill agitation. As an advanced
thinker on political and social subjects he is to-
day more esteemed by his readers than when he
first put his thoughts into print. The test of time
proved him to be eminently worthy to have his
50-vear-old letters republished as penny pam-
phlets for the toiling millions. Ruskin wrote of
many things other than the fine arts, and what-
ever he wrote had wisdom in it. To those hard-
shell supporters of Great Britain’s policy, who
snort at The Bulletin’s “scurrilous abuse” of
Britain’s Brave Brown Allies, the study of Rus-
kin’s “Time and Tide” may be cordially com-
mended. Hear what the honest democratic man
of culture has to say in Letter V.: —
A company of Japanese jugglers has come over to
exhibit in London (Feb., 1867). There has long been an
increasing interest in Japanese art, which has been very
harmful to many of our own painters, and I greatly
desired to see what these people were, and what they
did. Well, I have seen Blondin, and various English and
French circus work, but never yet anything that sur-
prised me as did one of these men’s exercises on a sus-
pended pole. Its special characteristic was a close ap-
proximation to the action and power of the monkey ;
even to the prehensile power in the feet; so that I asked
a sculptor friend who sat in front of me whether he
thought such a grasp could he acquired by practice, or
indicated difference in race. He said he thought it
might be got by practice Then there was some
fairly good sleight-of-hand juggling of little interest ;
ending with a dance by the juggler, first as an animal
and then as a goblin. Now, there was this great differ-
ance between the Japanese masks and our common pan-
tomime masks for beasts and demons—that our English
masks are only stupidly and loathsomely ugly, by exag-
gerations of feature, or defects of feature. But the
Japanese masks (like the frequent monsters of Japanese
art) were inventively frightful, like fearful dreams ;
and whatever power it is that acts on human minds,
enabling them to invent such, appears to me not
only to deserve the term “demoniacal,” but to be logi-
cally capable of no other definition. The impression,
therefore, produced upon me by the whole scene, was
that of being in the presence of human creatures of a
partially inferior race, but not without great human
gentleness, domestic affection, and ingenious intellect,
who were, nevertheless, as a nation, afflicted by an evil
spirit, and driven by it to recreate themselves in achiev-
ing, or beholding the achievement, through years of prac-
tice, of a certain correspondence with the nature of the
lower animals.
And hear him on another subject which is of
topical interest: —
No religion that ever was preached on this earth of
God’s founding ever proclaimed any salvation to sellers
of bad goods. . . . You cannot make punishment too
stern for subtle knavery. For light weights and false
measure, or for proved adulteration, or dishonest manu-
facture of article, the penalty should be simply confisca-
tion of goods, and sending out of the country.
Let us have plenty of Ruskin at a penny.
Australia cannot deport the manufacturers of the
wickedest goods which this country consumes, but
it could at least confiscate the adulterated stuff
they consign to the fat Foreign-traders who im-
pede the advance of native industry.
Victoria’s new Senator, Russell, makes a
good start: —
I don’t want to be recognised as a mere barracker for
my State. I want to assist in developing the whole of
Australia. I am an Australian Protectionist—not a
revenue tariffist. I want to see two-thirds of the
£50,000,000 of imports that now come into Australia
manufactured in Australia by white people, earning
decent wages. In what State the goods are manufac-
tured I do not care, so long as they are produced in
Australia.
In the whole British empire there is now,
according to the Spectator (London), only one
firm that manufactures revolvers. All the rest
of the trade has gone to Protectionist countries.
Possibly a time will come when Freetrade Britain
will import all its weapons and ammunition, for
the rifle industry is also fading; and then the
Australian factory at Lithgow may get a chance
under the Federal tariff to supply Bull, our
father, with the guns that defend old England’s
shore.


One Hundred and Eighteenth Half-Yearly Report of the
Commercial Banking Company of Sydney
-LIMITED.
Presented to the Shareholders at an Ordinary General Meeting held at the
Head Banking House, George-street, Sydney, on Friday, 19th July, 1907.
THE Directors submit to the Shareholders a Balance-sheet showing the Liabilities and Assets of the Bank on the
1 30th June, 1907, and present the following Report:—
The balance at the credit of Profit and Loss Account, after deducting rebate on current bills and accrued
interest on fixed deposits, reducing premises account, paying note tax, and providing for land ami income taxes and
bad and doubtful debts, amounts to £97 659 19 2
out of which the Directors recommend the following appropriations :
To Payment of a Dividend for the half-year at the rate of lOpercent. per annum £50,000 0 0
„ Transfer to Reserve Fund 20 000 0 0
„ Balance being undivided Profit ” ’’ 27 659 19 2
97,659 19 2
At an Extraordinary Genera] Meeting, held on the 19th April, and subsequently confirmed at another Extra-
ordmary General Meeting on the 10th May last, it was decided to increase the Paid-up Capital of the Hank from
£1,000,000 to £1,250,000, by the creation of 20,000 new shares to be allotted to the Shareholders at a premium of

SPECIAL RESOLUTION.
Mr. E. W. Knox moved the following resolution: —
“ That the Directors be requested to procure a portrait of Mr. T. A. Dibbs, to be hung in the Banking Room as a
remembrance of his long and successful term of office as General Manager, and that a service of plate be
presented to Mr. Dibbs in commemoration of the completion of the sixtieth year of his employment by th
Bank.” (Applause.) e
Mr. Knox said : In proposing the resolution which I have just read, I would first say that I submit this not as
one who has on several occasions served on the Board, not as a shareholder and customer of nearly forty years’
standing, but as the son of the man who appointed Mr. Dibbs as junior officer, and who for more than half a century
was himself intimately connected with the development of the business which is the pride of all of us.
As Mr. Dibbs entered the Bank three months after I was born—two or three hundred yards from here—l cannot
claim to have known him during the whole term of his service, but I was not very old when I began to hear my
ather speaking of his ability and his interest in the work, and I know that he never afterwards ceased tn held
highest opinion of your General Manager or to recognise the great services he had rendered to the Rant h ° ld the

ABRIDGED PROSPECTUS
OF
TheTewksbury Consolidated. Gold Dredging
Company, Limited., (T ° be RegistereA)
CAPITAL, £66,500 IN 66,500 SHARES AT £1 EACH.
32,300 Shares, fully paid up, are to be handed to the Vendors in part payment for the properties described below.
34 200 Shares are offered for sale on the following terms : 10s. per share cash on application, and 10s. per share
cash on allotment, of which sum £33,200 in cash is to be paid to the vendors, leaving £lOOO
in hand.

THE NATIONAL BANK OF AUSTRALASIA, LIMITED, Sydney and Bright (Vic.).
Secretary pro tem. s
E. H. SULLY (of Kent, Brierley & Sully), 50 The Strand, Sydney, N.S.W.
