T. J. THOMPSON & SONS, 92 Pitt Street, Sydney.
A. WARREN CLARK, Tuckett Chambers, Collins Street, Melbourne.
This Company is being'formed to consolidate and take over as a going concern, as from July Ist, 1907, the Assets,
except cash and gold in hand, of the Tewksbury, Tewksbury Freehold, Eurobin and Freeburgh Gold Dredging Com-
panies, including five dredges and all dredging rights over 1000 acres of auriferous land, on which the dredges are
working in the valley of the Ovens River, near Bright, Victoria.
All preliminary legal, flotation, and advertising expenses and brokerage will be met by the vendors, leaving the
new Company the'profits clear as from the Ist July, 1907.
The Company will be tfividend-paying at once, as there are four dredges already working, and the fifth will be
under steam by the end of the year.
The dredges are modern plants, in first-class working order, and the whole of the land is very suitable for
dredging.
Three of the dredges have been at work for an average period of fifteen months, and in that time have earned a
profit; of 60 per cent, on the capital invested in them. . . , ~ . ,
On the basis of the work already done, it is expected that during the ensuing sixteen years the ground under
offer will yield over 125,000 ounces of gold, worth more than £500,000. Neatly half of this amount (or according to
the calculations made, £233,660) is expected to be profit, giving an average of £14,115 per annum, equal to 21 per
Cen *The statements °o't BMk « «*** I***.
BnK ih e proposition is not of a speculative nature, but is regarded as a sound investment which will return sub-
stantial and regular dividends.
All shares are fully paid up, and there is no liability attaching to them.
Th e*! h are hold ers° in 6 th e < ori gfn a? Companies, to whom cash is payable by the Vendors, have the option of taking
shares in the new Company in lieu of cash, and some of the largest holders have already expressed their intention
° f a The^hole e oTfhe%ripe h rties P he°rei'n described were originally taken up by Mr. P W. Tewksbury, who personally
prospected them, and! after satisfying himself that they were payable propositions formed Companies to work same
He is also the promoter of the well-known Ovens Valley Company, and now controls no less than seven dredges, and
has a record of never having floated a “ duffer ”or “ wild cat.” His Tewksbury dredge holds the record for Vic-
toria for one week’s yield, viz. .-—133 ounces for 120 hours’ work. The Freehold No. 2 secured 101 ounces for a
similar period Mr. Tewksbury’s personal energy, enterprise, and foresight are alone responsible for his immense
success At the present time he holds the Managing Directorship of all the Companies herein described and which
it is proposed to amalgamate, and is willing to retain the same position in the Consolidated Company, if so desired,
so chat all dredging operations will continue to be under his personal supervision.
The National Bank of Australasia Ltd.

WHAT IS CATARRH ?
CATARRH is inflammation of the lining
membrane of the nose and adjoining
passages. If this inflammation is not ar-
rested it invades the passages which lead
from the nose to the head, ears, throat and
lungs. It injures the sight and hearing,
destroys the sense of taste and smell, ren-
ders the breath offensive, breaks down
the affected tissues, and consumes the
nasal cartilages. The discharge, passing
through the lungs and stomach, causes
dyspepsia, also consumption. Do you
want relief and cure? If so, try our
great remedy.

ma. Write for booklet free, or send

“ Chibur ” : A parade of the metropolitan
troops of Queensland was arranged for the 26th
July, but was cancelled owing to the football
match! .It is to be hoped that a time will come
when it will be the leather-kicking match that
will be postponed in such cases.
The importers have struck a new idea, and
are filling the credulous old S.M. Herald up
with it. Every day there are paragraphs setting
out that goods are really imported as soon as they
have reached their port of destination, so in case
of an increase of duties they “should be permitted
to enter the Commonwealth at the old rates, the
increase to apply to subsequent arrivals only.”
Then, having hammered that in for a few days,
the further interesting suggestion is made that
the goods are really imported as soon as the ship
that brings them has touched Fremantle. But
why stop there? Why not say they’re imported
as soon as the Australian Calico Jimmy has sent
off his order to the foreigner? Or if Calico
James can swear that he had decided to send an
order, why shouldn’t that count, too ? Calico
James’ struggle to dodge ninepenn’orth of duty is
too pathetic, and is liable to tie the Customs
Department into knots if it listens to him. The
universal practice by which goods are imported
when the import duty is paid ought to be good
enough for any Department that doesn’t want to
get itself in a tangle.
W. J. Ferguson, who used to be one of
the bright members of the N.S.W. Labor party,
and had hopes once of being its leader, turned up
the other day as a candidate for the Carruthers
nomination at Botany. Still Ferguson hasn’t
fallen as far as he might have done—he didn’t
get the nomination. The local leagues preferred
an alderman.

Planetary Professor Foretells
Events, and Quickly Reads the
Lives of People, though Thou-
sands of Miles Away.
Sends Letters to the Rich and Poor
Alike, in which he advises them about
BUSINESS, MARRIAGE, SPECULATION,
LOVE AFFAIRS, WEALTH, ETC.
Offers Readings to all who write and
send Date of Birth, Sex, Married or
Single, with 1/- to cover cost of
postage, etc.
IN his office in Melbourne, surrounded by
Charts and Dials of strange design, Prof.
Henri De Astro studies daily over the lives
of men and women who have written him for
advice on affairs of business, love, speculation,
travel, marriage, health, and the important
events of life. The following letter gives an
idea of
MR. DE ASTRO’S ABILITY.
My Advice Brought Money.
Brisbane.
I am proud to tell you that I have profited greatly
by following-the advice contained in my Horoscope.
I had about given up taking a journey, but when you
advised it so strongly, I reconsidered and took the
journey, passed my examinations, and am now in a
position to earn from £2O to £25 per month. I would
not take £lO for the advice you gave me.
I believe every young person starting in life should
by all means secure a Horoscope from you. Nothing
I ever received has given me such power to control
conditions and circumstances as your Horoscope.
Wishing you every success, I am, yours truly,
B. H. RANDALL.
THE accuracy of recent predictions made by
this eminent Astrologer has caused many
of his friends to believe that he possesses
a supernatural power, but he modestly asserts
that his predictions are due alone to a scien-
tific understanding of natural laws. The many
thankful letters Prof. De Astro has received
from people who have benefited by his advice
furnish ample proof that he is sincere in his
work and has a kindly feeling towards
humanity.
Readers of The Bulletin can obtain a
Reading by addressing a letter to Prof. De
Astro, 366 Bourke Street, Melbourne. Simply
say you wish a Reading of your Life, stating
your birth date, sex, and whether married
or single, enclosing Is. Stamps or Postal Note
to pay postage etc., and the Reading will
be promptly sent.

“ Hamer ” : Something is happening to
Syria in Redfern (Sydney). That locality,
which is so much Syria that an R.C. church
in the neighborhood has its dedication re-
peated in Syrian characters, is becoming full
of empty shops. Syria is migrating. The
hope is that it is moving out of Australia, but
that is, possibly, too good to be true. Still, it
may be that the recent iniquitous legislation
against the importation of shoddy is spoiling the
Syrian’s business. A Chinaman, pointing to
the closed gambling dens in the Chows’ part of the
city, told me recently that tyrannical legislation
was driving capital out of the country.
“M. Macgregor ” on color :
Professor Macmillan Brown, of Maoriland, has given it
as his opinion that the Australian is turning black, and
he is deservedly a scoffed-at man in consequence. All
the same, it is likely that the future Australian will show
a marked differentiation of color from the average
European. The Jew of Palestine, who is racially identical
with the Syrian who carries his pack through the country
districts of Australia, is many degrees darker than
the Jew from England, or Germany, • or Russia ;
yet the latter has had less than a thousand years
in which to divest himself of his tawniness. And it
is much easier to take on color than to lose it. The sun-
bather acquires his deep-brown tint in a week or two, yet
he often retains much of it when the next season starts ;
and, in addition, the Australian is generally an individual
who courts the sunlight, whereas the Oriental shuns it.
The first thing the Hindu does when he can afford it
is to buy an umbrella, to shelter his head from the
rays that the Australian delights to bask in.
Dago, but the man whose skin is brown on the surface is
an Australian every time. The visitor to London can
pick out his compatriots even before he hears them speak.
It is odds on, if you meet a brown man in Fleet-street,
and ask him if he’s an Australian, his face will brighten
and he’ll ariswer “ Yair.” The future Australian will be
brown ; but, as for being black, that is another question,
The true Arab has lived in the North of Africa longer
than history can record, but he is no blacker than the
Sydney youth after a summer on the sands of Bondi,
The Caucasian seems to have a maximum color point
beyond which the sun rays have no effect.

Business, Bobbery, etc.
N.S.W. Grocers’ Association has discovered
that monopoly is a bad thing. Just now the retail grocer
has a monopoly of the discredit attached to a prosecution
for selling impure foodstuffs. He has also a monopoly of
the bad advertisement, and for the first time in "the
history of the Ass. he has objected violently. He wants
the discredit distributed equally between the wholesaler
and the manufacturer, and himself left out in the cold.
Although the wholesaler at present pays all the fines, the
retailer finds that the advertisement is a killing one. He
maintains, with solid horse-sense, that it is easier for the
wholesaler to analyse his stock than for the struggling
retailer to do so. In all probability the wholesaler will
wantto pass the advertisement along to the manufacturer,
but as that disreputable person is largely domiciled
abroad, and beyond the reach of the local courts, the
public will not derive much satisfaction if he is left to
bear the brunt alone. Even his bad advertisement is
easily overcome—the news of the prosecution does not
circulate very widely. Of course, if Australia did practi-
cally all its own manufacturing-, and had its manufacturer
here where the police could get at him, they would
naturally go for him, and not for the mere retailer.
Therefore, all retail grocers should cry out for 100 per
cent. Protection, so that the manufacturer will be here.
* » • •
“Victim ” : Cast a casual curse at the Barrios
Diamond Co., which for some three years has been selling
mock jewellery (on a sliding scale) to many people in
Melbourne. The “ Barriosities,” glittering under a tricky
arrangement of electric light, were originally sold at 245.
or £1 per brooch, ring, shirt stud, or what not, on the
understanding that they were rather high-class imita-
tions of precious stones with honestly-gilt settings. As
time went on the shop—shifting from one side of
Swanston-street to t’other—offered the same old class of
goods at a diminishing price. The 20s. fell to 125., then
to Bs., then to 45., then 25., and now the remnant of the
stock is being cleared out at a “bob” per article,
which appears to be about fair value, on an
average. Jones buys for one shilling a dupli-
cate of, say, a mock diamond stud which cost
Brown twenty times the price, three years ago.
But whilst Jones is congratulating himself on liis
comparative bargain purchase, he is likely to be had
with the offer of an alleged “ 16-carat gold-shell watch-
chain, filled with rolled gold,” which is “ warranted ” to
preserve its genuine appearance for half a lifetime. A
test of one of these chains reveals it as brass, gold-
washed. Its ordinary retail price, at Brummagem-g-oods
houses, is half-a-crown. And in selling this at ten
shillings to-day the “ Barrios ” shopkeeper states—truth-
fully, no doubt—that his original price for it was £2.
The vaguely-worded “ guarantee ” given with these extra-
special two-and-sixpenny watch-chains, now reduced
from £2 to ten shillings, should bring the proceedings
within the reach of the Law, but the game of legal pro-
ceedings is not worth the candle.
* * * •
Dr. Macdonald, the “white” sugar enthusiast,
has been letting in a little light on the monopolistic
Colonial Sugar Co. According to him, the co. pays
£8 10s. per ton for the raw material at the refinery,
spends another 30s. on the refining, and sells the finished
product at £26. And only a few weeks back the glut-
tonous co. advanced sugar another 10s. per ton, “ owing
to the advance in the price of cane.”
* * * •
Experiments in growing cigar leaf tobacco,
made by Teitzel Bros., of Bowen (Q.), under the guidance
of Queensland Government Expert Neville, have come
out all right so far. From about an acre and a quarter,
a ton and a half has been harvested, and if it is worth the
alleged Bd. to lid. per lb., there is money in the business.
Hitherto the trouble with tobacco growers has been that
the prices have rarely, if ever, come up to expectations ;
as with opals when the gouger finds the buyer, there has
always been something shocking the matter with the
stuff. Still, the Teitzels intend planting a larger area,
and other farmers thereabouts intend giving up growing
maize, potatoes, and tomatoes to go in for the cultiva-
tion of the combustible stuff that made Dowie call out
“ stinkpot.”
* * * •
European manufacturers of shoddy explosives
used to be troubled by the failure of their rubbish to
pass the Abel test—the only generally recognised test of
the safety and stability of blowing-up substances. They
discovered, however, that the addition of a little mercury
bichloride—as little as one part in 200,000 —enabled the
low-grade explosive to pass the test, which rests
on the application of heat. Dr. Dupre, the most
eminent European analyst of explosives, could find
no perfectly satisfactory method of discovering the
fraud till he fell back on a spectroscopic device, and even
that fails to reveal how much of the adulterant is present.
The Chief Inspector of Explosives in England lately
informed the Government Analysts at Brisbane and
Adelaide that shipments of adulterated explosives were
on their way. The shipments were stopped, and upon
examination by the spectroscopic method, were found to
be shoddy. Both Australian analysts thereupon set to
work to discover a chemical method of discerning the
mercury, and both succeeded. By Henderson’s method
Henderson is the Brisbane analyst—the presence of such
a small quantity as one part in 1,000,000 can be dis-
cerned. This is another proof that, in almost any branch
of research or industry, the Australian can hold his own.
“ Hassan ” : An extraordinary species of trades-
man called at our house yesterday. His line was a new
corn-cure—“ perfec’ly ’armless.” To prove this, he ate
half a cake with simulated relish. He informed me that
a parson’s wife in Balmain had cured all her corns with it
in one night—hundreds of corns, thousands of corns—
speech failed him as he thought of the multitudinous
corns of the parson’s wife at Balmain, and he waved his
arms helplessly. We learned also that upon one occasion
a “gentleman” insisted upon him (the tradesman)
personally applying the remedy to the toe of the
lady of the house. “Naow, that was a nice thing
to ask, wasn’t it, sir?” Here he blushed. This stuff
would also cure warts. That was how he discovered it.
As a boy he played with frogs—result, warts all over his
body. Now he hadn’t one—he invited the servant girl to
search him. She refused with much indignation. I
asked him would it cure bankruptcy, and when he said
it wouldn’t I let the dog loose, and be dashed round to
the gate with halt a cake of his invention in his mouth,
and desperation in his eye. The dog ate one of the pack-
ages he shed as he ran, and seemed rather to enjoy it.
ihe steamer Ferret, which is still dancing
round the coast, came to Australian waters under
peculiar circumstances 26 years ago. It arrived
at Hobson’s Bay in 1881 under the name of the India,
but was promptly identified as the same old Ferret—a
vessel stolen in Scotland the previous year. The enter-
prising ship-lifters had chartered the steamer at Glasgow
for a yachting cruise in the Mediterranean, and had for-
gotten to take it back again. Instead of going to the
Mediterranean, the picnickers steered for the Cape, thence
to Mauritius, thence to Melbourne. Suspicions were
aroused in Melbourne, with the result that the bogus
owners were arrested for petty larceny of the Great
Eastern, or something. The vessel was not sent
back to Scotland, but was sold here. The Ferret
is now 36 years old, and is owned by the Adelaide
Steamship Co. For many years it was in the South
Australian Gulf trade, but has lately been on the Wes-
tralian coast, plodding between Albany and Esperance.
* * * * .
I he high price of copper has started a new in-
dustry in England—stealing telegraph wire. Some of
the heavy wires run about 2001 b. to the mile, and
thus a few hundred yards are worth sundry drinks. The
thieves have to work in exposed positions, but along
canal sides and in quiet country places there is a steady
cutting and carting away.
* * * *
“ Dejected ” : The other day I went into a shop
in Adelaide to order a suit of clothes. The boss of the
establishment put before my gaze about a dozen rolls of
different alleged all-wool tweeds—Scotch, English and
sundries. I asked him to show me some Australian
tweeds, and he replied briefly, “ Don’t stock’em. Can’t
sell’em. No good.” When I inquired why they were
no good, he told me, after some hesitation, that they were
all wool. I replied that I wanted all wool, and that he had
just been boasting that the imported stuff he showed me
was all wool. Then he struggled hard in his mind for a
feasible narrative, and at last he got it. “Oh, yes,” he
said, “ but you see they get the pick of the world’s wool
in England, and the Australian wool isn’t so good.”
Asked him where the better wool came from, and he
waved his hands vaguely and said, “Oh, all sorts of
places.”
* * • *
“Bondite”: On the La Perouse tram, the
other day, I ran across an aged fisherman who ought to
be a Standard Oil Trust, or something of the sort. He
was Anti-Sosh personified-the spirit of sturdy indivi-
dualism and all the daily paper virtues condensed into
about nine-stone-two of stringy manhood. Also, the odor
of the basket he bore was sufficient to gain him admission
into the caucuses of commerce. He sat in one corner of
a tram, smoking vigorously. I was the only other occu-
pant of the car until reaching Kensington, when a party
of elegantly-caparisoned youths hopped on board. One
drew out a gold cigarette-case and offered it to his
pals. “No, old fellah, try one of mine,” said the
second. “ No, one of mine,” protested the third,
Eventually they effected a general exchange of
cigarettes that seemed satisfactory to all, and then
found that they hadn’t a match among them. They
appealed to me. I was in the same predicament. They
asked the driver. He turned out to be president
of an anti-cigarette league. They asked the aged
fisherman. “Mebbe I have, an’ mebbe I haven’t,”
observed the astute financier, dragging a dirty box from
his pocket. “ It’s this way, gents,” he continued. “No
offence, but business is business. I’m sellin’ these ’ere
matches. I’m takin’one o’them cigarettes from each o’
you gents fur three matches. No fear,” he added, as the
nearest proffered a cigarette. “If it’s all the same ter
yous, I’ll take the three at onst. I’m not sayin’ anythin’,
yer know ; but you might give the rest a light, an’
where’d I be? The three at onst. Thank yer, gents,
Kin I do anything in the fish line with yer ter-day ? ”

“Hassan”: How’s this for meanness? A
Sydney society woman, whom I could name but won’t,
secured an eider-down quilt “on appro.” at a big draper’s
shop. She sent it back two months later, saying it
wouldn’t do, and the collapsed assistant struggled feebly
as they loosened his collar. His last words were : “ It’s
the limit 1 ” I think it was.

* * * *
When you get a parcel of goods “on appro”
is is just as well to see if the tradesman has acci-
dntally dropped a figure or two on to the paper
wrapping. Some English wool people sent a parcel
to possible buyers, and there were figures on the
paper—figures representing several pence under what
the wool was said to be worth. The buyers promptly
wired acceptance at the price quoted on the package,
The wool people said there wasn’t any price on the pack-
age ; but, when the buyers produced the paper with the
figures on it, the Judge found for them ; and the Brad-
ford Chamber of Commerce is mad about it, because the
wool people say that they wrapped the sample up in any
old paper, and that the figures just happened to
be on the old paper. Possibly an Australian Court
wouldn’t necessarily follow what a Judge at Leicester
did ; still, if you get a parcel of diamonds “ on appro,”
and “ 2d.” happens to be marked on the paper, it would
be worth sending a wire stating that you will take the
lot—just on spec, so to speak,

“Dux” from the North Island: I have been
overwhelmed by the flood-tide of weird expectancy. A
Maori, after pressing his nose against the shop window
for a considerable time, entered and delivered himself
thus: “You got te spect’le to suit all sights, pboss?”
I said I had, and invited him into my parlor, as the spider
did to the fly that proved to be a wasp. His case, a
simple one of presbyopia, was soon corrected, and a
satisfied customer lit out for “ te pah,” while I put £1 in
the till. It was good business, something like 1000 per
cent., and I shouted myself to the play that night. But
the end was not yet. Next morning the warrior, now
filled with a violent anger, burst into the shop, with quite
a tribe of dusky ones ; and all demanded with much
gesticulation, “Why you sellem te spect’le to suit all
sights?” “ Why, what’s the matter?” I shouted back at
them. “ You te prurry rogue ! We fetcliem te polishman
prurry quick,” they shrieked. “What the adjective
blank’s wrong with you? Has the blanky tribe gone
mad?” I bawled. “Me tellem you what te matter
prurry quick,” spoke the warrior. “You sellum te
spect’le to suit all sights, yes. Te spect’le orlri’to me,
yes. Te spect’le no tarn goot to Hinemoa ; no tarn goot
to Hape; no tam goot to Tohara, te pig chief; no tarn
goot to all te Maori, no”; and with vehement threaten-
ing—“ You givem te quid back prurry quick. Wefetch’etn
te polishman. Berrar look out! ” There were too many
ot them, and they looked like breaking the stock. I
wearily handed over 19s. and a Boer war medal, receiving
ln return a dilapidated pair of spectacles which had
evidently been tried on by the whole tribe. You can’t
reason Maorn

The Jap is a strenuous trader all right, T other
day, at Frisco, while the Health people were fumigating
some packing cases in a vessel’s hold, they heard the
cargo break out into fits of sneezing. They had hfever

“ Thataroa ” : There is a scandalous boom in
land in Maoriland just now, prices reaching £4O per acre,
mostly through “ the cow ” ; and a Government grader
goes out of his way to say the figures are justified by
butter prices. Butter prices, perhaps; but what about
the price of human life?
THE IVANHOE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The thousand pounder’s beating
Thrills through and through the camps,
The hills around repeating
This thunder of the stamps.
Like hearts of giants breaking,
The pistons grunt and sigh,
The stormy music making
A miner’s battle-cry.
'Tis like the roaring ocean,
With wave and tide allow—
This sound and force and motion,
Upon the Ivauhoe.
The belts and shafts are singing,
As tireless runs the gear,
And strident tones are ringing
Upon the atmosphere.
“ Shake up that damned condenser 1 ”
We mustn’t stop, you know,
Or a thousand horse-power’s loafing,
Upon the Ivanhoe.
Out, in, and round about here,
It’s clang, and crash, and din ;
We tear the metal out here,
And cram the forest in.
A gale upon the surface,
A hurricane below,
It’s no place for the nervous—
This roaring Ivanhoe.
We risk our lives for clobber,
And sorts of tucker. We
Provide the swell and jobber
With gold for gay Paree.
Would but some burly statesman
Bid these Usurpers go,
'Twould pay our country’s debts, man ;
This gold from Ivanhoe.
The thousand pounder’s beating
Thrills through and through the camps,
The hills around repeating
This thunder of the stamps,
Like hearts of giants breaking,
The pistons grunt and sigh,
The stormy music making
A miner’s battle-cry.
’Tis like the roaring ocean,
With wave and tide allow,
This constant roar and motion,
Upon the Ivanhoe.
WESTRAMA.
M. J. CALLANAN.

REMARKABLE
INVENTION
FOR THE
CULTURE
OF HAIR
CHE EVANS VACUUM CAP is a practical invention constructed on scientific and
hygienic principles, by the simple means of which a free and normal circulation
is restored throughout the scalp. The minute blood vessels are gently stimulated
to activity, thus allowing the food supply, which can only be derived from the blood,
to be carried to the hair roots, the effects of which are quickly seen in a healthy, vigorous
growth of hair. There is no rubbing, and as no drugs or chemicals of whatsoever kind
are employed, there is nothing to cause irritation. It is only necessary to wear the Gap
three or four minutes daily.
60 DAYS’ FREE TRIAL!
The Company's Guarantee*
An EVANS VACUUM CAP will be sent you for sixty days’ free
trial. If you do not see a gradual development of a new growth of hair,
and are not convinced that the Cap will completely restore your hair, you
are at liberty to return the Cap with no expense whatever to yourself. It
is requested, as an evidence of good faith, that the price of the Cap be
deposited with the Chancery Lane Safe Deposit Company of London, the
largest financial and business institution of the kind in the world, who will
issue a receipt guaranteeing that the money will be returned in full, on
demand, without questions or comment, at any time during the trial period.
The eminent Dr. I. N. LOVE, in his address to the Medical Board on the subject of Alopsecia (loss of
hair) stated that if a moans could be devised to bring nutrition to the hair follicles (hair roots), witnout
resorting to any irritating process, the problem of hair growth would be solved. Later on, wnen
the EVANS VACUUM CAP was submitted to him for inspection, he remarked that the Cap would
fulfil and confirm in practice the observations he had previously made before the Medical Board.
Dr. W. MOORE, referring to the invention, says that the principle upon which the Evans Vacuum
Cap is founded is absolutely correct and indisputable.

An Order for a Song.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
YOU know not, lady, what you will,
Or what an eerie throng
I have to summon to fulfil
Your order for a song.
They heard you in the outer waste,
And, throbbing with desire
To press their claims on me, they haste
In coracles of fire.
You do not know their ardent pleas,
These strange poetic norms,
To leave their filmy destinies
And incarnate in forms.
They’ve waited through the ages, some,
They’ve knocked at every door
The bards have shown, but deaf or dumb
Have all men seemed before.
And some had been half-heard, but yearn
In riper ears to say
What seers were then too young to learn,
Or too alarmed to weigh.
And all the dreams you yearn to sing,
But somehow fail to voice.
Leave you anon and to me spring
To influence my choice.
So norms from you and norms from me
Their wedding rites rehearse,
And lead their children silently
To cradle in a verse.
Around the door of mind they veer,
Its threshold ’neath, above;
And there are misty wraiths of Fear,
And golden clouds of Love.
For Poets (that all strive to be
Who view the Vision Real)
Are but the doors ajar they see
Through which they yearn to steal.
The worlds they live in have, like ours,
Their life and land and sea,
Imagination’s blue-rimmed towers,
And vales of reverie;
Germ-thoughts in races group themselves,
In dim migrations go;
Faint fairy duties war with elves
Of sin in embryo.
So, while your order flutters to
My chambers of the mind.
These things that are to be throng through,
Their human robes to find.
They bribe the wayside grassy blades
Their wishes to suggest;
I note their tiny ambuscades
In smile, in heaving breast.
Yea, some employ such subtle wiles
To prove to me their worth,
They launch my boat on eerie Niles
And row me from the earth,
Till larger continents we find,
And stars that woo me, burn
Above their land of “ Absent Mind,”
By the world of “ No-Return” :
Till we alone are real, and all
The stormy facts of day
Into Illusion’s chasm fall,
A rainbow o’er its spray ;
Till so my boatmen’s ichors surge
Through being and through thought
(’Tis here, they say, with God we merge)
That Earth becomes as nought.
But then, with instantaneous skill,
Down earth-ward currents strong
They row me, lady, to fulfil
Your order for a song.
VIO.
BERNARD O'DOWD.
The Night Hawks’ Retreat,
A MELBOURNE “CAFE DE NUIT.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
WHEN the theatre lights are out, the
clocks have struck 12, and the last
train is not lost, but gone before ; when
the chic cafes, restaurants, and oyster saloons are
closed ; when the last glass has been drained and
the last butt tossed away ; when, in his club room,
the dude removes his skin-tight trousers, and the
respectable ratepayer is already snoring in his
suburban villa—then, when the moon looks down
on the lonely river, and the deserted streets glitter
under the electric lights, the prowlers of the night
come into their own.
Melbourne is not a late city. Like London, it
“douses the glim” somewhere about midnight.
Berlin and Vienna and New York keep moving
always; and in Paris, at the Montmartre “cafes
de nuit,” Bohemia drinks bock, smokes, cake-
walks, and abolishes the Symbolists till six in the
morning.
But we be busy folk, slaves of the day’s work.
All things revolve in the light o’ the sun. Except
some stately John and some late and delaying
drunk, hardly an alien soul can be seen poaching
on the night-hawks’ preserves.
“ What about supper?” observes the Mystic.
The motion is put and carried. We leave the
Hall of Dialectics.
“Isn’t the night lovely?” exclaims a Damosel
of the Crayon. “ Why do people ever go to bed?”
After midnight the city sleeps. Through the
luminous mist buildings rise huge and mysterious;
all harsh outlines are softened by the magic of
night. The bridge is strangely etched against the
violet sky. Yellow lights twinkle; the river
flows sullenly beneath. The long, deserted streets,
marked like a chessboard, stretch into the romantic
darkness.
No pushes are about; lately they have kept to
the suburbs. A tall “cop” stands under a verandah
watching a house opposite ; perhaps it is a two-up
school he means to raid. A cabby passes, whip
in hand, following a prospective fare, said fare
being a youtig man who seems to have considerable
philosophic doubt both as to where he is ar.d
where he wants to go. In the shadow of a door-
way an old battered derelict lies sleeping. Three
bedraggled women shout drunkenly at the corner.
In the middle of the road a swaggie, with bluey and
billy, stares hopelessly around. He is bushed.
“ Hang the coffee-stall! Let’s go to Guido’s,”
remarks the Busy Journalist. “That’s the only
place for supper. ”
The Red-Headed Sport relates two of his
youthful adventures at this famous hash-house.
The company is impressed.
A glittering window starts out in the darkness.
This is the Night Hawks’ Retreat, a highly
popular all-night restaurant. We find the place
in full swing. Guido accords us a joyous welcome.
The fire blazes; pots and pans rattle; steaks are
being grilled, eggs boiled, sausages fried ; cus-
tomers bawl their orders. The interesting pro-
prietor, a short, powerfully-built, tawny-skinned,
humorous Dago, leans over the counter and shouts,
“ All-aright, give er bloke-a er chance.”
Even in the back rooms set apart for the more
distinguished visitors a pungent odor of cooking
thickens the already tobacco-clouded air. The
Crayon Damosel is interested.
Big brown stains splatter the sawdust floor and
the flyblown kalsomined walls. Deal bars, form-
ing a kind of shutter, block the window in order
to protect the glass from missiles that passionate
patrons fling at one another promiscuously.
It is a hot, steamy night. Lazy blowflies crawl
round the walls, and drop casually from the ceil-
ing into the hot food. On the swab, with its
stuffing oozing out, curious cockroaches lie in
ambush. A battered kerosene tin serves as a
scrap bucket.
Round the room there are prints and oleographs
of prize fighters and Dago generals. Over the
mantelpiece is a marvellous battle scene between
the Greeks and Turks, a modern Marathon in
which the Hellenes appear like gaudy dolls from
a Noah’s Ark. Placards bear the legends that
“All meals must be paid for on delivery,” and
“Gents are requested not to spit.”
Guido carts round the dishes. How the plates
clatter and the patrons bellow ! It is a revel of
sausages fried in fat, steak and chips, bacon and
eggs, tripe and onions, fried mullet —all hot from
the kitchen, and washed down with chicory for
coffee, tannin for tea, and flour for cocoa.
And what a wondrous crowd gorges round these
deal ta.bles —men and women, old and young, of
all nationalities seemingly and of many classes !
That absurdly fat man, with cheeks puffed out
like a bladder, is a German bassoon player. The
dandy-colored coon opposite, toying with a bunch
of tripe, is a professional pug.
Among the crowd we have a Yankee morphia
maniac—a highly educated man, once manager
of a big business, but now a derelict, all brought
about by the drug. Every hour or so he injects
the poison into his system, till his neck and arms
and throat are but one mass of ghastly punctures.
A brace of spielers add spice to the company.
One is Benno the Bird. Benno wears a silver
watch chain with horseshoe locket attached,
sharp-pointed jockey boots, and a red, white and
blue buttonhole ; he sports a gaudy-colored
“neck wi]ie ” flung round his throat; and he
tilts his “lid” far to the back of his head in order
to display the lovely hair, well oiled and arranged
according to some strict geometrical design, de-
corating his low, sloping forehead.
A few unfortunate women, some merely girls,
shout and curse with their blokes.
Bookies, touts, jockeys, journalists, gamblers,
J’s.P., barristers, doctors—members of all the
professions and none—call at one time or another
at Guido’s when they are having a night out.
Nothing astonishes Guido. A man of the
world, he has the same smile for the violinist as
for the pavement artist; the same for the pug as
for the heavy tragedian. ’
A wonderful man, this Guido. A Greek, he
has been to most countries on the map; he has
tried many trades, from running a brewery round
Spencer’s Gulf and pearl-fishing at Torres Straits,
to cutting scrub in Gippsland and running a hash
house in Melbourne. He has been over 20 years
in Australia, and has always had money. He is
a man who could pick up coins in Collins-street.
Guido is afraid of nothing that walks. Under
stress of circumstances, he fired out a middle-
weight wrestler. The wrestler was a little the
worse for liquor, ’tis true, yet it was a daring feat
long remembered by his clients.
And Guido is a philosopher.
“My son ’e wos—a ” he begins.
There is a disturbance in front. Guido cocks
his ears and sallies forth to suppress the riot.
“ Himmelkreuzdonnerwetter,” observes the
Teutonic musician. It sounded like a ’Frisco
’quake. A blowfly had committed suicide in his
stew —that was all.
During the disturbance a “ mixed double ” rise
and ask Guido, in matter-of-fact tones, to keep
their steak hot.
“We’ll be back in ten minutes,” says the lady.
Guido continues. “ I not believe in ze educa-
zion. Make my son too dam-a lazy. I put ’im in
three shoppa. ’E knock-a round, never do a
tappa. Zey go bung, bung. I bought ’im er
nice-a little-a farm with ze pig and poultry an’ ze
duck; ’e play ze football all daylonga; ze pig
alia die ; Sacramento Christi, maledetto, ’e costa
me two-a ’undreda quid. ’E ’ad ze educazion. I
kick ’im out, give ’im ze shoota, eh ? ’E drive
a cab-a now. ’E’ll come-a back. Dad es ’is best-a
friend. I should-a smile.”
Our Mystic contends that a supper should be
composed as a complete whole, all details being
chastened and subdued to preserve the general
harmony. No dish should stand out in bold
relief. It should be a thread, the necessary com-
plement to what preceded it, and the gracious in-
troduction to what is to follow. The Red-Headed
Sport is too busy making good with the Crayon
Damosel to heed this subtlety, and the Busy
Journalist tries to talk Homeric Greek to Guido.
“Righto ” —Guido rushes to attend to clamorous
customers.
“This bloomin’flounder orter git ther old ige
pension,” says a disgusted lady in puce.
“Say, Guido,” breaks in the Yank, “these
oysters are game. They’re small, but full o’
fight.”
“ Yon big vat you call de blowfly, qgh, ’e swim
inter de soup, ” —this from the disciple of Wagner
—“vat is dat is wrong mit decatch-flies ? ”
All the crowd chyaels Guido. That’s what he’s
there for, but his genial beam never leaves his
classic dial.
A scramble takes places in t,he corner be-
tween two of the push. Beer talks, and Dirty
Sam reckons he’s another Squires. Guido grabs
Sam by the scruff of the neck, shakes him, and
then empties the “coming champion” out into
the wide, wide world.
“ What can I do-a for you ? ” inquires the chef,
looking round pleasantly before returning to the
eternal frying-pan.
It is time to go.
Outside there is a strong smell of yeast in the
air. The bakers are at work. Smoke rises from
some cool-storage factories. A cock crows shrilly,
heralding the dawn. A tall policeman trudges
slowly past. Carts laden with fruit and vege-
tables rumble towards the market.
“ Good-night, Guido.”
The Mystic pays. He always does. That’s the
sort of mystic we appreciate. The Busy Jour-
nalist has cut out the Red-Headed Sport. The
Damosel Artistique likes variety,
“And ’ow was-a the sand mullet?” beams
Guido.
“ We had the sand,” says the Sport; “ we’ll get
the mullet next time.”
Guido leans over the counter resignedly,
It will soon be dawn. One feels already the
keenness of the morning air. Soon the night-
hawks will slink off to their dens. Red morn
scatters them. Night-hawks are a sorry sight in
the glad light of the sun.
LOUIS ESSON.

Mr. P. Smith, 1 Cheltenham Street, Balmain, Sydney, says :—“I used
Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills for kidney trouble, and they worked a
miracle in my case. I had been under three doctors at different times, but
their treatment did me no good. I used to be so bad that I would have to
be carried home on a cart, if an attack came on when working in the street.
Sometimes I have been taken to the hospital when one of these bad attacks
came on. My wife says she thought that I would never live through some
of these bad turns. I suffered with a kind of biliousness and vomiting.
My secretions were very bad, I could never bend my back or move at all
when I had a bad turn. The people at the hospital gave me medicines,
and recommended rubbing in liniments, but it was of no use, they did not
benefit me. I saw an advertisement for Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills,
and procured this remedy. I only took one box and was cured. When I
finished them I started work, and have worked ever since. It is heavy
work lam doing, but it does not affect me. These pills cured me after
years of suffering, and the relief was almost instantaneous.”
Mrs. Smith says, eight years later : “My husband has had wonderful
health since he used Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills. lam convinced that
he owes his life to this remedy. He takes a dose of the pills now and
again, just as a precaution, and because he thinks the kidneys need a tonic
sometimes. He is now so well, thanks to Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills.

Bulotu.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
TO a happy, heathen haven,
By warm, azure waters laven,
Where the sea and sky are blended
in the Pathway of the Sun,
With his earth’s probation over,
Does the brown man’s soul turn rover,
In pursuit of the reward his earthly pilgrimage
has won.
’Tis a guerdon worth securing ;
Ceaseless happiness ensuring,
Hope’s supernal dreams’ fulfilment, full fruition
of desires;
Life repeating earth’s successes,
While avoiding all its messes,
Life for ever and for ever as the liver’s will
requires.
Free from jealousies and fighting,
For all wrong must find its righting
In that clear, sane understanding that the gods
alone supply;
And as all are gods, and clever,
Peace amongst them reigns forever,
In this realm of loving-kindness, where the
blossoms never die.
Every wished-for boon surrounding,
Here they dwell in luck abounding;
Here they learn at last that rapture which no
mortals ever know;
And, ambrosially nourished,
Thrive as never men have flourished,
As they lave, and drink elation, where the Living
Waters flow.
While loved music’s soothings joy them,
Rhythmic strains that never cloy them,
No harps’ twanging, but the harmonies their
mortal senses knew ;
They can hear their conch-shells blowing,
And their vibrant lalis going,
And their bamboo flutes still tootling as they
always used to do.
To Bulotu find admittance,
On their debt of Nature’s quittance,
All things moving, all things growing, without
query or rebate;
Lasting life they share together,
Man and beast, and fish and feather,
Tree and shrub, and fruit and flower,i n an ama-
ranthine state.
And I like the legend greatly,
It appeals to me innately,
For I’m almost half a heathen, and I always loved
the sea ;
But I’m fretting in a city,
In a frenzy of self-pity,
And I’m weary, very weary, of what Fate has
done for me.
For a “ southerly ” is blowing,
Sydney’s very worst moods showing,
And my head is aching sadly with the streets’ in-
cessant roar;
And I’m longing, oh, so keenly !
Just to sail away serenely.
Just to sail away a-searching for some surf-besil-
vered shore;
Such as circles far Bulotu,
That the dead Kanakas go to;
Such as sparkles ever whitely round these Islands
of the Blest;
Luring islands, all-enthralling.
Which keep calling, calling, calling,
Till the hearer drops his burdens, and departs to
seek their rest.
And the winds are never bitter
Where the greeny-golden glitter
Of bewitching, glad Bulotu stars its silken
summer sea;
In its sweet sun, shining brightly,
All the days glide past so lightly,
That Eternity is Dream time, as Eternity should
be.
So I too would cross the portals
Of the gleeful brown immortals
If they’d only let me enter, if my way there I
could see;
’Tis a decent sort of heaven,
With a lump of earthly leaven,
And I hope that a Bulotu may be waiting yet for
me.
’Twould be pleasant to be thinking,
When my sands of time were sinking,
And old Charon cried a summons, most insist-
ently, for me,
He might waft me in his wherry,
Not across his Stygian ferry,
But away to fair Bulotu, through the lustrous
Coral Sea.
N.S.W.
TALAPEKAU.
Two Rounds.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
JORKINS leaped to his feet with a howl
of rage, and whirled one fat fist past
Johnson’s nose, gleaming with perspira-
tion in the half-darkness.
“ Stop him ! ” shrieked Johnson as he fled over
two chairs. “ Stop him —the coward ! ”
Three shadowy forms, shouting with excitement,
surged up in Jorkins’ path, and bore him back
against the dining-room wall.
“Let me at him!” gasped Jorkins, making
swimming motions among the encircling tide of
lodgers. “Let me—at him ! ”
Instantly there was a babel of excited voices.
“No—no !”
“Stop him ! ”
“ What did he say ? ”
“ Who started it ? ”
“ No, he didn’t! ”
“ Well, you shut up ! ”
There was a sudden shrieking and scuffling on
the stairs, and the partially-clothed landlady
burst in with her hair streaming and a comb
clutched in her right hand. She looked like
Ophelia before the battle of Waterloo.
“Oh !” she sobbed ; “oh ! oh-h! oh-h-h !”
Jorkins struggled ponderously, like a stage
prisoner in the hands of the torturers.
“ Madam !” he gasped, _“ I swear to you ”
The landlady shrieked like a cockatoo. “No,
no ! I can’t bear it.”
“ But, madam ”
The landlady collapsed on top of Jones, and,
clinging to his head, wept unreasonably down the
back of his neck.
Above the tumult and the shouting Jorkins
yelled: “He’’(gasp) “he called me’’(gasp) “a
fat actor J”
Johnson leaped on top of the sof<» nd aid,
“ So von are ! ”
“Let me—at him ! ” groaned Jorkins, in a fat,
terrible voice.
Then Nailer, the undertaker’s accountant, got
up on a chair and said solemnly, “For God’s sake
don’t let us have a scandal! ”
!” from the landlady.
“We,” continued Nailer, “must do the thing
decently!” (His easy professional manner sent
shudders down our spines. Johnson’s feverish
nose looked like a tombstone on a wet night.)
“Henry,” he resumed, “will bringdown the
gloves, and we will go out to the back and settle
this thing like gentlemen. We must do it
decently.”
Then the crowd of us surged out the back door
and deployed into the yard.
Henry fled upstairs and returned bearing a set
of Boz. boxing-gloves, which he threw dramati-
cally in the centre of the grass patch.
Nailer appointed himself referee, and detailed
the two tea-travellers to act as seconds to Jorkins.
Henry and myself acted in a like capacity for
Johnson. Milton, the bad-debt collector, was
time-keeper, and Hobson, the remittance man,
ten-seconds check.
At the direction of Nailer —Nailer had a genius
for organisation—the two combatants took off
their coats and vests, and Hobson brought out the
dinner-gong.
Then the seconds put the gloves on their
respective men, and Nailer explained that the
grass plot was to be regarded as the ring, and that
hitting “ in holts ” would not Vie allowed.
At the conclusion of his speech, he turned to
Milton and said ‘ ‘ Right! ” Milton, with a solemn
expression, hit the gong a resounding bang, and
the two principals rushed and fell on each other’s
necks. Then they hammered each other’s back-
bones until they were out of breath.
“ Break ! ” yelled the referee, pushing them off.
They came apart, panting and staring.
Nailer stepped back and bawled “Box on !”
Johnson lurched forward, joggled his gloves in
front of him, and suddenly hit. By the grace of
Heaven, the blow caught Jorkins fairly on the
nose, and sent him staggering back into his cor-
ner, where he collapsed, with a snort, on his chair.
There was a round of applause, and Johnson,
following up, sparred at the empty air in front of
the gasping Jorkins.
“ One,” shouted Hobson “ two three —
four”
Jorkins’ seconds —the tea-travellers—tried to
rouse him to a sense of his duty by shouting “ Get
up ! ” and pulled the chair from underneath him.
Jorkins fell on the grass, scrambled to his feet,
and punched one of the tea-travellers in the
stomach.
As he turned, Johnson again seized the oppor-
tunity to hit him on the nose.
Jorkins fell to the ground with blood stream-
ing down his countenance, and said angrily,
“ Coward ! ”
“No foul !” yelled the energetic Nailer, keep-
ing Johnson off with one hand.
The tea-travellers, who were not well versed in
the etiquette of the ring, rushed at Jorkins,
dragged him, protesting, to his feet, and hurled
him in the direction of Johnson.
Jorkins swung his right as he stumbled forward,
and clouted Nailer heavily on the ear.
Nailer kicked him into the arms of the exhausted
Johnson, who punched him feebly on the nose and
fell on him.
Jorkins said, in a muffled voice, “ It’s a damned
conspiracy ! ”
Then the gong went, and the seconds disen-
tangled their men and led them to their corners.
The referee lectured Jorkins while the tea-
travellers were mopping the gore off, and warned
him to be more careful.
Jorkins sat with his mouth hanging open, and
glared wildly at the triumphant Johnson, who
was being fanned by Henry and myself.
Bang ! The gong went, and Jorkins rose with
fury in his eye and ran heavily at our man.
Johnson lashed out at Jorkins’ nose and
missed.
Jorkins snorted indignantly and punched
wildly at Johnson’s head. Both hits landed on
the face. Then he stopped to consider where
he’d hit Johnson next, and Johnson, with his
last atom of strength, drove in a faded left and
jobbed him on the nose.
Then they threw their arms round each other’s
neck and panted over each other’s shoulder.
“Break ! ” yelled Nailer.
Jorkins looked at him with a dull, fishy eye,
and hissed “ Go t’ hell ! ”
Nailer jumped in and wrenched them apart.
“Box on !” he shouted.
They circled round each other, making weird,
tired motions with their hands and keeping well
out of reach.
One of the tea-travellers said, “Don’t forget,
Jorkins, he called you a fat actor ! ”
Jorkins responded to the goad by swiping
wearily at Johnson’s head.
The blow caught Johnson on the ear and upset
him on the grass in a curious position. He looked
as if he had started to stand on his head, but had
stopped midway to recollect where he had left his
umbrella.
“ One—two—three four—five—six seven—
eight”
Johnson rose with a dreary expression, and
surged towards the vengeful Jorkins.
The tea-travellers yelled to Jorkins to finish.
Jorkins edged in, sparring nervously, and met
a miracle. What malignant fate guided John-
son’s blind hit will never be known, but the fact
remains that his glove again landed with a dull
sound on Jorkins’ nose.
Jorkins, half-dazed, dropped his hands and
snorted like an elephant.
Johnson took careful aim, and clouted him
methodically on the same old spot.
It was too much for Jorkins. With a frenzied
howl, he. lifted his foot and basely kicked John-
son. Then he leaped into the air and fell on him.
“I’ve won !” he muttered, as we dragged him
off the prostrate form. “I’ve killed him ! ”
Johnson opened one eye, and whispered “Fat
actor ! ”
Nailer pushed Jorkins off, and tried to explain
that the fight was over, and that it wasn’t the
right thing to kick a man when he was down.
“But,” protested Jorkins, “he called me a
fat actor ! ”
Nailer took him by the arm and said, very
earnestly, “ Well, aren't you a fat actor ? ”
Everybody shouted, “Yes, of course he’s a fat
actor ! ”
There was a pause, during which Jorkins ground
his teeth. Then someone said in a severe voice,
“ And serve him darned well right—that’s all he
deserves to be ! ”
I never saw a man so enraged by a chance
remark as Jorkins was by that observation. For
five minutes he waved his hands, and heaved
remarks at us that would have been worth six
months in any court. Then he tramped inside to
the weeping landlady, and got her to wash the
blood off him. Three weeks after that he married
her, and we all left and continued on our way to
the dogs in various other hash mansions.
